모모1
모모
모모IT WAS AUGUST, and it shouldn't have been raining. Perhaps rain was too strong a word for the drizzle that blurred the landscape and kept my windshield wipers going. I was driving south, about halfway between Los Angeles and San Diego.
모모
모모The school lay off the highway to my right, in large grounds of its own which stretched along the seashore. Toward the sea I caught the dull sheen of the slough that gave the place its name, Laguna Perdida. A blue heron, tiny in the distance, stood like a figurine at the edge of the ruffled water.
모모
모모I entered the grounds through automatic gates, which lifted when my car passed over a treadle. A gray-headed man in a blue serge uniform came out of a kiosk and limped in my direction.
모모
모모'You got a pass?'
모모
모모'Dr Sponti wants to see me. My name is Archer.'
모모
모모'That's right, I got your name here.'
모모
모모He took a typewritten list out of the inside breast pocket of his jacket and brandished it as if he was proud of his literacy. 'You can park in the lot in front of the administration building. Sponti's office is right inside.'
모모
모모He gestured toward a stucco building a hundred yards down the road.
모모
모모I thanked him. He started to limp back to his kiosk, then paused and turned and struck himself on the leg. 'Bad knee. World War 1' 
모모
모모'You don't look that old.'
모모
모모'I'm not. I was fifteen when I enlisted; told them I was eighteen. Some of the boys in here,' he said with a sudden flashing look around him, 'could do with a taste of fire.'
모모
모모There were no boys anywhere in sight. The buildings of the school, widely distributed among bare fields and dripping eucalyptus groves, lay under the gray sky like scattered components of an unbuilt city.
모모
모모'Do you know the Hillman boy?'
모모
모모I said to the guard.
모모
모모'I heard about him. He's a troublemaker. He had East Hall all stirred up before he took off: Patch was fit to be tied.'
모모
모모'Who's Patch?'
모모
모모'Mr. Patch,' he said without affection, 'is the supervisor for East Hall. He lives in with the boys, and it plays hell with his nerves.'
모모
모모'What did the Hillman boy do?'
모모
모모'Tried to start a rebellion, according to Patch. Said the boys in the school had civil rights like anybody else. Which ain't so. They're all minors, and most of them are crazy in the head, besides. You wouldn't believe some of the things I've seen in my fourteen years on this gate.'
모모
모모'Did Tommy Hillman go out through the gate?'
모모
모모'Naw. He went over the fence. Cut a screen in the boys' dorm and sneaked out in the middle of the night.'
모모
모모'Night before last?'
모모
모모'That's right. He's probably home by now.'
모모
모모He wasn't or I wouldn't have been there.
모모
모모Dr Sponti must have seen me parking my car. He was waiting for me in the secretary's enclosure outside the door of his office. He had a glass of buttermilk in his left hand and a dietetic wafer in his right. He popped the wafer into his mouth and shook my hand, munching, 'I'm glad to see you.'
모모
모모He was dark and florid and stout, with the slightly desperate look of a man who had to lose weight. I guessed that he was an emotional man-he had that liquid tremor of the eye-but one who had learned to keep his feelings under control. He was expensively and conservatively dressed in a dark-pinstripe suit which hung on him a little loosely. His hand was soft and chilly.
모모
모모Dr Sponti reminded me of undertakers I had known. Even his office, with its dark mahogany furniture and the gray light at the window, had a funereal look, as if the school and its director were in continuous mourning for its students.
모모
모모'Sit down,' he said with a melancholy flourish. 'We have a little problem, as I told you on the long-distance telephone. Ordinarily we don't employ private detectives to-ah-persuade our lost boys to come home. But this is a rather special case, I'm afraid.'
모모
모모'What makes it special?'
모모
모모Sponti sipped his buttermilk, and licked his upper lip with the tip of his tongue. 'Forgive me. Can I offer you some lunch?'
모모
모모'No thanks.'
모모
모모'I don't mean this.'
모모
모모Irritably, he jiggled the sluggish liquid in his glass. 'I can have something hot sent over from dining commons. Veal scallopini is on the menu today.'
모모
모모'No thanks. I'd rather you gave me the information I need and let me get to work. Why did you call me in to pick up a runaway? You must have a lot of runaways.'
모모
모모'Not as many as you might think. Most of our boys become quite school-centered in time. We have a rich and varied program for them. But Thomas Hillman had been here less than a week, and he showed very little promise of becoming group-oriented. He's quite a difficult young man.'
모모
모모'And that's what makes him special?'
모모
모모'I'll be, frank with you, Mr. Archer,' he said, and hesitated. 'This is rather a prickly situation for the school. I accepted Tom Hillman against my better judgment, actually without full knowledge of his history, simply because his father insisted upon it. And now Ralph Hillman blames us for his son's esca-that is, his surreptitious leave-taking. Hillman has threatened to sue if any harm comes to the boy. The suit wouldn't stand up in court-we've had such lawsuits before-but it could do us a great deal of public harm.'
모모
모모He added, almost to himself 'Patch really was at fault.'
모모
모모'What did Patch do?'
모모
모모'I'm afraid he was unnecessarily violent. Not that I blame him as man to man. But you'd better talk to Mr. Patch yourself. He can give you all the details of Tom's-ah-departure.'
모모
모모'Later, I'd like to talk to him. But you can tell me more about the boy's background.'
모모
모모'Not as much as I'd like. We ask the families, or their doctors, to give us a detailed history of our entering students. Mr. Hillman promised to write one, but he hasn't as yet. And I've had great difficulty in getting any facts out of him. He's a very proud and very angry man.'
모모
모모'And a wealthy one?'
모모
모모'I don't know his Dun and Bradstreet rating. Most of our parents are comfortably fixed,' he added with a quick little smug smile.
모모
모모'I'd like to see Hillman. Does he live in town?'
모모
모모'Yes, but please don't try to see him, at least not today. He's just been on the phone to me again, and it would only stir him up further.'
모모
모모Sponti rose from his desk and moved to the window that overlooked the parking lot. I followed him. The fine rain outside hung like a visible depression in the air.
모모
모모'I still need a detailed description of the boy, and everything I can find out about his habits.'
모모
모모'Patch can give you that, better than I. He's been in daily contact with him. And you can talk to his housemother, Mrs. Mallow. She's a trained observer.'
모모
모모'Let's hope somebody is.'
모모
모모I was getting impatient with Sponti. He seemed to feel that the less he told me about the missing boy, the less real his disappearance was. 'How old is he, or is that classified material?'
모모
모모Sponti's eyes crossed slightly, and his rather pendulous cheeks became faintly mottled. 'I object to your tone.'
모모
모모'That's your privilege. How old is Tom Hillman?'
모모
모모'Seventeen.'
모모
모모'Do you have a picture of him?'
모모
모모'None was provided by the family, though we ask for one as a matter of routine. I can tell you briefly what he looks like. He's quite a decent-looking young chap, if you overlook the sullen expression he wears habitually. He's quite big, around six feet, he looks older than his age.'
모모
모모'Eyes?'
모모
모모'Dark blue, I think. His hair is dark blond. He has what might be called aquiline features, like his father.'
모모
모모'Identifying marks?'
모모
모모He shrugged his shoulders. 'I know of none.'
모모
모모'Why was he brought here?'
모모
모모'For treatment, of course. But he didn't stay long enough to benefit.'
모모
모모'Exactly what's the matter with him? You said he was difficult, but that's a pretty general description.'
모모
모모'It was meant to be. It's hard to tell what ails these boys in adolescent storm. Often we help them without knowing how or why. I'm not a medical doctor, in any case.'
모모
모모'I thought you were.'
모모
모모'No. We have medical doctors associated with our staff, of course, both physicians and psychiatrists. There wouldn't be much point in talking to them. I doubt if Tom was here long enough even to meet his therapist. But there's no doubt he was high.'
모모
모모'High?'
모모
모모'Emotionally high, running out of control. He was in a bad way when his father brought him here. We gave him tranquilizers, but they don't always work in the same way on different subjects.'
모모
모모'Did he cause you a lot of trouble?'
모모
모모'He did indeed. Frankly, I doubt if we'll readmit him even if he does come back.'
모모
모모'But you're hiring me to find him.'
모모
모모'I have no choice.'
모모
모모We discussed money matters, and he gave me a check. Then I walked down the road to East Hall. Before I went in to see Mr. Patch, I turned and looked at the mountains on the far side of the valley. They loomed like half-forgotten faces through the overcast. The lonely blue heron rose from the edge of the slough and sailed toward them.
모모
모모2
모모
모모EAST HALL WAS a sprawling one-story stucco building, which somehow didn't belong on that expansive landscape. Its mean and unprepossessing air had something to do with the high little windows, all of them heavily screened. Or with the related fact that it was a kind of prison which pretended not to be. The spiky pyracantha shrubs bordering the lawn in front of the building were more like barriers than ornaments. The grass looked dispirited even in the rain.
모모
모모So did the line of boys who were marching in the front door as I came up. Boys of all ages from twelve to twenty, boys of all shapes and sizes, with only one thing in common: they marched like members of a defeated army. They reminded me of the very young soldiers we captured on the Rhine in the last stages of the last war.
모모
모모Two students' leaders kept them in some sort of line. I followed them, into a big lounge furnished with rather dilapidated furniture. The two leaders went straight to a Ping-Pong table that stood in one corner, picked up paddles, and began to play a rapid intense game with a ball that one of them produced from his windbreaker pocket. Six or seven boys began to watch them. Four or five settled down with comic books. Most of the rest of them stood around and watched me.
모모
모모A hairy-faced young fellow who ought to have started to shave came up to me smiling. His smile was brilliant, but it faded like an optical illusion. He came so close that his shoulder nudged my arm. Some dogs will nudge you like that, to test your friendliness.
모모
모모'Are you the new supervisor?'
모모
모모'No. I thought Mr. Patch was the supervisor.'
모모
모모'He won't last.'
모모
모모A few of the younger boys giggled. The hairy one responded like a successful comedian. 'This is the violent ward. They never last.'
모모
모모'It doesn't look so violent to me. Where is Mr. Patch?'
모모
모모'Over at dining commons. He'll be here in a minute. Then we have organized fun.'
모모
모모'You sound pretty cynical for your age. How old are you?'
모모
모모'Ninety-nine.'
모모
모모His audience murmured encouragingly. 'Mr. Patch is only forty-nine. It makes it hard for him to be my father-image.'
모모
모모'Maybe I could talk to Mrs. Mallow.'
모모
모모'She's in her room drinking her lunch. Mrs. Mallow always drinks her lunch.'
모모
모모The bright malice in his eyes alternated with a darker feeling. 'Are you a father?'
모모
모모'No.'
모모
모모In the background the Ping-Pong ball was clicking back and forth like mindless conversation.
모모
모모A member of the audience spoke up. 'He's not a father.'
모모
모모'Maybe he's a mother,' said the hairy boy. 'Are you a mother?'
모모
모모'He doesn't look like a mother. He has no bosoms.'
모모
모모'My mother has no bosoms,' said a third one. 'That's why I feel rejected.'
모모
모모'Come off it, boys.'
모모
모모The hell of it was, they wished I was a father, or even a mother, one of theirs, and the wish stood in their eyes. 'You don't want me to feel rejected, do you?'
모모
모모Nobody answered. The hairy boy smiled up at me. It lasted a little longer than his first smile. 'What's your name? I'm Frederick Tyndal the Third.'
모모
모모'I'm Lew Archer the First.'
모모
모모I drew the boy away from his audience. He pulled back from my touch, but he came along and sat down with me on a cracked leather couch. Some of the younger boys had put an overplayed record on a player. Two of them began to dance together to the raucous self-parodying song. 'Surfin' ain't no sin,' was the refrain.
모모
모모'Did you know Tom Hillman, Fred?'
모모
모모'A little. Are you his father?'
모모
모모'No. I said I wasn't a father.'
모모
모모'Adults don't necessarily tell you the truth.'
모모
모모He plucked at the hairs on his chin as if he hated the signs of growing up. 'My father said he was sending me away to military school. He's a big shot in the government,' he added flatly, without pride, and then, in a different tone: 'Tom Hillman didn't get along with his father, either. So he got railroaded here. The Monorail to the Magic Kingdom.'
모모
모모He produced a fierce ecstatic hopeless grin.
모모
모모'Did Tom talk to you about it?'
모모
모모'A little. He wasn't here long. Five days. Six. He came in Sunday night and took off Saturday night.'
모모
모모He squirmed uneasily on the creaking leather. 'Are you a cop?'
모모
모모'No.'
모모
모모'I just wondered. You ask questions like a cop.'
모모
모모'Did Tom do something that would interest the cops?'
모모
모모'We all do, don't we?'
모모
모모His hot and cold running glance went around the room, pausing on the forlorn antics of the dancing boys. 'You don't qualify for East Hall unless you're a juvie. I was a criminal mastermind myself. I forged the big shot's name on a fifty-dollar check and went to San Francisco for the weekend.'
모모
모모'What did Tom do?'
모모
모모'Stole a car, I guess. It was a first offense, he said, and he would of got probation easy. But his father didn't want the publicity, so he put him in here. Also, I guess Tom had a fight with his father.'
모모
모모'I see.'
모모
모모'Why are you so fascinated in Tom?'
모모
모모'I'm supposed to find him, Fred.'
모모
모모'And bring him back here?'
모모
모모'I doubt that they'll readmit him.'
모모
모모'He's lucky.'
모모
모모More or less unconsciously, he moved against me again. I could smell the untended odor of his hair and body, and sense his desolation. 'I'd break out of here myself if I had a place to go. But the big shot would turn me over to the Youth Authority. It would save him money, besides.'
모모
모모'Did Tom have a place to go?'
모모
모모He jerked upright and looked at my eyes from the corners of his. 'I didn't say that.'
모모
모모'I'm asking you.'
모모
모모'He wouldn't tell me if he had.'
모모
모모'Who was closest to him in the school?'
모모
모모'He wasn't close to anybody. He was so upset when he came in, they put him in a room by himself. I went in and talked to him one night, but he didn't say much to me.'
모모
모모'Nothing about where he planned to go?'
모모
모모'He didn't plan anything. He tried to start a riot Saturday night but the rest of us were chicken. So he took off. He seemed to be very excited.'
모모
모모'Was he emotionally disturbed?'
모모
모모'Aren't we all?'
모모
모모He tapped his own temple and made an insane face. 'You ought to see my Rorschach.'
모모
모모'Some other time.'
모모
모모'Be my guest.'
모모
모모'This is important, Fred. Tom is very young, and excited, as you said. He's been missing for two nights now, and he could get into very serious trouble.'
모모
모모'Worse than this?'
모모
모모'You know it, or you'd be over the fence yourself. Did Tom say anything about where he was going?'
모모
모모The boy didn't answer.
모모
모모'Then I assume he did tell you something?'
모모
모모'No.'
모모
모모But he wouldn't meet my eyes.
모모
모모Mr. Patch came into the room and changed its carefree atmosphere. The dancing boys pretended to be wrestling. Comic books disappeared like bundles of hot money. The Ping-Pong players put away their ball.
모모
모모Patch was a middle-aged man with thinning hair and thickening jowls. His double-breasted tan gabardine suit was creased across his rather corpulent front. His face was creased, too, into a sneer of power, which didn't go with his sensitive small mouth. As he looked around the room, I could see that the whites of his eyes were tinted with red.
모모
모모He strode to the record player and turned it off, insinuating his voice into the silence: 'Lunchtime isn't music time, boys. Music time is after dinner, from seven to seven-thirty.'
모모
모모He addressed one of the Ping-Pong players: 'Bear that in mind, Deering. No music in the daytime. I'll hold you responsible.'
모모
모모'Yessir.'
모모
모모'And weren't you playing ping-pone' 
모모
모모'We were just rallying, sir.'
모모
모모'Where did you get the ball? I understood the balls were locked up in my desk.'
모모
모모'They are, sir.'
모모
모모'Where did you get the one you were playing with?'
모모
모모'I don't know, sir.'
모모
모모Deering fumbled at his windbreaker. He was a gawky youth with an Adam's apple that looked like a hidden Ping-Pong ball. 'I think I must of found it.'
모모
모모'Where did you find it? In my desk?'
모모
모모'No sir. On the grounds, I think it was.'
모모
모모Mr. Patch walked toward him with a kind of melodramatic stealth. As he moved across the room, the boys behind him made faces, waved their arms, did bumps and grinds. One boy, one of the dancers, fell silently to the floor with a throat-slitting gesture, held the pose of a dying gladiator for a single frozen second, then got back onto his feet.
모모
모모Patch was saying in a long-suffering tone: 'You bought it, didn't you, Deering? You know that regulations forbid you fellows to bring in private Ping-Pong balls of your own. You know that, don't you? You're president of the East Hall Legislative Assembly, you helped to frame those regulations yourself. Didn't you?'
모모
모모'Yessir.'
모모
모모'Then give it to me, Deering.'
모모
모모The boy handed Patch the ball. Patch stooped to place it on the floor-while a boy behind him pretended to kick him-and squashed it under his heel. He gave Deering the misshapen ball.
모모
모모'I'm sorry, Deering. I have to obey the regulations just as you do.'
모모
모모He turned to the roomful of boys, who snapped into conformity under his eyes, and said mildly: 'Well, fellows, what's on the agenda-?'
모모
모모'I think I am,' I said, getting up from the couch. I gave him my name and asked if I could talk to him in private.
모모
모모'I suppose so,' he said with a worried smile, as if I might in fact be his successor. 'Come into my office, such as it is. Deering and Bronson, I'm leaving you in charge.'
모모
모모His office was a windowless cubicle containing a cluttered desk and two straight chairs. He closed the door on the noise that drifted down the corridor from the lounge, turned on a desk lamp, and sat down sighing.
모모
모모'You've got to stay on top of them.'
모모
모모He sounded like a man saying his prayers. 'You wanted to discuss one of my boys?'
모모
모모'Tom Hillman.'
모모
모모The name depressed him. 'You represent his father?'
모모
모모'No. Dr Sponti sent me to talk to you. I'm a private detective.'
모모
모모'I see.'
모모
모모He pushed out his lips in a kind of pout. 'I suppose Sponti's been blaming me, as usual.'
모모
모모'He did say something about unnecessary violence.'
모모
모모'That's nonsense!'
모모
모모He pounded the desk between us with his clenched fist. His face became congested with blood. Then it went starkly pale, like a raw photograph. Only the reddish whites of his eyes held their color. 'Sponti doesn't work down here with the animals. I ought to know when physical discipline is necessary. I've been in juvenile work for twenty-five years.'
모모
모모'It seems to be getting you down.'
모모
모모With an effort that crumpled up his face, he brought himself under control. 'Oh no, I love the work, I really do. Anyway, it's the only thing I'm trained for. I love the boys. And they love me.'
모모
모모'I could see that.'
모모
모모He wasn't listening for my irony. 'I'd have been pals with Tom Hillman if he'd lasted.'
모모
모모'Why didn't he?'
모모
모모'He ran away. You know that. He stole a pair of shears from the gardener and used it to cut the screen on his bedroom window.'
모모
모모'Exactly when was this?'
모모
모모'Sometime Saturday night, between my eleven-o'clock bedcheck and my early-morning one.'
모모
모모'And what happened before that?'
모모
모모'Saturday night, you mean? He was stirring up the other boys, inciting them to attack the resident staff: I'd left the common room after dinner, and I heard him from in here, making a speech. He was trying to convince the boys that they had been deprived of their rights and should fight for them. Some of the more excitable ones were affected. But when I ordered Hillman to shut up, he was the only one who rushed me.'
모모
모모'Did he hit you?'
모모
모모'I hit him first,' Patch said. 'I'm not ashamed of it. I had to preserve my authority with the others.'
모모
모모He rubbed his fist. 'I knocked him cold. You have to make a show of manliness. When I hit them, they go down for the count. You have to give them an image to respect.'
모모
모모I said to stop him: 'What happened after that?'
모모
모모'I helped him to his room, and then I reported the incident to Sponti. I thought the boy should be put in the padded room. But Sponti countermanded my advice. Hillman would never have broken out if Sponti had let me put him in the padded room. Just between you and me, it's Sponti's fault.'
모모
모모He brought himself up short and said in a smaller voice: 'Don't quote me to him.'
모모
모모'All right.'
모모
모모I was beginning to despair of getting anything useful out of Patch. He was a little dilapidated, like the furniture in the common room. The noise coming from that direction was becoming louder. Patch rose wearily to his feet.
모모
모모'I'd better get back there before they tear the place down.'
모모
모모'I just wanted to ask you, do you have any thoughts on where Tom Hillman went after he left here?'
모모
모모Patch considered my question. He seemed to be having difficulty in imagining the outside world into which the boy had vanished. 'LA,' he said finally. 'They usually head for LA or else they head south for San Diego and the border.'
모모
모모'Or east?'
모모
모모'If their parents live east, they sometimes go that way.'
모모
모모'Or west across the ocean?'
모모
모모I was baiting him.
모모
모모'That's true. One boy stole a thirty-foot launch and headed for the islands.'
모모
모모'You seem to have a lot of runaways.'
모모
모모'Over the years, we have quite a turnover. Sponti's opposed to strict security measures, like we used to have at juvenile Hall. With all the breakouts we've had, I'm surprised he wants to make such a production out of this one. The boy'll turn up, they nearly always do.'
모모
모모Patch sounded as if he wasn't looking forward to the prospect.
모모
모모Somebody tapped at the door behind me. 'Mr. Patch?' a woman said through the panels.
모모
모모'Yes, Mrs. Mallow.'
모모

모모'The boys are getting out of hand. They won't listen to me. What are you doing in there?'
모모
모모'Conferring. Dr Sponti sent a man.'
모모
모모'Good. We need a man.'
모모
모모'Is that so?'
모모
모모He brushed past me and opened the door. 'Keep your cracks to yourself, please, Mrs. Mallow. I know one or two things that Dr Sponti would dearly love to know.'
모모
모모'So do I,' the woman said.
모모
모모She was heavily rouged, with dyed red hair arranged in bangs on her forehead. She had on a dark formal dress, about ten years out of fashion, and several loops of imitation pearls. Her face was pleasant enough, in spite of eyes that had been bleared by horrors inner and outer.
모모
모모She brightened up when she saw me. 'Hello.'
모모
모모'My name is Archer,' I said. 'Dr Sponti brought me in to look into Tom Hillman's disappearance.'
모모
모모'He's a nice-looking boy,' she said. 'At least he was until our local Marquis de Sade gave him a working-over.'
모모
모모'I acted in self-defense,' Patch cried. 'I don't enjoy hurting people. I'm the authority figure in East Hall, and when I'm attacked it's just like killing their father.'
모모
모모'You better go and make with the authority, Father. But if you hurt anybody this week I'll carve the living heart out of your body.'
모모
모모Patch looked at her as if he believed she might do it. Then he turned on his heel and strode away toward the roaring room. The roaring subsided abruptly, as if he had closed a soundproof door behind him.
모모
모모'Poor old Patch,' said Mrs. Mallow. 'He's been around too long. Poor old all of us. Too many years of contact with the adolescent mind, if mind is the word, and eventually we all go blah.'
모모
모모'Why stay?'
모모
모모'We get so we can't live in the outside world. Like old convicts. That's the real hell of it.'
모모
모모'People around here are extraordinarily ready to spill their problems-' 
모모
모모'It's the psychiatric atmosphere.'
모모
모모'But,' I went on, 'they don't tell me much I want to know. Can you give me a clear impression of Tom Hillman?'
모모
모모'I can give you my own impression.'
모모
모모She had a little difficulty with the word, and it seemed to affect her balance. She walked into Patch's office and leaned on his desk facing me. Her face, half-shadowed in the upward light from the lamp, reminded me of a sibyl's.
모모
모모'Tom Hillman is a pretty nice boy. He didn't belong here. He found that out in a hurry. And so he left.'
모모
모모'Why didn't he belong here?'
모모
모모'You want me to go into detail? East Hall is essentially a place for boys with personality and character problems, or with a sociopathic tendency. We keep the more disturbed youngsters, boys and girls, in West Hall.'
모모
모모'And Tom belonged there?'
모모
모모'Hardly. He shouldn't have been sent to Laguna Perdida at all. This is just my opinion, but it ought to be worth something. I used to be a pretty good clinical psychologist.'
모모
모모She looked down into the light.
모모
모모'Dr Sponti seems to think Tom was disturbed.'
모모
모모'Dr Sponti never thinks otherwise, about any prospect. Do you know what these kids' parents pay? A thousand dollars a month, plus extras. Music lessons. Group therapy.'
모모
모모She laughed harshly. 'When half the time it's the parents who should be here. Or in some worse place.
모모
모모'A thousand dollars a month,' she repeated. 'So Dr Sponti so-called can draw his twenty-five thousand a year. Which is more than six times what he pays me for holding the kids' hands.'
모모
모모She was a woman with a grievance. Sometimes grievances made for truth-telling, but not always. 'What do you mean, Dr Sponti so called?'
모모
모모'He's not a medical doctor, or any other kind of real doctor. He took his degree in educational administration, at one of the diploma mills down south. Do you know what he wrote his dissertation on? The kitchen logistics of the medium-sized boarding school.'
모모
모모'Getting back to Tom,' I said, 'why would his father bring him here if he didn't need psychiatric treatment?'
모모
모모'I don't know. I don't know his father. Probably because he wanted him out of his sight.'
모모
모모'Why?' I insisted.
모모
모모'The boy was in some kind of trouble.'
모모
모모'Did Tom tell you that?'
모모
모모'He wouldn't talk about it. But I can read the signs.'
모모
모모'Have you heard the story that he stole a car?'
모모
모모'No, but it would help to explain him. He's a very unhappy young man, and a guilty one. He isn't one of your hardened J.D.' s. Not that any of them really are.'
모모
모모'You seem to have liked Tom Hillman.'
모모
모모'What little I saw of him. He didn't want to talk last week, and I try never to force myself on the boys. Except for class hours, he spent most of the time in his room. I think he was trying to work something out.'
모모
모모'Like a plan for revolution?'
모모
모모Her eyes glinted with amusement. 'You heard about that, did you? The boy had more gumption than I gave him credit for. Don't look so surprised. I'm on the boys' side. Why else would I be here?'
모모
모모I was beginning to like Mrs. Mallow. Sensing this, she moved toward me and touched my arm. 'I hope that you are, too. On Tom's side, I mean.'
모모
모모'I'll wait until I know him. It isn't important, anyway.'
모모
모모'Yes it is. It's always important.'
모모
모모Just what happened between Tom and Mr. Patch Saturday night?'
모모
모모'I wouldn't know, really. Saturday night is my night off. You can make a note of that if you like, Mr. Archer.'
모모
모모She smiled, and I caught a glimpse of her life's meaning. She cared for other people. Nobody cared for her.
모모
모모3
모모
모모SHE LET ME out through a side door which had to be unlocked. The rain was just heavy enough to wet my face. Dense-looking clouds were gathering over the mountains, which probably meant that the rain was going to persist.
모모
모모I started back toward the administration building. Sponti was going to have to be told that I must see Tom Hillman's parents, whether he approved or not. The varying accounts of Tom I'd had, from people who liked or disliked him, gave me no distinct impression of his habits or personality. He could be a persecuted teen-ager, or a psychopath who knew how to appeal to older women, or something in between, like Fred the Third.
모모
모모I wasn't looking where I was going, and a yellow cab almost ran me down in the parking lot. A man in tweeds got out of the back seat. I thought he was going to apologize to me, but he didn't appear to see me.
모모
모모He was a tall, silver-haired man, well-fed, well-cared for, probably good-looking under normal conditions. At the moment he looked haggard. He ran into the administration building. I walked in after him, and found him arguing with Sponti's secretary.
모모
모모'I'm very sorry, Mr. Hillman,' she intoned. 'Dr Sponti is in conference. I can't possibly interrupt him.'
모모
모모'I think you'd better,' Hillman said in a rough voice.
모모
모모'I'm sorry. You'll have to wait.'
모모
모모'But I can't wait. My son is in the hands of criminals. They're trying to extort money from me.'
모모
모모'Is that true?'
모모
모모Her voice was unprofessional and sharp.
모모
모모'I'm not in the habit of lying.'
모모
모모The girl excused herself and went into Sponti's office, closing the door carefully behind her. I spoke to Hillman, telling him my name and occupation: 'Dr Sponti called me in to look for your son. I've been wanting to talk to you. It seems to be time I did.'
모모
모모'Yes. By all means.'
모모
모모He took my hand. He was a large, impressive-looking man. His face had the kind of patrician bony structure that doesn't necessarily imply brains or ability, or even decency, but that generally goes with money. He was deep in the chest and heavy in the shoulders. But there was no force in his grasp. He was trembling all over, like a frightened dog.
모모
모모'You said something about criminals and extortion.'
모모
모모'Yes.' But his steel-gray eyes kept shifting away to the door of Sponti's office. He wanted to talk to somebody he could blame. 'What are they doing in there?' he said a little wildly.
모모
모모'It hardly matters. If your son's been kidnapped, Sponti can't help you much. It's a matter for the police.'
모모
모모'No! The police stay out. I've been instructed to keep them out.'
모모
모모His eyes focused on me for the first time, hard with suspicion. 'You're not a policeman, are you?'
모모
모모'I told you I was a private detective. I just came down from Los Angeles an hour ago. How did you find out about Tom, and who gave you your instructions?'
모모
모모'One of the gang. He telephoned my house when we were just sitting down to lunch. He warned me to keep the matter quiet. Otherwise Tom will never come back.'
모모
모모'Did he say that?'
모모
모모'Yes.'
모모
모모'What else did he say?'
모모
모모'They want to sell me information about Tom's whereabouts. It was just a euphemism for ransom money.'
모모
모모'How much?'
모모
모모'Twenty-five thousand dollars.'
모모
모모'Do you have it?'
모모
모모'I'll have it by the middle of the afternoon. I'm selling some stock. I went into town to my broker's before I came here.'
모모
모모'You move fast, Mr. Hillman.'
모모
모모He needed some mark of respect. 'But I don't quite understand why you came out here.'
모모
모모'I don't trust these people,' he said in a lowered voice. Apparently he had forgotten, or hadn't heard, that I was working for Sponti. 'I believe that Tom was lured away from here, perhaps with inside help, and they're covering up.'
모모
모모'I doubt that very much. I've talked to the staff member involved. He and Tom had a fight Saturday night, and later Tom cut a screen and went over the fence. One of the students confirmed this, more or less.'
모모
모모'A student would be afraid to deny the official story.'
모모
모모'Not this student, Mr. Hillman. If your son's been kidnapped, it happened after he left here. Tell me this, did he have any criminal connections?'
모모
모모'Tom? You must be out of your mind.'
모모
모모'I heard he stole a car.'
모모
모모'Did Sponti tell you that? He had no right to.- ' 
모모
모모'I got it from other sources. Boys don't usually steal cars unless they've had some experience outside the law, perhaps with a juvenile gang-' 
모모
모모'He didn't steal it.'
모모
모모Hillman's eyes were evasive. 'He borrowed it from a neighbor. The fact that he wrecked it was pure accident. He was emotionally upset-' Hillman was, too. He ran out of breath and words. He opened and closed his mouth like a big handsome fish hooked by circumstance and yanked into alien air. 
모모
모모I said: 'What are you supposed to do with the twenty-five thousand? Hold it for further instructions?'
모모
모모Hillman nodded, and sat down despondently in a chair. Dr Sponti's door had opened, and he had been listening, I didn't know for how long. He came out into the anteroom now, flanked by his secretary and followed by a man with a long cadaverous face.
모모
모모'What's this about kidnapping?'
모모
모모Sponti said in a high voice. He forced his voice down into a more soothing register: 'I'm sorry, Mr. Hillman.'
모모
모모Hillman's sitting position changed to a kind of crouch. 'You're going to be sorrier. I want to know who took my son out of here, and under what circumstances, and with whose connivance.'
모모
모모'Your son left here of his own free will, Mr. Hillman.'
모모
모모'And you wash your hands of him, do you?'
모모
모모'We never do that with any of our charges, however short their stay. I've hired Mr. Archer here to help you out. And I've just been talking to Mr. Squerry here, our comptroller.'
모모
모모The cadaverous man bowed solemnly. Black stripes of hair were pasted flat across the crown of his almost naked head. He said in a precise voice: 'Dr Sponti and I have decided to refund in full the money you paid us last week. We've just written out a check, and here it is.'
모모
모모He handed over a slip of yellow paper. Hillman crumpled it into a ball and threw it back at Mr. Squerry. It bounced off his thin chest and fell to the floor. I picked it up. It was for two thousand dollars.
모모
모모Hillman ran out of the room. I walked out after him, before Sponti could terminate my services, and caught Hillman as he was getting into the cab.
모모
모모'Where are you going?'
모모
모모'Home. My wife's in poor shape.'
모모
모모'Let me drive you.'
모모
모모'Not if you're Sponti's man.'
모모
모모'I'm nobody's man but my own. Sponti hired me to find your son. I'm going to do that if it's humanly possible. But I'll need some cooperation from you and Mrs. Hillman.'
모모
모모'What can we do?'
모모
모모He spread his large helpless hands.
모모
모모'Tell me what kind of a boy he is, who his friends are, where he hangs out-' 'What's the point of all that? He's in the hands of gangsters. They want money. I'm willing to pay them.'
모모
모모The cab driver, who had got out of his seat to open the door for Hillman, stood listening with widening mouth and eyes.
모모
모모'It may not be as simple as that,' I said. 'But we won't talk about it here.'
모모
모모'You can trust me,' the driver said huskily. 'I got a brother-in-law on the Highway Patrol. Besides, I never blab about my fares.'
모모
모모'You better not,' Hillman said.
모모
모모He paid the man, and came along with me to my car.
모모
모모'Speaking of money,' I said when we were together in the front seat, 'you didn't really want to throw away two thousand dollars, did you?'
모모
모모I smoothed out the yellow check and handed it to him.
모모
모모There's no way to tell what will make a man break down. A long silence, or a telephone ringing, or the wrong note in a woman's voice. In Hillman's case, it was a check for two thousand dollars. He put it away in his alligator wallet, and then he groaned loudly. He covered his eyes with his hands and leaned his forehead on the dash. Cawing sounds came out of his mouth as if an angry crow was tearing at his vitals.
모모
모모After a while he said: 'I should never have put him in this place.'
모모
모모His voice was more human than it had been, as if he had broken through into a deeper level of self-knowledge.
모모
모모'Don't cry over spilt milk.'
모모
모모He straightened up. 'I wasn't crying.'
모모
모모It was true his eyes were dry.
모모
모모'We won't argue, Mr. Hillman. Where do you live?'
모모
모모'In El Rancho. It's between here and the city. I'll tell you how to get there by the shortest route.'
모모
모모The guard limped out of his kiosk, and we exchanged half-salutes. He activated the gates. Following Hillman's instructions, I drove out along a road which passed through a reedy wasteland where blackbirds were chittering, then through a suburban wasteland jammed with new apartments, and around the perimeter of a college campus. We passed an airport, where a plane was taking off. Hillman looked as if he wished he were on it.
모모
모모'Why did you put your son in Laguna Perdida School?'
모모
모모His answer came slowly, in bits and snatches. 'I was afraid. He seemed to be headed for trouble. I felt I had to prevent it somehow. I was hoping they could straighten him out so that he could go back to regular school next month. He's supposed to be starting his senior year in high school.'
모모
모모'Would you mind being specific about the trouble he was in? Do you mean car theft?'
모모
모모'That was one of the things. But it wasn't a true case of theft, as I explained.'
모모
모모'You didn't explain, though.'
모모
모모'It was Rhea Carlson's automobile he took. Rhea and Jay Carlson are our next-door neighbors. When you leave a new Dart in an open carport all night with the key in the ignition, it's practically an invitation to a joyride. I told them that. Jay would've admitted it, too, if he hadn't had a bit of a down on Tom. Or if Tom hadn't wrecked the car. It was fully covered, by my insurance as well as theirs, but they had to take the emotional approach.'
모모
모모'The car was wrecked?'
모모
모모'It's a total loss. I don't know how he managed to turn it over, but he did. Fortunately he came out of it without a scratch.'
모모
모모'Where was he going?'
모모
모모'He was on his way home. The accident happened practically at our door. I'll show you the place.'
모모
모모'Then where had he been?'
모모
모모'He wouldn't say. He'd been gone all night, but he wouldn't tell me anything about it.'
모모
모모'What night was that?'
모모
모모'Saturday night. A week ago Saturday night. The police brought him home about six o'clock in the morning, and told me I better have our doctor go over him, which I did. He wasn't hurt physically, but his mind seemed to be affected. He went into a rage when I tried to ask him where he had spent the night. I'd never seen him like that before. He'd always been a quiet-spoken boy. He said I had no right to know about him, that I wasn't really his father, and so on and so forth. I'm afraid I lost my temper and slapped him when he said that. Then he turned his back on me and wouldn't talk at all, about anything.'
모모
모모'Had he been drinking?'
모모
모모'I don't think so. No. I would have smelled it on him.'
모모
모모'What about drugs?'
모모
모모I could see his face turn toward me, large and vague in my side vision. 'That's out of the question.'
모모
모모'I hope so. Dr Sponti told me your son had a peculiar reaction to tranquilizers. That sometimes happens with habitual users.'
모모
모모'My son was not a drug user.'
모모
모모'A lot of young people are, nowadays, and their parents are the last to know about it.'
모모
모모'No. It wasn't anything like that,' he said urgently. 'The shock of the accident affected his mind.'
모모
모모'Did the doctor think so?'
모모
모모'Dr Shanley is an orthopedic surgeon. He wouldn't know about psychiatric disturbance. Anyway, he didn't know what happened that morning, when I went to the judge's house to arrange for bail. I haven't told anyone about it.'
모모
모모I waited, and listened to the windshield wipers. A green and white sign on the shoulder of the road announced: 'El Rancho.'
모모
모모Hillman said, as if he was glad to have something neutral to say: 'You turn off in another quarter mile.'
모모
모모I slowed down. 'You were going to tell me what happened that Sunday morning.'
모모
모모'No. I don't believe I will. It has no bearing on the present situation.'
모모
모모'How do we know that?'
모모
모모He didn't answer me. Perhaps the thought of home and neighbors had silenced him.
모모
모모'Did you say the Carlsons had a down on Tom?'
모모
모모'I said that, and it's true.'
모모
모모'Do you know the reason for it?'
모모
모모'They have a daughter, Stella. Tom and Stella Carlson were very close. Jay and Rhea disapproved, at least Rhea did. So did Elaine, my wife, for that matter.'
모모
모모I turned off the main road. The access road passed between ... tall stone gateposts and became the palm-lined central road bisecting El Rancho. It was one of those rich developments whose inhabitants couldn't possibly have troubles. Their big houses sat far back behind enormous lawns. Their private golf course lay across the road we were travelling on. The diving tower of their beach club gleamed with fresh aluminum paint in the wet distance.
모모
모모But like the drizzle, troubles fall in or out of season on everybody.
모모
모모The road bent around one corner of the golf course. Hillman pointed ahead to a deep gouge in the bank, where the earth was still raw. Above it a pine tree with a damaged trunk was turning brown in places.
모모
모모'This is where he turned the car over.'
모모
모모I stopped the car. 'Did he explain how the accident happened?'
모모
모모Hillman pretended not to hear me. We got out of the car. There was no traffic in sight, except for a foursome of die-hard golfers approaching in two carts along the fairway.
모모
모모'I don't see any brake- or skid-marks,' I said. 'Was your son an experienced driver?'

모모
모모'Yes. I taught him to drive myself. I spent a great deal of time with him. In fact, I deliberately reduced my work load at the firm several years ago, partly so that I could enjoy Tom's growing up.'
모모
모모His phrasing was a little strange, as if growing up was something a boy did for his parents' entertainment. It made me wonder. If Hillman had been really close to Tom, why had he clapped him into Laguna Perdida School at the first sign of delinquency? Or had there been earlier signs which he was suppressing? One of the golfers waved from his cart as he went by. Hillman gave him a cold flick of the hand and got into my car. He seemed embarrassed to be found at the scene of the accident.
모모
모모'I'll be frank with you,' I said as we drove away. 'I wish you'd be frank with me. Laguna Perdida is a school for disturbed and delinquent minors. I can't get it clear why Tom deserved, or needed, to be put there.'
모모
모모'I did it for his own protection. Good-neighbor Carlson was threatening to prosecute him for car theft.'
모모

모모'That's nothing so terrible. He'd have rated probation, if this was a first offense. Was it?'
모모
모모'Of course it was.'
모모
모모'Then what were you afraid of?'
모모
모모'I wasn't-' he started to say. But he was too honest, or too completely conscious of his fear, to finish the sentence.
모모
모모'What did he do Sunday morning, when you went to see the judge?'
모모
모모'He didn't do anything, really. Nothing happened.'
모모
모모'But that nothing hit you so hard you won't discuss it.'
모모
모모'That's correct. I won't discuss it, with you or anyone. Whatever happened last Sunday, or might have happened, has been completely outdated by recent events. My son has been kidnapped. He's a passive victim, don't you understand?'
모모
모모I wondered about that, too. Twenty-five thousand dollars was a lot of money in my book, but it didn't seem to be in Hillman's. If Tom was really in the hands of professional criminals, they would be asking for all that the traffic would bear.
모모
모모'How much money could you raise if you had to, Mr. Hillman?'
모모
모모He gave me a swift look. 'I don't see the point.'
모모
모모'Kidnappers usually go the limit in their demands. I'm trying to find out if they have in this case. I gather you could raise a good deal more than twenty-five thousand.'
모모
모모'I could, with my wife's help.'
모모
모모'Let's hope it won't be necessary.'
모모
모모4
모모
모모THE HILLMANS' PRIVATE drive meandered up an oak-covered rise and circled around in a lawn in front of their house. It was a big old Spanish mansion, with white stucco walls, wrought-iron ornamentation at the windows, red tile roof gleaming dully in the wet. A bright black Cadillac was parked in the circle ahead of us.
모모
모모'I meant to drive myself this morning,' Hillman said. 'But then I didn't trust myself to drive. Thanks for the lift.'
모모
모모It sounded like a dismissal. He started up the front steps, and I felt a keen disappointment. I swallowed it and went after him, slipping inside the front door before he closed it.
모모
모모It was his wife he was preoccupied with. She was waiting for him in the reception hall, bowed forward in a high-backed Spanish chair which made her look tinier than she was. Her snakeskin shoes hung clear of the polished tile floor. She was a beautifully made thin blonde woman in her forties. An aura of desolation hung about her, a sense of uselessness, as if she was in fact the faded doll she resembled. Her green dress went poorly with her almost greenish pallor.
모모
모모'Elaine?'
모모
모모She had been sitting perfectly still, with her knees and fists together. She looked up at her husband, and then over his head at the huge Spanish chandelier suspended on a chain from the beamed ceiling two stories up. Its bulbs protruded like dubious fruit from clusters of wrought-iron leaves.
모모
모모'Don't stand under it,' she said. 'I'm always afraid it's going to fall. I wish you'd have it taken down, Ralph.'
모모
모모'It was your idea to bring it back and put it there.'
모모
모모'That was a long time ago,' she said. 'I thought the space needed filling.'
모모
모모'It still does, and it's still perfectly safe.'
모모
모모He moved toward her and touched her head. 'You're wet. You shouldn't have gone out in your condition.'
모모
모모'I just walked down the drive to see if you were coming. You were gone a long time.'
모모
모모'I couldn't help it.'
모모
모모She took his hand as it slid away from her head, and held it against her breast. 'Did you hear anything?'
모모
모모'We can't expect to hear anything yet for a while. I made arrangements for the money. Dick Leandro will bring it out later this afternoon. In the meantime we wait for a phone call.'
모모
모모'It's hideous, waiting.'
모모
모모'I know. You should try to think about something else.'
모모
모모'What else is there?'
모모
모모'Lots of things.'
모모
모모I think he tried to name one, and gave up. 'Anyway, it isn't good for you to be sitting out here in the cold hall. You'll give yourself pneumonia again.'
모모
모모'People don't give it to themselves, Ralph.'
모모
모모'We won't argue. Come into the sitting room and I'll make you a drink.'
모모
모모He remembered me and included me in the invitation, but he didn't introduce me to his wife. Perhaps he considered me unworthy, or perhaps he wanted to discourage communication between us. Feeling rather left out, I followed them up three tile steps into a smaller room where a fire was burning. Elaine Hillman stood with her back to it. Her husband went to the bar, which was in an alcove decorated with Spanish bullfight posters.
모모
모모She held out her hand to me. It was ice cold. 'I don't mean to monopolize the heat. Are you a policeman? I thought we weren't to use them.'
모모
모모'I'm a private detective. Lew Archer is my name.'
모모
모모Her husband called from the alcove: 'What will you drink, darling?'
모모
모모'Absinthe.'
모모
모모'Is that such a good idea?'
모모
모모'It has wormwood in it, which suits my mood. But I'll settle for a short Scotch.'
모모
모모'What about you, Mr. Archer?'
모모
모모I asked for the same. I needed it. While I rather liked both of the Hillmans, they were getting on my nerves. Their joint handling of their anxiety was almost professional, as if they were actors improvising a tragedy before an audience of one. I don't mean the anxiety wasn't sincere. They were close to dying of it.
모모
모모Hillman came back across the room with three lowball glasses on a tray. He set it down on a long table in front of the fireplace and handed each of us a glass. Then he shook up the wood fire with a poker. Flames hissed up the chimney. Their reflection changed his face for a moment to a red savage mask.
모모
모모His wife's face hung like a dead moon over her drink. 'Our son is very dear to us, Mr. Archer. Can you help us get him back?'
모모
모모'I can try. I'm not sure it's wise to keep the police out of this. I'm only one man, and this isn't my normal stomping ground.'
모모
모모'Does that make a difference?'
모모
모모'I have no informers here.'
모모
모모'Do you hear him, Ralph?' she said to her crouching husband. 'Mr. Archer thinks we should have the police in.'
모모
모모'I hear him. But it isn't possible.'
모모
모모He straightened up with a sigh, as if the whole weight of the house was on his shoulders. 'I'm not going to endanger Tom's life by anything I do.'
모모
모모'I feel the same way,' she said. 'I'm willing to pay through the nose to get him back. What use is money without a son to spend it on?'
모모
모모That was another phrase that was faintly strange. I was getting the impression that Tom was the center of the household, but a fairly unknown center, like a god they made sacrifices to and expected benefits from, and maybe punishments, too. I was beginning to sympathize with Tom.
모모
모모'Tell me about him, Mrs. Hillman.'
모모
모모Some life came up into her dead face. But before she could open her mouth Hillman said: 'No. You're not going to put Elaine through that now.'
모모
모모'But Tom's a pretty shadowy figure to me. I'm trying to get some idea of where he might have gone yesterday, how he got tangled up with extortionists.'
모모
모모'I don't know where he went,' the woman said.
모모
모모'Neither do I. If I had,' Hillman said, 'I'd have gone to him yesterday.'
모모
모모'Then I'm going to have to go out and do some legwork. You can let me have a picture, I suppose.'
모모
모모Hillman went into an adjoining room, twilit behind pulled drapes, where the open top of a grand piano leaned up out of the shadows. He came back with a silver-framed studio photograph of a boy whose features resembled his own. The boy's dark eyes were rebellious, unless I was projecting my own sense of the household into them. They were also intelligent and imaginative. His mouth was spoiled.
모모
모모'Can I take this out of the frame? Or if you have a smaller one, it would be better to show around.'
모모
모모'To show around?'
모모
모모'That's what I said, Mr. Hillman. It's not for my memory book.'
모모
모모Elaine Hillman said: 'I have a smaller one upstairs on my dressing table. I'll get it.'
모모
모모'Why don't I go up with you? It might help if I went through his room.'
모모
모모'You can look at his room,' Hillman said, 'but I don't want you searching it.'
모모
모모'Why?'
모모
모모'I just don't like the idea. Tom has the right to some privacy, even now.'
모모
모모The three of us went upstairs, keeping an eye on each other. I wondered what Hillman was afraid I might find, but I hesitated to ask him. While everything seemed to be under control, Hillman could flare up at any moment and order me out of his house.
모모
모모He stood at the door while I gave the room a quick once-over. It was a front bedroom, very large, furnished with plain chests of drawers and chairs and a table and a bed which all looked hand-finished and expensive. A bright red telephone sat on the bedside table. There were engravings of sailing ships and Audubon prints hung with geometric precision around the walls, Navajo rugs on the floor, and a wool bedspread matching one of them.
모모
모모I turned to Hillman. 'Was he interested in boats and sailing?'
모모
모모'Not particularly. He used to come out and crew for me occasionally, on the sloop, when I couldn't get anyone else. Does it matter?'
모모
모모'I was just wondering if he hung around the harbor much.'
모모
모모'No. He didn't.'
모모
모모'Was he interested in birds?'
모모
모모'I don't think so.'
모모
모모'Who chose the pictures?'
모모
모모'I did,' Elaine Hillman said from the hallway. 'I decorated the room for Tom. He liked it, didn't he, Ralph?'
모모
모모Hillman mumbled something. I crossed the room to the deeply set front windows, which overlooked the semicircular driveway. I could see down the wooded slope, across the golf course, all the way to the highway, where cars rolled back and forth like children's toys out of reach. I could imagine Tom sitting here in the alcove and watching the highway lights at night.
모모
모모A thick volume of music lay open on the leather seat. I looked at the cover. It was a well-used copy of The Well-Tempered Clavier.
모모
모모'Did Tom play the piano, Mr. Hillman?'
모모
모모'Very well. He had ten years of lessons. But then he wanted-' His wife made a small dismayed sound at his shoulder. 'Why go into all that?'
모모
모모'All what?'
모모
모모I said. 'Trying to get information out of you people is like getting blood out of a stone.'
모모
모모'I feel like a bloodless stone,' she said with a little grimace. 'This hardly seems the time to rake up old family quarrels.'
모모
모모'We didn't quarrel,' her husband said. 'It was the one thing Tom and I ever disagreed on. And he went along with me on it. End of subject.'
모모
모모'All right. Where did he spend his time away from home?'
모모
모모The Hillmans looked at each other, as if the secret of Tom's whereabouts was somehow hidden in each other's faces. The red telephone interrupted their dumb communion, like a loud thought. Elaine Hillman gasped. The photograph in her hand fell to the floor. She wilted against her husband.
모모
모모He held her up. 'It wouldn't be for us. That's Tom's private telephone.'
모모
모모'You want me to take it?'
모모
모모I said through the second ring.
모모
모모'Please do.'
모모
모모I sat on the bed and picked up the receiver. 'Hello.'
모모
모모'Tom?' said a high, girlish voice. 'Is that you, Tommy?'
모모
모모'Who is this calling?'
모모
모모I tried to sound like a boy.
모모
모모The girl said something like 'Augh' and hung up on me. I set down the receiver: 'It was a girl or a young woman. She wanted Tom.'
모모
모모The woman spoke with a touch of malice that seemed to renew her strength: 'That's nothing unusual. I'm sure it was Stella Carlson. She's been calling all week.'
모모
모모'Does she always hang up like that?'
모모
모모'No. I talked to her yesterday. She was full of questions, which of course I refused to answer. But I wanted to make sure that she hadn't seen Tom. She hadn't.'
모모
모모'Does she know anything about what's happened?'
모모
모모'I hope not,' Hillman said. 'We've got to keep it in the family. The more people know, the worse-' He left another sentence dangling in the air.
모모
모모I moved away from the telephone and picked up the fallen photograph. In a kind of staggering march step, Elaine Hillman went to the bed and straightened out the bedspread where I had been sitting. Everything had to be perfect in the room, I thought, or the god would not be appeased and would never return to them. When she had finished smoothing the bed, she flung herself face down on it and lay still.
모모
모모Hillman and I withdrew quietly and went downstairs to wait for the call that mattered. There was a phone in the bar alcove off the sitting room, and another in the butler's pantry, which I could use to listen in. To get to the butler's pantry we had to go through the music room, where the grand piano loomed, and across a formal dining room which had a dismal air, like a reconstructed room in a museum.
모모
모모The past was very strong here, like an odor you couldn't quite place. It seemed to be built into the very shape of the house, with its heavy dark beams and thick walls and deep windows; it would almost force the owner of the house to feel like a feudal lord. But the role of hidalgo hung loosely on Hillman, like something borrowed for a costume party. He and his wife must have rattled around in the great house, even when the boy was there.
모모
모모Back in the sitting room, in front of the uncertain fire, I had a chance to ask Hillman some more questions. The Hillmans had two servants; a Spanish couple named Perez who had looked after Tom from infancy. Mrs. Perez was probably out in the kitchen. Her husband was in Mexico, visiting his family.
모모
모모'You know he's in Mexico?'
모모
모모'Well,' Hillman said, 'his wife has had a card from Sinaloa. Anyway, the Perezes are devoted to us, and to Tom. We've had them with us ever since we moved here and bought this house.'
모모
모모'How long ago was that?'
모모
모모'Over sixteen years. We moved here, the three of us, after I was separated from the Navy. Another engineer and I founded our own firm here, Technological Enterprises. We're had very gratifying success, supplying components to the military and then NASA. I was able to go into semi retirement not long ago.'
모모
모모'You're young to retire, Mr. Hillman.'
모모
모모'Perhaps.'
모모
모모He looked around a little restlessly, as if he disliked talking about himself. 'I'm still the chairman of the board, of course. I go down to the office several mornings a week. I play a lot of golf, do a lot of hunting and sailing.'
모모
모모He sounded weary of his life. 'This summer I've been teaching Tom calculus. It isn't available in his high school. I thought it would come in handy if he made it to Cal Tech or MIT I went to MIT myself. Elaine was a student at Radcliffe. She was born on Beacon Street, you know.'
모모
모모We're prosperous and educated people, he seemed to be saying, first-class citizens: how can the world have aimed such a dirty blow at us? He leaned his large face forward until his hands supported it again.
모모
모모The telephone rang in the alcove. I heard it ring a second time as I skidded around the end of the dining-room table. At the door of the butler's pantry I almost knocked down a small round woman who was wiping her hands on her apron. Her emotional dark eyes recoiled from my face.
모모
모모'I was going to answer it,' she said.
모모
모모'I will, Mrs. Perez.'
모모
모모She retreated into the kitchen and I closed the door after her. The only light in the pantry came through the semicircular hatch to the dining room. The telephone was on the counter inside it, no longer ringing. Gently I raised the receiver.
모모
모모'What was that?' a man's voice said. 'You got the FBI on the line or something?'
모모
모모The voice was a western drawl with a faint whine in it.
모모
모모'Certainly not. I've followed your instructions to the letter.'
모모
모모'I hope I can believe you, Mr. Hillman. If I thought you were having this call traced I'd hang up and goodbye Tom.'
모모
모모The threat came easily, with a kind of flourish, as if the man enjoyed this kind of work.
모모
모모'Don't hang up.'
모모
모모Hillman's voice was both pleading and loathing. 'I have the money for you, at least I'll have it here in a very short while. I'll be ready to deliver it whenever you say.'
모모
모모'Twenty-five thousand in small bills?'
모모
모모'There will be nothing larger than a twenty.'
모모
모모'All unmarked?'
모모
모모'I told you I've obeyed you to the letter. My son's safety is all I care about.'
모모
모모'I'm glad you get the picture, Mr. Hillman. You pick up fast, and I like that. Matter of fact, I hate to do this to you. And I'd certainly hate to do anything to this fine boy of yours.'
모모
모모'Is Tom with you now?'
모모
모모Hillman said.
모모
모모'More or less. He's nearby.'
모모
모모'Could I possibly talk to him?'
모모
모모'No.'
모모
모모'How do I know he's alive?'
모모
모모The man was silent for a long moment. 'You don't trust me, Mr. Hillman. I don't like that.'
모모
모모'How can I trust-?'
모모
모모Hillman bit the sentence in half.
모모
모모'I know what you were going to say. How can you trust a lousy creep like me? That isn't our problem, Hillman. Our problem is can I trust a creep like you. I know more about you than you think I do, Hillman.'
모모
모모Silence, in which breath wheezed.
모모
모모'Well, can I?'
모모
모모'Can you what?'
모모
모모Hillman said in near-despair.
모모
모모'Can I trust you, Hillman?'
모모
모모'You can trust me.'
모모
모모Wheezing silence. The wheeze was in the man's voice when he spoke again: 'I guess I'll have to take your word for it, Hillman. Okay. You'd probably like to talk all day about what a creep I am, but it's time to get down to brass tacks. I want my money, and this isn't ransom money, get that straight. Your son wasn't kidnapped, he came to us of his own free will-' 
모모
모모'I don't-' Hillman strangled the words in his throat.
모모
모모'You don't believe me? Ask him, if you ever have a chance. You're throwing away your chances, you realize that? I'm trying to help you pay me the money-the information money, that's fellow sounded practically subhuman.'
모모
모모His face became gaunter. 'And you tell me I'm at his mercy. I 'Your son is. Could there be any truth in what he said about Tom going to him voluntarily?'
모모
모모'Of course not. Tom is a good boy.'
모모
모모'How is his judgment?'
모모
모모Hillman didn't answer me, except by implication. He went to the bar, poured himself a stiff drink out of a bourbon bottle, and knocked it back. I followed him to the bar.
모모
모모'Is there any possible chance that Tom cooked up this extortion deal himself, with the help of one of his buddies, or maybe with hired help?'
모모
모모He hefted the glass in his hand, as if he was thinking of throwing it at my head. I caught a glimpse of his red angry mask before he turned away. 'It's quite impossible. Why do you have to torment me with these ideas?'
모모
모모'I don't know your son. You ought to.'
모모
모모'He'd never do a thing like that to me.'
모모
모모'You put him in Laguna Perdida School.'
모모
모모'I had to.'
모모
모모'Why? He turned on me furiously. 'You keep hammering away at the same stupid question. What has it got to do with anything?'
모모
모모'I'm trying to find out just how far gone Tom is. If there was reason to think that he kidnapped himself, to punish you or raise money, we'd want to turn the police loose-' 
모모
모모'You're crazy!'
모모
모모'Is Tom?'
모모
모모'Of course not. Frankly, Mr. Archer, I'm getting sick of you and your questions. If you want to stay in my house, it's got to be on my terms.'
모모
모모I was tempted to walk out, but something held me. The case was getting its hooks into my mind.
모모
모모Hillman filled his glass with whisky and drank half of it down.
모모
모모'If I were you, I'd lay off the sauce,' I said. 'You have decisions to make. This could be the most important day of your life.'
모모
모모He nodded slowly. 'You're right.'
모모
모모He reached across the bar and poured the rest of his whisky into the metal sink. Then he excused himself, and went upstairs to see to his wife.
모모
모모5
모모
모모I LET MYSELF out the front door, quietly, got a hat and raincoat out of the trunk of my car, and walked down the winding driveway. In the dead leaves under the oak trees the drip made rustling noises, releasing smells and memories. When I was seventeen I spent a summer working on a dude ranch in the foothills of the Sierra. Toward the end of August, when the air was beginning to sharpen, I found a girl, and before the summer was over we met in the woods. Everything since had been slightly anticlimactic.
모모
모모Growing up seemed to be getting harder. The young people were certainly getting harder to figure out. Maybe Stella Carlson, if I could get to her, could help me understand Tom.
모모
모모The Carlsons' mailbox was a couple of hundred yards down the road. It was a miniature replica, complete with shutters, of their green-shuttered white colonial house, and it rubbed me the wrong way, like a tasteless advertisement. I went up the drive to the brick stoop and knocked on the door.
모모
모모A handsome redheaded woman in a linen dress opened the door and gave me a cool green look. 
모모
모모'Yes?'
모모
모모I didn't think I could get past her without lying. 'I'm in the insurance-'
모모
모모'Soliciting is not allowed in El Rancho.'
모모
모모'I'm not selling, Mrs. Carlson, I'm a claims adjuster.'
모모
모모I got an old card out of my wallet, which supported the statement. I had worked for insurance companies in my time.
모모
모모'If it's about my wrecked car,' she said, 'I thought that was all settled last week.'
모모
모모'We're interested in the cause of the accident. We keep statistics, you know.'
모모
모모'I'm not particularly interested in becoming a statistic.'
모모
모모'Your car already is. I understand it was stolen.'
모모
모모She hesitated, and glanced behind her, as if there was a witness in the hallway. 'Yes,' she said finally. 'It was stolen.'
모모
모모'By some young punk in the neighborhood, is that right?'
모모
모모She hushed in response to my incitement. 'Yes, and I doubt very much that it was an accident. He took my car and wrecked it out of sheer spite.'
모모
모모The words boiled out as if they had been simmering in her mind for days.
모모
모모'That's an interesting hypothesis, Mrs. Carlson. May I come in and talk it over with you?'
모모
모모'I suppose so.'
모모
모모She let me into the hallway. I sat at a telephone table and took out my black notebook. She stood over me with one hand on the newel post at the foot of the stairs.
모모
모모'Do you have anything to support that hypothesis?' I said with my pencil poised.
모모
모모'You mean that he wrecked the car deliberately?'
모모
모모'Yes.'
모모
모모Her white teeth closed on her full red lower lip, and left a brief dent in it.
모모
모모'It's something you couldn't make a statistic out of. The boy-his name is Tom Hillman-was interested in our daughter. He used to be a much nicer boy than he is now. As a matter of fact, he used to spend most of his free time over here. We treated him as if he were our own son. But the relationship went sour. Very sour.'
모모
모모She sounded both angry and regretful.
모모
모모'What soured it?'
모모
모모She made a violent sideways gesture. 'I prefer not to discuss it. It's something an insurance company doesn't have to know. Or anybody else.'
모모
모모'Perhaps I could talk to the boy. He lives next door, doesn't he?'
모모
모모'His parents do, the Hillmans. I believe they've sent him away somewhere. We no longer speak to the Hillmans,' she said stiffly. 'They're decent enough people, I suppose, but they've made awful fools of themselves over that boy.'
모모
모모'Where did they send him?'
모모
모모'To some kind of reform school, probably. He needed it. He was running out of control.'
모모
모모'In what way?'
모모
모모'Every way. He smashed up my car, which probably means he was drinking. I know he was spending time in the bars on lower Main Street.'

모모
모모'The night before he wrecked your car?'
모모
모모'All summer. He even tried to teach his bad habits to Stella. That's what soured the relationship, if you want to know.'
모모
모모I made a note. 'Could you be a little more specific, Mrs. Carlson? We're interested in the whole social background of these accidents.'
모모
모모'Well, he actually dragged Stella with him to one of those awful dives. Can you imagine, taking an innocent sixteen-year old girl to a wino joint on lower Main? That was the end of Tom Hillman, as far as we were concerned.'
모모
모모'What about Stella?'
모모
모모'She's a sensible girl.'
모모
모모She glanced up toward the head of the stairs. 'Her father and I made her see that it wasn't a profitable relationship.'
모모
모모'So she wasn't involved in the borrowing of your car?'
모모
모모'Certainly not.'
모모
모모A small clear voice said from the head of the stairs: 'That isn't true, Mother, and you know it. I told you-' 
모모
모모'Be quiet, Stella. Go back to bed. If you're ill enough to stay home from camp, you're ill enough to stay in bed.'
모모
모모As she was talking, Mrs. Carlson surged halfway up the stairs. She had very good calves, a trifle muscular. Her daughter came down toward her, a slender girl with lovely eyes that seemed to take up most of her face below the forehead. Her brown hair was pulled back tight. She had on slacks and a high-necked blue wool sweater, which revealed the bud-sharp outlines of her breasts.
모모
모모'I'm feeling better, thank you,' she said with adolescent iciness. 'At least I was, until I heard you lying about Tommy.'
모모
모모'How dare you? Go to your room.'
모모
모모'I will if you'll stop telling lies about Tommy.'
모모
모모'You shut up.'
모모
모모Mrs. Carlson ran up the three or four steps that separated them, grabbed Stella by the shoulders, turned her forcibly, and marched her up out of sight. Stella kept repeating the word 'Liar,' until a door slammed on her thin clear voice.
모모
모모Five minutes later Mrs. Carlson came down wearing fresh makeup, a green hat with a feather in it, a plaid coat, and gloves. She walked straight to the door and opened it wide.
모모
모모'I'm afraid I have to rush now. My hairdresser gets very angry with me when I'm late. We were getting pretty far afield from what you wanted, anyway.'
모모
모모'On the contrary. I was very interested in your daughter's remarks.'
모모
모모She smiled with fierce politeness. 'Pay no attention to Stella. She's feverish and hysterical. The poor child's been upset ever since the accident.'
모모
모모'Because she was involved in it?'
모모
모모'Don't be silly.'
모모
모모She rattled the doorknob. 'I really have to go now.'
모모
모모I stepped outside. She followed, and slammed the door hard behind me. She'd probably had a lot of practice slamming doors.
모모
모모'Where's your car?' she called after me.
모모
모모'I parachuted in.'
모모
모모She stood and watched me until I reached the foot of the driveway. Then she went back into her house. I plodded back to the Hillmans' mailbox and turned up their private lane. The rustlings in the woods were getting louder. I thought it was a towhee scratching in the undergrowth. But it was Stella.
모모
모모She appeared suddenly beside the trunk of a tree, wearing a blue ski jacket with the hood pulled up over her head and tied under her chin. She looked about twelve. She beckoned me with the dignity of a full-grown woman, ending the motion with her finger at her lips.
모모
모모'I better stay out of sight. Mother will be looking for me.'
모모
모모'I thought she had an appointment with the hairdresser.'
모모
모모'That was just another lie,' she said crisply. 'She's always lying these days.'
모모
모모'Why?'
모모
모모'I guess people get in the habit of it or something. Mother always used to be a very straight talker. So did Dad. But this business about Tommy has sort of thrown them. It's thrown me, too,' she added, and coughed into her hand.
모모
모모'You shouldn't be out in the wet,' I said. 'You're sick.'
모모
모모'No, really, I mean not physically. I just don't feel like facing the kids at camp and having to answer their questions.' 
모모
모모'About Tommy?'
모모
모모She nodded. 'I don't even know where he is. Do you?'
모모
모모'No, I don't.'
모모
모모'Are you a policeman, or what?'
모모
모모'I used to be a policeman. Now I'm a what.'
모모
모모She wrinkled her nose and let out a little giggle. Then she tensed in a listening attitude, like a yearling fawn. She threw off her hood.
모모
모모'Do you hear her? That's Mother calling me.'
모모
모모Far off through the trees I heard a voice calling: 'Stella.'
모모
모모'She'll kill me,' the girl said. 'But somebody has to tell the truth some time. I know. Tommy has a tree house up the slope, I mean he used to have when he was younger. We can talk there.'
모모
모모I followed her up a half-overgrown foot trail. A little redwood shack with a tar-paper roof sat on a low platform among the spreading branches of an oak. A homemade ladder, weathered gray like the tree house, slanted up to the platform. Stella climbed up first and went inside. A red-capped woodpecker flew out of an unglazed window into the next tree, where he sat and harangued us. Mrs. Carlson's voice floated up from the foot of the slope. She had a powerful voice, but it was getting hoarse.
모모
모모'Swiss Family Robinson,' Stella said when I went in. She was sitting on the edge of a built-in cot which had a mattress but no blankets. 'Tommy and I used to spend whole days up here, when we were children.'
모모
모모At sixteen, there was nostalgia in her voice. 'Of course when we reached puberty it had to stop. It wouldn't have been proper.'
모모
모모'You're fond of Tommy.'
모모
모모'Yes. I love him. We're going to be married. But don't get the wrong idea about us. We're not even going steady. We're not making out and we're not soldered.'
모모
모모She wrinkled her nose, as if she didn't like the smell of the words. 'We'll be married when the time is right, when Tommy's through college or at least has a good start. We won't have any money problems, you see.'
모모
모모I thought she was using me to comfort herself a little with a story, a simple story with a happy ending. 'How is that?'
모모
모모'Tommy's parents have lots of money.'
모모
모모'What about your parents? Will they let you marry him?'
모모
모모'They won't be able to stop me.'
모모
모모I believed her, if Tommy survived. She must have seen the 'if cross my eyes like a shadow. She was a perceptive girl.
모모
모모'Is Tommy all right?' she said in a different tone.
모모
모모'I hope so.'
모모
모모She reached up and plucked at my sleeve. 'Where is he, Mister-?'
모모
모모'I don't know, Stella. My name is Lew Archer. I'm a private detective working on Tommy's side. And you were going to tell me the truth about the accident.'
모모
모모'Yes. It was my fault. Mother and Dad seem to think they have to cover up for me, but it only makes things worse for Tommy. I was the one responsible, really.'
모모
모모Her direct upward look, her earnest candor, reminded me of a child saying her prayers.
모모
모모'Were you driving the car?'
모모
모모'No. I don't mean I was with him. But I told him he could take it and I got the key for him out of Mother's room. It's really my car, too-I mean, to use.'
모모
모모'She knows this?'
모모
모모'Yes. I told her and Dad on Sunday. But they had already talked to the police, and after that they wouldn't change their story, or let me. They said it didn't alter the fact that he took it.'
모모
모모'Why did you let him take it?'
모모
모모'I admit it wasn't such a good idea. But he had to go someplace to see somebody and his father wouldn't let him use one of their cars. He was grounded. Mother and Dad were gone for the evening, and Tommy said he'd be back in a couple of hours. It was only about eight o'clock, and I thought it would be okay. I didn't know he was going to be out all night.'
모모
모모She closed her eyes and hugged herself. 'I was awake all night, listening for him.'
모모
모모'Where did he go?'
모모
모모'I don't know.'
모모
모모'What was he after?'
모모
모모'I don't know that, either. He said it was the most important thing in his life.'
모모
모모'Could he have been talking about alcohol?'
모모
모모'Tommy doesn't drink. It was somebody he had to see, somebody very important.'
모모
모모'Like a drug pusher?'
모모
모모She opened her wonderful eyes. 'You're twisting meanings, the way Dad does when he's mad at me. Are you mad at me, Mr. Archer?'
모모
모모'No. I'm grateful to you for being honest.'
모모
모모'Then why do you keep dragging in crummy meanings?'
모모
모모'I'm used to questioning crummy people, I guess. And sometimes an addict's own mother, or own girl, doesn't know he's using drugs.'
모모
모모'I'm sure Tommy wasn't. He was dead against it. He knew what it had done to some-' She covered her mouth with her hand. Her nails were bitten.
모모
모모'You were going to say?'
모모
모모'Nothing.'
모모
모모Our rapport was breaking down. I did my best to save it. 'Listen to me, Stella, I'm not digging dirt for the fun of it. Tommy's in real danger. If he had contacts with drug users, you should tell me.'
모모
모모'They were just some of his musician friends,' she mumbled. 'They wouldn't do anything to hurt him.'
모모
모모'They may have friends who would. Who are these people?'
모모
모모'Just some people he played the piano with this summer, till his father made him quit. Tommy used to sit in on their jam sessions on Sunday afternoon at The Barroom Floor.'
모모
모모'Is that one of the dives your mother mentioned?'
모모
모모'It isn't a dive. He didn't take me to dives. It was merely a place where they could get together and play their instruments. He wanted me to hear them play.'
모모
모모'And Tommy played with them?'
모모
모모She nodded brightly. 'He's a very good pianist, good enough to make his living at it. They even offered him a weekend job.'
모모
모모'Who did?'
모모
모모'The combo at The Barroom Floor. His father wouldn't let him take it, naturally.'
모모
모모'Tell me about the people in the combo.'
모모
모모'Sam Jackman is the only one I know. He used to be a locker boy at the beach club. He plays the trombone. Then there was a saxophonist and a trumpeter and a drummer. I don't remember their names.'
모모
모모'What did you think of them?'
모모
모모'I didn't think they were very good. But Tommy said they were planning to make an album.'
모모
모모'Every combo is. I mean, what kind of people were they?'
모모
모모'They were just musicians. Tommy seemed to like them.'
모모
모모'How much time had he been spending with them?'
모모
모모'Just Sunday afternoons. He called it his other life.'
모모
모모'His other life?'
모모
모모'Uh-huh. You know, at home he had to hit the books and make his parents feel good and all that stuff. The same way I have to do when I'm at home. But it hasn't been working too well since the accident. Nobody feels good.'
모모
모모She shivered. A cold wet wind was blowing through the windows of the tree house. Mrs. Carlson's voice could no longer be heard. I felt uneasy about keeping the girl away from her mother. But I didn't want to let her go until she had told me everything she could.
모모
모모I squatted on my heels in front of her. 'Stella, do you think Tommy's appointment that Saturday night had to do with his musician friends?'
모모
모모'No. He would have told me if it had. It was more of a secret than that.'
모모
모모'Did he say so?'
모모
모모'He didn't have to. It was something secret and terribly important. He was terribly excited.'
모모
모모'In a good way or a bad way?'
모모
모모'I don't know how you tell the difference. He wasn't afraid, if that's what you mean.'
모모
모모'I'm trying to ask you if he was sick.'
모모
모모'Sick?'
모모
모모'Emotionally sick.'
모모
모모'No. I-That's foolish.'
모모
모모'Then why did his father have him put away?'
모모
모모'You mean, put away in a mental hospital?' She leaned toward me, so close I could feel her breath on my face.
모모
모모'Something like that-Laguna Perdida School. I didn't mean to tell you, and I'm going to ask you not to tell your parents.'
모모
모모'Don't worry, I'll never tell them anything. So that's where he is! Those hypocrites!'
모모
모모Her eyes were fixed and wet. 'You said he was in danger. Are they trying to cut out his frontal lobe like in Tennessee Williams?'
모모
모모'No. He was in no danger where he was. But he escaped from the place, the night before last, and fell into the hands of thieves. Now, I'm not going to load your mind with any more of this. I'm sorry it came out.'
모모
모모'Don't be.'
모모
모모She gave me a second glimpse of the woman she was on her way to becoming. 'If it's happening to Tommy, it's just like it was happening to me.'
모모
모모Her forefinger tapped through nylon at the bone between her little breasts. 'You said he fell into the hands of thieves. Who are they?'
모모
모모'I'm trying to answer that question, in a hurry. Could they be his friends from The Barroom Floor?'
모모
모모She shook her head. 'Are they holding him prisoner or something?'
모모
모모'Yes. I'm trying to get to them before they do something worse. If you know of any other contacts he had in his other life, particularly underworld contacts-' 'No. He didn't have any. He didn't have another life, really. It was just talk, talk and music.'
모모
모모Her lips were turning blue. I had a sudden evil image of myself a heavy hunched figure seen from above in the act of tormenting a child who was already tormented. A sense went through me of the appalling ease with which the things you do in a good cause can slip over into bad.
모모
모모'You'd better go home, Stella.'
모모
모모She folded her arms. 'Not until you tell me everything. I'm not a child.'
모모
모모'But this is confidential information. I didn't intend to let any of it out. If it got to the wrong people, it would only make things worse.'
모모
