모모She said with some scorn: 'You keep beating around the bush, like Dad. Is Tommy being held for ransom?'
모모
모모'Yes, but I'm pretty sure it's no ordinary kidnapping. He's supposed to have gone to these people of his own free will.'
모모
모모'Who said so?'
모모
모모'One of them.'
모모
모모Her clear brow puckered. 'Then why would Tommy be in any danger from them?'
모모
모모'If he knows them,' I said, 'they're not likely to let him come home. He could identify them.'
모모
모모'I see.'
모모
모모Her eyes were enormous, taking in all at once the horror of the world and growing dark with it. 'I was afraid he was in some awful jam. His mother wouldn't tell me anything. I thought maybe he'd killed himself and they were keeping it quiet.'
모모
모모'What made you think that?'
모모
모모'Tommy did. He called me up and I met him here in the tree house the morning after the accident. I wasn't supposed to tell anyone. But you've been honest with me. He wanted to see me one more time-just as friends, you know-and say goodbye forever. I asked him if he was going away, or what he planned to do. He wouldn't tell me.'
모모
모모'Was he suicidal?'
모모
모모'I don't know. I was afraid that that was what it all meant. Not hearing from him since, I got more and more worried. I'm not as worried now as I was before you told me all those things.'
모모
모모She did a mental double take on one of them. 'But why would he deliberately go and stay with criminals?'
모모
모모'It isn't clear. He may not have known they were criminals. If you can think of anyone-' 
모모
모모'I'm trying.' She screwed up her face, and finally shook her head again. 'I can't, unless they were the same people he had to see that other Saturday night. When he borrowed our car.'
모모
모모'Did he tell you anything at all about those people?'
모모
모모'Just that he was terribly keen about seeing them.'
모모
모모'Were they men or women?'
모모
모모'I don't even know that.'
모모
모모'What about the Sunday morning, when you met him here? Did he tell you anything at all about the night before?'
모모
모모'No. He was feeling really low, after the accident and all, and the terrible row with his parents. I didn't ask him any questions. I guess I should have, shouldn't I? I always do the wrong thing, either by commission or omission.'
모모
모모'I think you do the right thing more often than most.'
모모
모모'Mother doesn't think so. Neither does Dad.'
모모
모모'Parents can be mistaken.'
모모
모모'Are you a parent?'
모모
모모The question reminded me of the sad boys in Laguna Perdida School.
모모
모모'No, I never have been. My hands are clean.'
모모
모모'You're making fun of me,' she said with a glum face.
모모
모모'Never. Hardly ever.'
모모
모모She gave me a quick smile. 'Gilbert and Sullivan. I didn't know detectives were like you.'
모모
모모'Neither do most of the other detectives.'
모모
모모Our rapport, which came and went, was flourishing again. 'There's one other thing I've been meaning to ask you, Stella. Your mother seems to believe that Tommy wrecked her car on purpose.'
모모
모모'I know she does.'
모모
모모'Could there be any truth in it?'
모모
모모She considered the question. 'I don't see how. He wouldn't do it to me, or her, unless-' She looked up in dark surmise.
모모
모모'Go on.'
모모
모모'Unless he was trying to kill himself, and didn't care about anything any more.'
모모
모모'Was he?'
모모
모모'He may have been. He didn't want to come home, he told me that much. But he didn't tell me why.'
모모
모모'I might learn something from examining the car. Do you know where it is?'
모모
모모'It's down in Ringo's wrecking yard. Mother went to see it the other day.'
모모
모모'Why?'
모모
모모'It helps her to stay mad, I guess. Mother's really crazy about Tommy, at least she used to be, and so was Dad. This business has been terribly hard on them. And I'm not making it any easier staying away from home now.'
모모
모모She got to her feet, stamping them rapidly. 'Mother will be calling out the gendarmes. Also she'll kill me.'
모모
모모'No she won't.'
모모
모모'Yes she will.'
모모
모모But she wasn't basically afraid for herself. 'If you find out anything about Tommy, will you let me know?'
모모
모모'That might be a little tough to do, in view of your mother's attitude. Why don't you get in touch with me when you can? This number will always get me, through my answering service.'
모모
모모I gave her a card.
모모
모모She climbed down the ladder and flitted away through the trees, one of those youngsters who make you feel like apologizing for the world.
모모
모모6
모모
모모I MADE MY way back to the Hillmans' house. It resembled a grim white fortress under the lowering sky. I didn't feel like going in just now and grappling with the heavy, smothering fear that hung in the rooms. Anyway, I finally had a lead. Which Hillman could have given me if he'd wanted to.
모모
모모Before I got into my car I looked up at the front window of Tom's room. The Hillmans were sitting close together in the niche of the window, looking out. Hillman shook his head curtly: no phone call.
모모
모모I drove into town and turned right off the highway onto the main street. The stucco and frame buildings in this segment of town, between the highway and the railroad tracks, had been here a long time and been allowed to deteriorate. There were tamale parlors and pool halls and rummage stores and bars. The wet pavements were almost empty of people, as they always were when it rained in California.
모모
모모I parked and locked my car in front of a surplus and sporting goods store and asked the proprietor where The Barroom Floor was. He pointed west, toward the ocean: 'I don't think they're open, in the daytime. There's lots of other places open.'
모모
모모'What about Ringo's auto yard?'
모모
모모'Three blocks south on Sanger Street, that's the first stop-light below the railroad tracks.'
모모
모모I thanked him.
모모
모모'You're welcome, I'm sure.'
모모
모모He was a middle-aged man with a sandy moustache, cheerfully carrying a burden of unsuccess. 'I can sell you a rainproof cover for your hat.'
모모
모모'How much?'
모모
모모'Ninety-eight cents. A dollar-two with tax.'
모모
모모I bought one. He put it on my hat. 'It doesn't do much for the appearance, but-' 'Beauty is functional.'
모모
모모He smiled and nodded. 'You took the words out of my mouth. I figured you were a smart man. My name's Botkin, by the way, Joseph Botkin.'
모모
모모'Lew Archer.'
모모
모모We shook hands.
모모
모모'My pleasure, Mr. Archer. If I'm not getting too personal, why would a man like you want to do your drinking at The Barroom Floor?'
모모
모모'What's the matter with The Barroom Floor?'
모모
모모'I don't like the way they handle their business, that's all. It lowers the whole neighborhood. Which is low enough already, God knows.'
모모
모모'How do they handle their business?'
모모
모모'They let young kids hang out there, for one thing-I'm not saying they serve them liquor. But they shouldn't let them in at all.'
모모
모모'What do they do for another thing?'
모모
모모'I'm talking too much.'
모모
모모He squinted at me shrewdly. 'And you ask a lot of questions. You wouldn't be from the Board of Equalization by any chance?'
모모
모모'No, but I probably wouldn't tell you if I was. Is The Barroom Floor under investigation?'
모모
모모'I wouldn't be surprised. I heard there was a complaint put in on them.'
모모
모모'From a man named Hillman?'
모모
모모'Yeah. You are from the Board of Equalization, eh? If you want to look the place over for yourself, it opens at five.'
모모
모모It was twenty past four. I wandered along the street, looking through the windows of pawnshops at the loot of wrecked lives.
모모
모모The Barroom Floor was closed all right. It looked as if it was never going to reopen. Over the red-checked half-curtains at the windows, I peered into the dim interior. Red-checked tables and chairs were grouped around a dime-size dance floor; and farther back in the shadows was a bandstand decorated with gaudy paper. It looked so deserted you'd have thought all the members of the band had hocked their instruments and left town years ago.
모모
모모I went back to my car and drove down Sanger Street to Ringo's yard. It was surrounded by a high board fence on which his name was painted in six-foot white letters. I pushed in through the gate. A black German shepherd glided out of the open door of a shack and delicately grasped my right wrist between his large yellow teeth. He didn't growl or anything. He merely held me, looking up brightly at my face.
모모
모모A wide fat man, with a medicine ball of stomach badly concealed under his plaid shirt, came to the door of the shack.
모모
모모'That's all right, Lion.'
모모
모모The dog let go of me and went to the fat man.
모모
모모'His teeth are dirty,' I said. 'You should give him bones to chew. I don't mean wristbones.'
모모
모모'Sorry. We weren't expecting any customers. But he won't hurt you, will you, Lion?'
모모
모모Lion rolled his eyes and let his tongue hang out about a foot.
모모
모모'Go ahead, pet him.'
모모
모모'I'm a dog lover,' I said, 'but is he a man lover?'
모모
모모'Sure. Go ahead and pet him.'
모모
모모I went ahead and petted him. Lion lay down on his back with his feet in the air, grinning up at me with his fangs.
모모
모모'What can I do you for, mister?' Ringo said.
모모
모모'I want to look at a car.'
모모
모모He waved his hand toward the yard. 'I got hundreds of them. But there isn't a one of them you could drive away. You want one to cannibalize?'
모모
모모'This is a particular car I want to examine.'
모모
모모I produced my adjuster's card. 'It's a fairly new Dodge, I think, belonged to a Mrs. Carlson, wrecked a week or so ago.'
모모
모모'Yeah. I'll show it to you.'
모모
모모He put on a black rubber raincoat. Lion and I followed him down a narrow aisle between two lines of wrecked cars. With their crumpled grilles and hoods, shattered windshields, torn fenders, collapsed roofs, disemboweled seats, and blown-out tires, they made me think of some ultimate freeway disaster. Somebody with an eye for detail should make a study of automobile graveyards, I thought, the way they study the ruins and potsherds of vanished civilizations. It could provide a clue as to why our civilization is vanishing.
모모
모모'All the ones in this line are totaled out,' Ringo said. 'This is the Carlson job, second from the end. That Pontiac came in since. Head-on collision, two dead.'
모모
모모He shuddered. 'I never go on the highway when I can help it.'
모모
모모'What caused the accident to the Carlson car?'
모모
모모'It was taken for a joy ride by one of the neighbors' kids, a boy name of Hillman. You know how these young squirts are-if it isn't theirs, they don't care what happens to it. According to the traffic detail, he missed a curve and went off the road and probably turned it over trying to get back on. He must of rolled over several times and ended up against a tree.'
모모
모모I walked around the end of the line and looked over the Dart from all sides. There were deep dents in the roof and hood and all four fenders, as if it had been hit with random sledgehammers. The windshield was gone. The doors were sprung.
모모
모모Leaning in through the left-hand door, I noticed an oval piece of white plastic, stamped with printing, protruding from the space between the driver's seat and the back. I reached in and pulled it out. It was a brass door key. The printing on the plastic tab said: DACK'S AUTO COURT 7.
모모
모모'Watch the glass in there,' Ringo said behind me. 'What are you looking for?'
모모
모모I put the key in my pocket before I turned around. 'I can't figure out why the boy didn't get hurt.'
모모
모모'He had the wheel to hang on to, remember. Lucky for him it didn't break.'
모모
모모'Is there any chance he wrecked the car on purpose?'
모모
모모'Naw. He'd have to be off his rocker to do that. Course you can't put anything past these kids nowadays. Can you, Lion?'
모모
모모He stooped to touch the dog's head and went on talking, either to it or to me. 'My own son that I brought up in the business went off to college and now he don't even come home for Christmas some years. I got nobody to take over the business.'
모모
모모He straightened up and looked around at his wrecks with stern affection, like the emperor of a wasteland.
모모
모모'Could there have been anybody with him in the car?'
모모
모모'Naw. They would of been really banged up, with no seat belts and nothing to hang on to.'
모모
모모He looked at the sky, and added impatiently: 'I don't mind standing around answering your questions, mister. But if you really want the dope on the accident, talk to the traffic detail. I'm closing up.'
모모
모모It was ten minutes to five. I made my way back to The Barroom Floor. Somebody had turned on a few lights inside. The front door was still locked. I went back to my car and waited. I took out the Dack's Auto Court key and looked at it, wondering if it meant anything. It could have meant, among other farfetched possibilities, that the handsome Mrs. Carlson was unfaithful to her husband.
모모
모모Shortly after five a short dark man in a red jacket unlocked the front door of The Barroom Floor and took up his position behind the bar. I went in and sat down on a stool opposite him. He seemed much taller behind the bar. I looked over it and saw that he was standing on a wooden platform about a foot off the floor.
모모
모모'Yeah,' he said 'it keeps me on the level. Without it I can barely see over the bar.'
모모
모모He grinned. 'My wife, now, is five foot six and built in proportion. She ought to be here now,' he added in a disciplinary tone, and looked at the wristwatch on his miniature wrist. 'What will you have?'
모모
모모'Whisky sour. You own this place?'
모모
모모'Me and the wife, we have an interest in it.'
모모
모모'Nice place,' I said, though it wasn't particularly nice. It was no cleaner and no more cheerful than the average bar and grill with cabaret pretensions. The old waiter leaning against the wall beside the kitchen door seemed to be sleeping on his feet.
모모
모모'Thank you. We have plans for it.'
모모
모모As he talked, he made my drink with expert fingers. 'You haven't been in before. I don't remember your face.'
모모
모모'I'm from Hollywood. I hear you have a pretty fair jazz combo.'
모모
모모'Yeah.'
모모
모모'Will they be playing tonight?'
모모
모모'They only play Friday and Saturday nights. We don't get the weekday trade to justify 'em.'
모모
모모'What about the Sunday jam sessions? Are they still on?'
모모
모모'Yeah. We had one yesterday. The boys were in great form. Too bad you missed them.'
모모
모모He slid my drink across the bar. 'You in the music business?'
모모
모모'I represent musicians from time to time. I have an office on the Strip.'
모모
모모'Sam would want to talk to you. He's the leader.'
모모
모모'Where can I get in touch with him?'
모모
모모'I have his address somewhere. Just a minute, please.'
모모
모모A couple of young men in business suits with rain-sprinkled shoulders had taken seats at the far end of the bar. They were talking in carrying voices about a million-dollar real-estate deal. Apparently it was somebody else's deal, not theirs, but they seemed to enjoy talking about it.
모모
모모The small man served them short whiskies without being asked. A lavishly built young woman came in and struggled out of a transparent raincoat, which she rolled up and tossed under the bar. She had a Sicilian nose. Her neck was hung with jewelry like a bandit princess's.
모모
모모The small man looked at her sternly. 'You're late. I can't operate without a hostess.'
모모
모모'I'm sorry, Tony. Rachel was late again.'
모모
모모'Hire another baby sitter.'
모모
모모'But she's so good with the baby. You wouldn't want just anybody feeding him.'
모모
모모'We won't talk about it now. You know where you're supposed to be.'
모모
모모'Yes, Mr. Napoleon.'
모모
모모With a rebellious swing of the hip, she took up her post by the door. Customers were beginning to drift in by twos and fours. Most of them were young or young middle-aged. They looked respectable enough. Talking and laughing vivaciously, clinking her jewelry, the hostess guided them to the red-checked tables.
모모
모모Her husband remembered me after a while. 'Here's Sam Jackman's address. He has no phone, but it isn't far from here.'
모모
모모He handed me a sheet from a memo pad on which he had written in pencil: '169 Mimosa, apt. 2.'
모모
모모It was near the railroad tracks, an old frame house with Victorian gingerbread on the facade half chewed away by time. The heavy carved front door was standing open, and I went into the hallway, feeling warped parquetry under my feet. On a closed door to my right, a number 2 stamped from metal hung upside down by a single nail. It rattled when I knocked.
모모
모모A yellow-faced man in shirt sleeves looked out. 'Who is it you want?'
모모
모모'Sam Jackman.'
모모
모모'That's me.'
모모
모모He seemed surprised that anybody should want him. 'Is it about a job?'
모모
모모He asked the question with a kind of hollow hopefulness that answered itself in the negative.
모모
모모'No, but I want to talk to you about something important, Mr. Jackman.'
모모
모모He caught the 'mister' and inclined his head in acknowledgement. 'All right.'
모모
모모'May I come in? My name is Lew Archer. I'm a private detective.'
모모
모모'I dunno, the place is a mess. With the wife working all day-but come on in.'
모모
모모He backed into his apartment, as if he was afraid to expose his flank. It consisted of one large room, which might once have been the drawing room of the house. It still had its fine proportions, but the lofty ceiling was scabbed and watermarked, the windows hung with torn curtains. A cardboard wardrobe, a gas plate behind a screen, stood against the inner wall. Run-down furniture, including an unmade double bed in one corner, cluttered the bare wooden floor. On a table beside the bed, a small television set was reeling off the disasters of the day in crisp elocutionary sentences.
모모
모모Jackman switched it off, picked up a smoking cigarette from the lid of a coffee can on the table, and sat on the edge of the bed. It wasn't a marihuana cigarette. He was completely still and silent, waiting for me to explain myself. I sat down facing him.
모모
모모'I'm looking for Tom Hillman.'
모모
모모He gave me a swift glance that had fear in it, then busied himself putting out his cigarette. He dropped the butt into the pocket of his shirt.
모모
모모'I didn't know he was missing.'
모모
모모'He is.'
모모
모모'That's too bad. What would make you think that he was here?'
모모
모모He looked around the room with wide unblinking eyes. 'Did Mr. Hillman send you?'
모모
모모'No.'
모모
모모He didn't believe me. 'I just wondered. My Hillman has been on my back.'
모모
모모'Why?'
모모
모모'I interested myself in his boy,' he said carefully.
모모
모모'In what way?'
모모
모모'Personally.'
모모
모모He turned his hands palms upward on his knees. 'I heard him doodling on the piano at the beach club. That was one day last spring. I did a little doodling of my own. Piano isn't my instrument, but he got interested in some chords I showed him. That made me a bad influence.'
모모
모모'Were you?'
모모
모모'Mr. Hillman thought so. He got me fired from the beach club. He didn't want his precious boy messing with the likes of me.'
모모
모모His upturned hands lay like helpless pink-bellied animals on their backs. 'If Mr. Hillman didn't send you, who did?'
모모
모모'A man named Dr Sponti.'
모모
모모I thought the name would mean nothing to him, but he gave me another of his quick fearful looks. 'Sponti? You mean-?'
모모
모모He fell silent.
모모
모모'Go on, Mr. Jackman. Tell me what I mean.'
모모
모모He huddled down into himself, like a man slumping into sudden old age. He let his speech deteriorate: 'I wouldn't know nothin' about nothin', mister.'
모모
모모He opened his mouth in an idiotic smile that showed no teeth.
모모
모모'I think you know a good deal. I think I'll sit here until you tell me some of it.'
모모
모모'That's your privilege,' he said, although it wasn't.
모모
모모He took the butt out of his shirt pocket and lit it with a kitchen match. He dropped the distorted black match-end into the coffee lid. We looked at each other through smoke that drifted like ectoplasm from his mouth.
모모
모모'You know Dr Sponti, do you?'
모모
모모'I've heard the name,' Jackman said.
모모
모모'Have you seen Tom Hillman in the last two days?'
모모
모모He shook his head, but his eyes stayed on my face in a certain way, as if he was expecting to be challenged.
모모
모모'Where have you heard Sponti's name?'
모모
모모'A relative of mine. She used to work in the kitchen at LPS. He said with irony: 'That makes me an accessory, I guess.'
모모
모모'Accessory to what?'
모모
모모'Any crime in the book. I wouldn't even have to know what happened, would I?'
모모
모모He doused his butt in a carefully restricted show of anger.
모모
모모'That sort of talk gets us nowhere.'
모모
모모'Where does your sort of talk get us? Anything I tell you is evidence against me, isn't it?'
모모
모모'You talk like a man with a record.'
모모
모모'I've had my troubles.' He added after a long silence: 'I'm sorry Tommy Hillman is having his.'
모모
모모'You seem to be fond of him.'
모모
모모'We took to each other.' He threw the line away.
모모
모모'I wish you'd tell me more about him. That's really what I came here for.'
모모
모모My words sounded slightly false. I was suspicious of Jackman, and he knew it. He was a watcher and a subtle listener.
모모
모모'Now I got a different idea,' he said. 'I got the idea you're after Tommy to put him back in the LP School. Correct me if I'm wrong.'
모모
모모'You're wrong.'
모모
모모'I don't believe you.'
모모
모모He was watching my hands to see if I might hit him. There were marks on his face where he had been hit before. 'No offense, but I don't believe you, mister-' 
모모
모모I repeated my name. 'Do you know where Tommy is?'
모모
모모'No. I do know this. If Mr. Hillman put him in the L P School, he's better off on the loose than going home. His father had no right to do it to him.'
모모
모모'So I've been told.'
모모
모모'Who told you?'
모모
모모'One of the women on the staff there. She said Tom wasn't disturbed in her opinion, and didn't belong in the school. Tom seemed to agree with her. He broke out Saturday night.'
모모
모모'Good.'
모모
모모'Not so good. At least he was safe there.'
모모
모모'He's safe,' Jackman said, and quickly regretted saying it. He opened his mouth in its senseless toothless smile, a tragic mask pretending to be comic.
모모
모모'Where is he then?'
모모
모모Jackman shrugged his thin shoulders. 'I told you before and I'll tell you again, I don't know.'
모모
모모'How did you know that he was on the loose?'
모모
모모'Sponti wouldn't send you to me otherwise.'
모모
모모'You're quick on the uptake.'
모모
모모'I have to pick up what I can,' he said. 'You talk a lot without saying much.'
모모
모모'You say even less. But you'll talk, Sam.'
모모
모모He rose in a quick jerky movement and went to the door. I thought he was going to tell me to leave, but he didn't. He stood against the closed door in the attitude of a man facing a rifle squad.
모모
모모'What do you expect me to do?' he cried. 'Put my neck in the noose so Hillman can hang me?'
모모
모모I walked toward him.
모모
모모'Stay away from me!' The fear in his eyes was burning brightly, feeding on a long fuse of experience. He lifted one crooked arm to shield his head. 'Don't touch me!'
모모
모모'Calm down. That's hysterical talk, about a noose.'
모모
모모'It's a hysterical world. I lost my job for teaching his kid some music. Now Hillman is raising the ante. What's the rap this time?'
모모
모모'There is no rap if the boy is safe. You said he was. Didn't you?'
모모
모모No answer, but he looked at me under his arm. He had tears in his eyes.
모모
모모'For God's sake, Sam, we ought to be able to get together on this. You like the boy, you don't want anything bad to happen to him. That's all I have in mind.'
모모
모모'There's bad and bad.'
모모
모모But he lowered his defensive arm and kept on studying my face.
모모
모모'I know there's bad and bad,' I said. 'The line between them isn't straight and narrow. The difference between them isn't black and white. I know you favor Tom against his father. You don't want him cut off from you or your kind of music. And you think I want to drag him back to a school where he doesn't belong.'
모모
모모'Aren't you?'
모모
모모'I'm trying to save his life. I think you can help me.'
모모
모모'How?'
모모
모모'Let's sit down again and talk quietly the way we were. Come on. And stop seeing Hillman when you look at me.'
모모
모모Jackman returned to the bed and I sat near him.
모모
모모'Well, Sam, have you seen him in the last two days?'
모모
모모'See who? Mr. Hillman?'
모모
모모'Don't go into the idiot act again. You're an intelligent man. Just answer my question.'
모모
모모'Before I do, will you answer one of mine?'
모모
모모'If I possibly can.'
모모
모모'When you say you're trying to save his life, you mean save him from bad influences, don't you, put him back in Squares-ville with all the other squares?'
모모
모모'Worse things can happen to a boy.'
모모
모모'You didn't answer my question.'
모모
모모'You could have asked a better one. I mean save him from death. He's in the hands of people who may or may not decide to kill him, depending on how the impulse takes them. Am I telling you anything you don't know?'
모모

모모'You sure are, man.'
모모
모모His voice was sincere, and his eyes filled up with compunction. But he and I could talk for a year, and he would still be holding something back. Among the things he was holding back was the fact that he didn't believe me.
모모
모모'Why don't you believe me, Sam?'
모모
모모'I didn't say that.'
모모
모모'You don't have to. You're acting it out, by sitting on the information you have.'
모모
모모'I ain't sitting' on nothin', 'ceptin' this here old raunchy bed,' he said in broad angry parody.
모모
모모'Now I know you are. I've got an ear for certain things, the way you've got an ear for music. You play the trombone, don't you?'
모모
모모'Yeah.' He looked surprised.
모모
모모'I hear you blow well.'
모모
모모'Don't flatter me. I ain't no J.C. Higginbotham.'
모모
모모'And I ain't no Sherlock Holmes. But sooner or later you're going to tell me when you saw Tommy Hillman last. You're not going to sit on your raunchy ole bed and wait for the television to inform you that they found Tommy's body in a ditch.'
모모
모모'Did they?'
모모
모모'Not yet. It could happen tonight. When did you see him?'
모모
모모He drew a deep breath. 'Yesterday. He was okay.'
모모
모모'Did he come here?'
모모
모모'No sir. He never has. He stopped in at The Barroom Floor yesterday afternoon. He came in the back way and only stayed five minutes.'
모모
모모'What was he wearing?'
모모
모모'Slacks and a black sweater. He told me once his mother knitted that sweater for him.'
모모
모모'Did you talk to him yesterday afternoon?'
모모
모모'I played him a special riff and he came up and thanked me. That was all. I didn't know he was on the run. Shucks, he even had his girl friend with him.'
모모
모모'Stella?'
모모
모모'The other one. The older one.'
모모
모모'What's her name?'
모모
모모'He never told me. I only seen her once or twice before that. Tommy knew I wouldn't approve of him squiring her around. She's practically old enough to be his mother.'
모모
모모'Can you describe her?'
모모
모모'She's a bottle blonde, with a lot of hair, you know how they're wearing it now.'
모모
모모He swept his hand up from his wrinkled forehead. 'Blue eyes, with a lot of eye shadow. It's hard to tell what she looks like under all that makeup.'
모모
모모I got out my notebook and made some notes. 'What's her background?'
모모
모모'Show business, maybe. Like I say, I never talked to her. But she has the looks.'
모모
모모'I gather she's attractive.'
모모
모모'She appears to be to Tom. I guess she's his first. A lot of young boys start out with an older woman. But,' he added under his breath, 'he could do better than that.'
모모
모모'How old is she?'
모모
모모'Thirty, anyway. She didn't show me her birth certificate. She dresses younger-skirts up over her knees. She isn't a big girl, and maybe in some lights she can get away with the youth act.'
모모
모모'What was she wearing yesterday?'
모모
모모'A dark dress, blue satin or something like that, with sequins on it, a neckline down to here.'
모모
모모He touched his solar plexus. 'It grieved me to see Tom with his arm around her.'
모모
모모'How did she seem to feel about him?'
모모
모모'You're asking me more than I can answer. He's a good-looking boy, and she makes a show of affection. But I don't need X-ray eyes to know what is in her mind.'
모모
모모'Would she be a hustler?'
모모
모모'Could be.'
모모
모모'Did you ever see her with any other man?'
모모
모모'I never did. I only saw her once or twice with Tom.'
모모
모모'Once, or twice?'
모모
모모He ruminated. 'Twice before yesterday. The first time was two weeks ago yesterday. That was a Sunday, he brought her to our jam session that afternoon. The woman had been drinking and first she wanted to sing and then she wanted to dance. We don't allow dancing at these sessions, you have to pay cabaret tax. Somebody told her that and she got mad and towed the boy away.'
모모
모모'Who told her not to dance?'
모모
모모'I disremember. One of the cats sitting around, I guess, they object to dancing. The music we play Sundays isn't to dance to, anyway. It's more to the glory of God,' he said surprisingly.
모모
모모'What about the second time you saw her?'
모모
모모He hesitated, thinking. 'That was ten nights ago, on a Friday. They came in around midnight and had a sandwich. I drifted by their table, at the break, but Tom didn't introduce me or ask me to sit down. Which was all right with me. They seemed to have things to talk about.'
모모
모모'Did you overhear any part of their conversation?'
모모
모모'I did.'
모모
모모His face hardened. 'She needed money, she was telling him, money to get away from her husband.'
모모
모모'You're sure you heard that?'
모모
모모'Sure as I'm sitting here.'
모모
모모'What was Tom's attitude?'
모모
모모'Looked to me like he was fascinated.'
모모
모모'Had he been drinking?'
모모
모모'She was. He didn't drink. They don't serve drinks to minors at the Floor. No sir. She had him hyped on something worse than drink.'
모모
모모'Drugs?'
모모
모모'You know what I mean.'
모모
모모His hands molded a woman's figure in the air.
모모
모모'You used the word "hyped."
모모
모모'It was just a manner of speaking,' he said nervously, rubbing his upper arm through the shirt sleeve.
모모
모모'Are you on the needle?'
모모
모모'No sir. I'm on the TV,' he said with a sudden downward smile.
모모
모모'Show me your arms.'
모모
모모'I don't have to. You got no right.'
모모
모모'I want to test your veracity. Okay?'
모모
모모He unbuttoned his cuffs and pushed his sleeves up his thin yellow arms. The pitted scars in them were old and dry.
모모
모모'I got out of Lexington seven years ago,' he said, 'and I haven't fallen since, I thank the good Lord.'
모모
모모He touched his scars with a kind of reverence. They were like tiny extinct volcanoes in his flesh. He covered them up.
모모
모모'You're doing all right, Mr. Jackman. With your background, you'd probably know if Tom was on drugs.'
모모
모모I probably would. He wasn't. More than once I lectured him on the subject. Musicians have their temptations. But he took my lectures to heart.'
모모
모모He shifted his hand to the region of his heart. 'I ought to of lectured him on the subject of women.'
모모
모모'I never heard that it did much good. Did you ever see Tom and the blonde with anyone else?'
모모
모모'No.'
모모
모모'Did he introduce her to anyone?'
모모
모모'I doubt it. He was keeping her to himself. Showing her off, but keeping her to himself.'
모모
모모'You don't have any idea what her name is?'
모모
모모'No. I don't.'
모모
모모I got up and thanked him. 'I'm sorry if I gave you a rough time.'
모모
모모'I've had rougher.'
모모
모모7
모모
모모DACK'S AUTO COURT was on the edge of the city, in a rather rundown suburb named Ocean View. The twelve or fifteen cottages of the court lay on the flat top of a bluff, below the highway and above the sea. They were made of concrete block and painted an unnatural green. Three or four cars, none of them recent models, were parked on the muddy gravel.
모모
모모The rain had let up and fresh yellow light slanted in from a hole in the west, as if to provide a special revelation of the ugliness of Dack's Auto Court. Above the hutch marked 'Office', a single ragged palm tree leaned against the light. I parked beside it and went in.
모모
모모A hand-painted card taped to the counter instructed me to 'Ring for Proprietor.'
모모
모모I punched the handbell beside it. It didn't work.
모모
모모Leaning across the counter, I noticed on the shelf below it a telephone and a metal filing box divided into fifteen numbered sections. The registration card for number seven was dated three weeks before, and indicated that 'Mr. and Mrs. Robt. Brown' were paying sixteen dollars a week for that cottage. The spaces provided on the card for home address and license number were empty.
모모
모모The screen door creaked behind me. A big old man with a naked condor head came flapping into the office. He snatched the card from my fingers and looked at me with hot eyes. 'What do you think you're doing?'
모모
모모'I was only checking.'
모모
모모'Checking what?'
모모
모모'To see if some people I know are here. Bob Brown and his wife.'
모모
모모He held the card up to the light and read it, moving his lips laboriously around the easy words. 'They're here,' he said without joy. 'Leastways, they were this morning.'
모모
모모He gave me a doubtful look. My claim of acquaintanceship with the Browns had done nothing for my status. I tried to improve it. 'Do you have a cottage vacant?'
모모
모모'Ten of them. Take your pick.'
모모
모모'How much?'
모모
모모'Depends on if you rent by the day or the week. They're three-fifty a day, sixteen a week.'
모모
모모'I'd better check with the Browns first, see if they're planning to stay.'
모모
모모'I wouldn't know about that. They been here three weeks.'
모모
모모He had a flexible worried mouth in conflict with a stupid stubborn chin. He stroked his chin as if to educate it. 'I can let you have number eight for twelve a week single. That's right next door to the Browns' place.'
모모
모모'I'll check with them.'
모모
모모'I don't believe they're there. You can always try.'
모모
모모I went outside and down the dreary line of cottages. The door of number seven was locked. Nobody answered my repeated rapping.
모모
모모When I turned away, the old man was standing in front of number eight. He beckoned to me and opened the door with a flourish: 'Take a look. I can let you have it for ten if you really like it.'
모모
모모I stepped inside. The room was cold and cheerless. The inside walls were concrete block, and the same unnatural green as the outside. Through a crack in the drawn blind, yellow light slashed at the hollow bed, the threadbare carpet. I'd spent too many nights in places like it to want to spend another.
모모
모모'It's clean,' the old man said.
모모
모모'I'm sure it is, Mr. Dack.'
모모
모모'I cleaned it myself. But I'm not Dack, I'm Stanislaus. Dack sold out to me years ago. I just never got around to having the signs changed. What's the use? They'll be tearing everything down and putting up high-rise apartments pretty soon.'
모모
모모He smiled and stroked his bald skull as if it was a kind of golden egg. 'Well, you want the cottage?'
모모
모모'It really depends on Brown's plans.'
모모
모모'If I was you,' he said, 'I wouldn't let too much depend on him.'
모모
모모'How is that, Mr. Stanislaus?'
모모
모모'He's kind of a blowtop, ain't he? I mean, the way he treats that little blonde wife. I always say these things are between a man and his wife. But it rankles me,' he said. 'I got a deep respect for women.'
모모
모모'So have I. I've never liked the way he treated women.'
모모
모모'I'm glad to hear that. A man should treat his wife with love and friendship. I lost my own wife several years ago, and I know what I'm talking about. I tried to tell him that, he told me to mind my own business. I know he's a friend of yours-' 
모모
모모'He's not exactly a friend. Is he getting worse?'
모모
모모'Depends what you mean, worse. This very day he was slapping her around. I felt like kicking him out of my place. Only, how would that help her? And all she did was make a little phone call. He tries to keep her cooped up like she was in jail.'
모모
모모He paused, listening, as if the word jail had associations for him. 'How long have you known this Brown?'
모모
모모'Not so long,' I said vaguely. 'I ran into him in Los Angeles.'
모모
모모'In Hollywood?'
모모
모모'Yeah. In Hollywood.'
모모
모모'Is it true she was in the movies? She mentioned one day she used to be in the movies. He told her to shut up.'
모모
모모'Their marriage seems to be deteriorating.'
모모
모모'You can say that again.'
모모
모모He leaned toward me in the doorway. 'I bet you she's the one you're interested in. I see a lot of couples, one way and another, and I'm willing to bet you she's just about had her fill of him. If I was a young fellow like you, I'd be tempted to make her an offer.'
모모
모모He nudged me; the friction seemed to warm him. 'She's a red-hot little bundle.'
모모
모모'I'm not young enough.'
모모
모모'Sure you are.'
모모
모모He handled my arm, and chuckled. 'It's true she likes'em young. I been seeing her off and on with a teen-ager, even.'
모모
모모I produced the photograph of Tom that Elaine Hillman had given me. 'This one?'
모모
모모The old man lifted it to the daylight, at arm's length. 'Yeah. That's a mighty good picture of him. He's a good-looking boy.'
모모
모모He handed the photograph back to me, and fondled his chin. 'How do you come to have a picture of him?'
모모
모모I told him the truth, or part of it: 'He's a runaway from a boarding school. I'm a private detective representing the school.'
모모
모모The moist gleam of lechery faded out of Stanislaus's eyes. Something bleaker took its place, a fantasy of punishment perhaps. His whole face underwent a transformation, like quick-setting concrete.
모모
모모'You can't make me responsible for what the renters do.'
모모
모모'Nobody said I could.'
모모
모모He didn't seem to hear me. 'Let's see that picture again.'
모모
모모I showed it to him. He shook his head over it. 'I made a mistake. My eyes ain't what they used to be. I never seen him before.'
모모
모모'You made a positive identification.'
모모
모모'I take it back. You were talking to me under false pretenses, trying to suck me in and get something on me. Well, you got nothing on me. It's been tried before,' he said darkly. 'And you can march yourself off my property.'
모모
모모'Aren't you going to rent me the cottage?'
모모
모모He hesitated a moment, saying a silent goodbye to the ten dollars. 'No sir, I want no spies and peepers in my place.'
모모
모모'You may be harboring something worse.'
모모
모모I think he suspected it, and the suspicion was the source of his anger.
모모
모모'I'll take my chances. Now you git. If you're not off my property in one minute, I'm going to call the sheriff.' 
모모
모모That was the last thing I wanted. I'd already done enough to endanger the ransom payment and Tom's return. I got.
모모
모모8
모모
모모A BLUE SPORTS car stood in the drive behind the Hillman Cadillac. An athletic-looking young man who looked as if he belonged in the sports car came out of the house and confronted me on the front steps. He wore an Ivy League suit and had an alligator coat slung over his arm and hand, with something bulky and gun-shaped under it.
모모
모모'Point that thing away from me. I'm not armed.'
모모
모모'I w-want to know who you are.'
모모
모모He had a faint stammer.
모모
모모'Lew Archer. Who are you?'
모모
모모'I'm Dick Leandro.'
모모
모모He spoke the words almost questioningly, as if he didn't quite know what it meant to be Dick Leandro.
모모
모모'Lower that gun,' I reminded him. 'Try pointing it at your leg.'
모모
모모He dropped his arm. The alligator coat slid off it, onto the flagstone steps, and I saw that he was holding a heavy old revolver. He picked up the coat and looked at me in a rather confused way. He was a handsome boy in his early twenties, with brown eyes and dark curly hair. A certain little dancing light in his eyes told me that he was aware of being handsome.
모모
모모'Since you're here,' I said, 'I take it the money's here, too.'
모모
모모'Yes. I brought it out from the office several hours ago.'
모모
모모'Has Hillman been given instructions for delivering it?'
모모
모모He shook his head. 'We're still waiting.'
모모
모모I found Ralph and Elaine Hillman in the downstairs room where the telephone was. They were sitting close together as if for warmth, on a chesterfield near the front window. The waiting had aged them both.
모모
모모The evening light fell like gray paint across their faces. She was knitting something out of red wool. Her hands moved rapidly and precisely as if they had independent life.
모모
모모Hillman got to his feet. He had been holding a newspaper-wrapped parcel in his lap, and he laid it down on the chesterfield, gently, like a father handling an infant.
모모
모모'Hello, Archer,' he said in a monotone.' I moved toward him with some idea of comforting him. But the expression in his eyes, hurt and proud and lonely, discouraged me from touching him or saying anything very personal.
모모
모모'You've had a long hard day.'
모모
모모He nodded slowly, once. His wife let out a sound like a dry sob. 'Why haven't we heard anything from that man?'
모모
모모'It's hard to say. He seems to be putting on the screws deliberately.'
모모
모모She pushed her knitting to one side, and it fell on the floor unnoticed. Her faded pretty face wrinkled up as if she could feel the physical pressure of torture instruments. 'He's keeping us in hell, in absolute hell. But why?'
모모
모모'He's probably waiting for dark,' I said. 'I'm sure you'll be hearing from him soon. Twenty-five thousand dollars is a powerful attraction.'
모모
모모'He's welcome to the money, five times over. Why doesn't he simply take it and give us back our boy?'
모모
모모Her hand flung itself out, rattling the newspaper parcel beside her.
모모
모모'Don't fret yourself, Ellie.'
모모
모모Hillman leaned over her and touched her pale gold hair. 'There's no use asking questions that can't be answered. Remember, this will pass.'
모모
모모His words of comfort sounded hollow and forced.
모모
모모'So will I,' she said wryly and bitterly, 'if this keeps up much longer.'
모모
모모She smoothed her face with both hands and stayed with her hands in a prayerful position at her chin. She was trembling. I was afraid she might snap like a violin string. I said to Hillman: 'May I speak to you in private? I've uncovered some facts you should know.'
모모
모모'You can tell me in front of Elaine, and Dick for that matter.'
모모
모모I noticed that Leandro was standing just inside the door.
모모
모모'I prefer not to.'
모모
모모'You're not calling the shots, however.'
모모
모모It was a curious echo of the man on the telephone. 'Let's have your facts.'
모모
모모I let him have them: 'Your son has been seen consorting with a married woman named Brown. She's a blonde, show-business type, a good deal older than he is, and she seems to have been after him for money. The chances are better than even that Mrs. Brown and her husband are involved in this extortion bid. They seem to be on their uppers-' Elaine raised her open hands in front of her face, as if too many words were confusing her. 'What do you mean, consorting?'
모모
모모'He's been hanging around with the woman, publicly and privately. They were seen together yesterday afternoon.'
모모
모모'Where?' Hillman said.
모모
모모'At The Barroom Floor.'
모모
모모'Who says so?'
모모
모모'One of their employees. He's seen them before, and he referred to Mrs. Brown as 'Tom's girl friend, the older one.'
모모
모모I've had corroborating evidence from the man who owns the court where the Browns are living. Tom has been hanging around there, too.'
모모
모모'How old is this woman?'
모모
모모'Thirty or more. She's quite an attractive dish, apparently.'
모모
모모Elaine Hillman lifted her eyes. There seemed to be real horror in them. 'Are you implying that Tom has been having an affair with her?'
모모
모모'I'm simply reporting facts.'
모모
모모'I don't believe your facts, not any of them.'
모모
모모'Do you think I'm lying to you?'
모모
모모'Maybe not deliberately. But there must be some ghastly mistake.'
모모
모모'I agree,' Dick Leandro said from the doorway. 'Tom has always been a very clean-living boy.'
모모
모모Hillman was silent. Perhaps he knew something about his son that the others didn't. He sat down beside his wife and hugged the paper parcel defensively.
모모
모모'His virtue isn't the main thing right now,' I said. 'The question is what kind of people he's mixed up with and what they're doing to him. Or possibly what they're doing to you with his cooperation.'
모모
모모'What is that supposed to mean?' Hillman said.
모모
모모'We have to reconsider the possibility that Tom is in on the extortion deal. He was with Mrs. Brown yesterday. The man on the telephone, who may be Brown, said Tom came to them voluntarily.'
모모
모모Elaine Hillman peered up into my face as if she was trying to grasp such a possibility. It seemed to be too much for her to accept. She closed her eyes and shook her head so hard that her hair fell untidily over her forehead. Pushing it back with spread fingers, she said in a small voice that sent chills through me: 'You're lying, I know my son, he's an innocent victim. You're trying to do something terrible, coming to us in our affliction with such a filthy rotten smear.'
모모
모모Her husband tried to quiet her against his shoulder. 'Hush now, Elaine. Mr. Archer is only trying to help.'
모모
모모She pushed him away from her. 'We don't want that kind of help. He has no right. Tom is an innocent victim, and God knows what is happening to him.'
모모
모모Her hand was still at her head, with her pale hair sprouting up between her fingers. 'I can't take any more of this, Ralph-this dreadful man with his dreadful stories.'
모모
모모'I'm sorry, Mrs. Hillman. I didn't want you to hear them.'
모모
모모'I know. You wanted to malign my son without anyone to defend him.'
모모
모모'That's nonsense, Ellie,' Hillman said. 'I think you better come upstairs and let me give you a sedative.'
모모
모모He helped her to her feet and walked her out past me, looking at me sorrowfully across her rumpled head. She moved like an invalid leaning on his strength.
모모
모모Dick Leandro drifted into the room after they had left it, and sat on the chesterfield to keep the money company. He said in a slightly nagging way: 'You hit Elaine pretty hard with all that stuff: She's a sensitive woman, very puritanical about sex and such. And incidentally she's crazy about Tommy. She won't listen to a word against him.'
모모
모모'Are there words against him?'
모모
모모'Not that I know about. But he has been getting into trouble lately. You know, with the car wreck and all. And now you t-tell me he's been dipping into the fleshpots.'
모모
모모'I didn't say that.'
모모
모모'Yeah, but I got the message. Where does the g-girl live, anyway? Somebody ought to go and question her.'
모모
모모'You're full of ideas.'
모모
모모He had a tin ear for tone. 'Well, how about it? I'm game.'
모모
모모'You're doing more good here, guarding the money. How did Hillman happen to pick you to bring the money, by the way? Are you an old family friend?'
모모
모모'I guess you could say that. I've been crewing for Mr. Hillman since I was yay-high.'
모모
모모He held out his hand at knee level. 'Mr. Hillman is a terrific guy. Did you know he made Captain in the Navy? But he won't let anybody call him Captain except when we're at sea.
모모
모모'And generous,' the young man said. 'As a matter of fact, he helped me through college and got me a job at his broker's. I owe him a lot. He's treated me like a father.'
모모
모모He spoke with some emotion, real but intended, like an actor's. 'I'm an orphan, you might say. My family broke up when I was yay-high, and my father left town. He used to work for Mr. Hillman at the plant.'
모모
모모'Do you know Tom Hillman well?'
모모
모모'Sure. He's a pretty good kid. But a little too much of an egghead in my book. Which keeps him from being popular. No wonder he has his troubles.'
모모
모모Leandro tapped his temple with his knuckles. 'Is it true that Mr. Hillman put him in the booby-I mean, in a sanatorium?'
모모
모모'Ask him yourself.'
모모
모모The young man bored me. I went into the alcove and made myself a drink. Night was closing in. The garish bullfight posters on the walls had faded into darkness like long-forgotten corridor. There were shadows huddling with shadows behind the bar. I raised my glass to them in a gesture I didn't quite understand, except that there was relief in darkness and silence and whisky.
모모
모모I could hear Hillman's footsteps dragging down the stairs. The telephone on the bar went off like an alarm. Hillman's descending footsteps became louder. He came trotting into the room as the telephone rang a second time. He elbowed me out of his way.
모모
모모I started for the extension phone in the pantry. He called after me: 'No! I'll handle this myself.'
모모
모모There was command in his voice. I stood and watched him pick up the receiver, hold it to his head like a black scorpion, and listen to what it said.
모모
모모'Yes, this is Mr. Hillman. Just a minute.'
모모
모모He brought a business envelope and a ballpoint pen out of his inside pocket, turned on an overhead light, and got ready to write on the bar. 'Go ahead.'
모모
모모For about half a minute he listened and wrote. Then he said: 'I think so. Aren't there steps going down to the beach?'
모모
모모He listened and wrote. 'Where shall I walk to?'
모모
모모He turned the envelope over and wrote some more. 'Yes,' he said. 'I park two blocks away, at Seneca, and approach the steps on foot. I put the money under the right side of the top step. Then I go down to the beach for half an hour. Is that all?'
모모
모모There was a little more. He listened to it. Finally he said: 'Yes. But the deal is very much on as far as I'm concerned. I'll be there at nine sharp.'
모모
모모There was a pathetic note in his voice, the note of a salesman trying to nail down an appointment with a refractory client.
모모
모모'Wait,' he said, and groaned into the dead receiver.
모모
모모Dick Leandro, moving like a cat, was in the alcove ahead of me. 'What is it, Mr. Hillman? What's the trouble?'
모모
모모'I wanted to ask about Tom. He didn't give me a chance.'
모모
모모He lifted his face to the plaster ceiling. 'I don't know if he's alive or dead.'
모모
모모'They wouldn't kill him, would they?' the young man said. He sounded as though he'd had a first frightening hint of his own mortality.
모모
모모'I don't know, Dick. I don't know.'
모모
모모Hillman's head rolled from one side to the other.
모모
모모The young man put his arm around his shoulder. 'Take it easy now, Skipper. We'll get him back.'
모모
모모Hillman poured himself a heavy slug of bourbon and tossed it down. It brought a little color into his face. I said: 'Same man?'
모모
모모'Yes.'
모모
모모'And he told you where to make the money-drop.'
모모
모모'Yes.'
모모
모모'Do you want company?'
모모
모모'I have to go there alone. He said he'd be watching.'
모모
모모'Where are you to go?'
모모
모모Hillman looked at each of our faces in turn, lingeringly, as if he was saying goodbye. 'I'll keep that to myself. I don't want anything to wreck the arrangements.'
모모
모모'Somebody should know about them, though, in case anything does go wrong. You're taking a chance.'
모모
모모'I'd rather take a chance with my own life than my son's.'
모모
모모He said it as if he meant it, and the words seemed to renew his courage. He glanced at his wristwatch. 'It's twenty-five to nine. It will take me up to twenty minutes to get there. He didn't give me much leeway.'
모모
모모'Can you drive okay?' Leandro said.
모모
모모'Yes. I'm all right. I'll just go up and tell Ellie that I'm leaving. You stay in the house with her, won't you, Dick?'
모모
모모'I'll be glad to.'
모모
모모Hillman went upstairs, still clutching his scribbled-over envelope. I said to Leandro: 'Where is Seneca Street?'
모모
모모'Seneca Road. In Ocean View.'
모모
모모'Are there steps going down to the beach anywhere near there?'
모모
모모'Yeah, but you're not supposed to go there. You heard Mr. Hillman.'
모모
모모'I heard him.'
모모
모모Hillman came down and took the parcel of money out of Leandro's hands. He thanked the young man, and his voice was deep and gentle as well as melancholy.
모모
모모We stood on the flagstone steps and watched him drive away into the darkness under the trees. In the hole in the dark west a little light still persisted, like the last light there was ever going to be.
모모
모모11
모모
모모I WENT THROUGH the house to the kitchen and asked Mrs. Perez to make me a plain cheese sandwich. She grumbled, but she made it. I ate it leaning against the refrigerator. Mrs. Perez wouldn't talk about the trouble in the family. She seemed to have a superstitious feeling that trouble was only amplified by words. When I tried to question her about Tom's habits, she gradually lost her ability to understand my English.
모모
모모Dick Leandro had gone upstairs to sit with Elaine. He seemed more at home than Tom appeared to have been with his own family. I went out through the reception hall. It was nine o'clock, and I couldn't wait any longer.
모모
모모Driving along the highway to Ocean View, I argued jesuitically with myself that I had stayed clear of the money-drop, I wasn't double-dealing with Hillman, who wasn't my client in any case, and besides I had no proof that Mrs. Brown and her husband were connected with the extortion attempt.
모모
모모It was deep night over the sea, moonless and starless. I left my car at a view-point near Dack's Auto Court. The sea was a hollow presence with a voice. I hiked down the access road to the court, not using the flashlight that I carried with me.
모모
모모The office was lighted and had a neon 'Vacancy' sign above the door. Avoiding the spill of light from it, I went straight to cottage number seven. It was dark. I knocked, and got no answer. I let myself in with the key I had and closed the self-locking door behind me.
모모
모모Mrs. Brown was waiting; I stumbled over her foot and almost fell on top of her before I switched on my flashlight. She lay in her winking sequined gown under the jittery beam. Blood was tangled like tar in her bright hair. Her face was mottled with bruises, and misshapen. She looked as though she had been beaten to death.
모모
모모I touched her hand. She was cold. I turned the light away from her lopsided grin.
모모
모모The beam jumped around the green walls, the newspaper littered floor. It found a large strapped canvas suitcase standing at the foot of the bed with two paper bags beside it. One of the bags contained a bottle of cheap wine, the other sandwiches that were drying out.
모모
모모I unstrapped the suitcase and opened it. An odor rose from its contents like sour regret. Men's and women's things were bundled indiscriminately together, dirty shirts and soiled slips, a rusting safety razor and a dabbled jar of cold cream and a bottle of mascara, a couple of dresses and some lingerie, a man's worn blue suit with a chain-store label and nothing in the pockets but tobacco powder and, tucked far down in the outer breast pocket, a creased yellow business card poorly printed on cheap paper: 
모모
모모HAROLD 'HAR' HARLEY 
모모Application Photos Our Specialty 
모모
모모I found the woman's imitation snakeskin purse on a chair by the side window. It contained a jumble of cosmetics and some frayed blue chip stamps. No wallet, no identification, no money except for a single silver dollar in the bottom of the bag. There were also a pack of cards, slick with the oil of human hands, and a dice which came up six all three times I rolled it.
모모
모모I heard a car approaching, and headlights swept the window on the far side. I switched off my flashlight. The wheels of the car crunched in the gravel and came to a halt directly in front of the cottage. Someone got out of the car and turned the cottage doorknob. When the door refused to open, a man's voice said: 'Let me in.'
모모
모모It was the slightly wheezing, whining voice I'd heard that afternoon on Hillman's phone. I moved toward the door with the dark flashlight raised in my hand. The man outside rattled the knob.
모모
모모'I know you're in there, I saw the light. This is no time to carry a grudge, hon.'
모모
모모The woman lay in her deep waiting silence. I stepped around her and stood against the wall beside the door. I shifted the flash to my left hand and fumbled for the spring lock with my right.
모모
모모'I hear you, damn you. You want another taste of what you had today?'
모모
모모He waited, and then said: 'If you won't open the door, I'll shoot the lock out.'
모모
모모I heard the click of a hammer. I stayed where I was beside the door, holding the flashlight like a club. But he didn't fire.
모모
모모'On the other hand,' he said, 'there's nothing in there I need, including you. You can stay here on your can if you want to. Make up your mind right now.'
모모
모모He waited. He couldn't outwait her.
모모
모모'This is your last chance. I'll count to three. If you don't open up, I'm traveling alone.'
모모
모모He counted, one, two, three, but it would take bigger magic to reach her. 'Good riddance to bad rubbish,' he said.
모모
모모His footsteps moved away on the stones. The car door creaked. I couldn't let him go.
모모
모모I snapped back the lock and opened the door and rushed him. His shadowy hatted figure was halfway into his car, with one foot on the ground. He whirled. The gun was still in his hand. It gave out a hot little flame. I could feel it sear me.
모모
모모I staggered across the gravel and got hold of his twisting body. He hammered my hands loose with the butt of his gun. I had blood in my eyes, and I couldn't avoid the gun butt when it smashed into my skull. A kind of chandelier lit up in my head and then crashed down into darkness.
모모
모모Next thing I was a VIP traveling with a police guard in the back of a chauffeured car. The turban I could feel on my head suggested to the joggled brain under it that I was a rajah or a maharajah. We turned into a driveway under a red light, which excited me. Perhaps I was being taken to see one of my various concubines.
모모
모모I raised the question with the uniformed men sitting on either side of me. Gently but firmly, they helped me out of the patrol car and walked me through swinging doors, which a man in white held open, into a glaring place that smelled of disinfectants.
모모
모모They persuaded me to sit down on a padded table and then to lie down. My heart hurt. I felt it with my hands. It had a towel around it, sticky with blood.
모모
모모A large young face with a moustache leaned over me upside down. Large hairy hands removed the towel and did some probing and scouring in my scalp. It stung.
모모
모모'You're a lucky man. It parted your hair for you, kind of permanently.'
모모
모모'How bad is it, Doctor?'
모모
모모'The bullet wound isn't serious, just a crease. As I said, you're lucky. This other lesion is going to take longer to heal. What did you get hit with?'
모모
모모'Gun butt. I think.'
모모
모모'More fun and games,' he said.
모모
모모'Did they catch him?'
모모
모모'You'll have to ask them. They haven't told me a thing.'
모모
모모He clipped parts of my head and put some clamps in it and gave me a drink of water and an aspirin. Then he left me lying alone in the white-partitioned cubicle. My two guards moved rapidly into the vacuum.
모모
모모They were sheriff's men, wearing peaked hats and tan uniforms. They were young and hearty, with fine animal bodies and rather animal, not so fine, faces. Good earnest boys, but a little dull. They said they wanted to help me.
모모
모모'Why did you kill her?' the dark one said.
모모
모모'I didn't. She'd been dead for some time when I found her.'
모모
모모'That doesn't let you out. Mr. Stanislaus said you were there earlier in the day.'
모모
모모'He was with me all the time.'
모모
모모'That's what you say,' the fair one said.
모모
모모This repartee went on for some time, like a recording of an old vaudeville act, which some collector had unwisely preserved. I tried to question them. They wouldn't tell me anything. My head was feeling worse, but oddly enough I began to think better with it. I even managed to get up on my elbows and look at them on the level.
모모
모모'I'm a licensed private detective from Los Angeles.'
모모
모모'We know that,' the dark one said.
모모
모모I felt for my wallet. It was missing. 'Give me my wallet.'
모모
모모'You'll get it back all in good time. Nobody's going to steal it.'
모모
모모'I want to talk to the sheriff.' 
모모
모모'He's in bed asleep.'
모모
모모'Is there a captain or lieutenant on duty?'
모모
모모'The lieutenant is busy at the scene of the crime. You can talk to him in the morning. The doctor says you stay here overnight. Concussion. What did the woman hit you with, anyway?'
모모
모모'Her husband hit me, with a gun butt.'
모모
모모'I hardly blame him,' the fair one said emotionally, 'after what you did to his wife.'
모모
모모'Were you shacked up with her?' the dark one said.
모모
모모I looked from one healthy smooth face to the other. They didn't look sadistic, or sound corrupt, and I wasn't afraid for myself. Sooner or later the mess would be straightened out. But I was afraid.
모모
모모'Listen,' I said, 'you're wasting time on me. I had legitimate business at the court. I was investigating-'The fear came up in my throat and choked off the rest of the sentence. It was fear for the boy.
모모
모모'Investigating what?' the dark one said.
모모
모모'Law enforcement in this country. It stinks.'
모모
모모I wasn't feeling too articulate.
모모
모모'We'll law-enforcement you,' the dark one said. He was broad, with muscular shoulders. He moved them around in the air a little bit and pretended to catch a fly just in front of my face.
모모
모모'Lay off, muscle,' I said.
모모
모모The large, moustached face of the doctor appeared in the entrance to the cubicle. 'Everything okay in here?'
모모
모모I said above the deputies' smiling assurances: 'I want to make a phone call.'
모모
모모The doctor looked doubtfully from me to the officers. 'I don't know about that.'
모모
모모'I'm a private detective investigating a crime. I'm not free to talk about it without the permission of my principal. I want to call him.'
모모
모모'There's no facilities for that,' the dark deputy said.
모모
모모'How about it, Doctor? You're in charge here, and I have a legal right to make a phone call.'
모모
모모He was a very young man behind his moustache. 'I don't know. There's a telephone booth down the hall. Do you think you can make it?'
모모
모모'I never felt better in my life.'
모모
모모But when I swung my legs down, the floor seemed distant and undulant. The deputies had to help me to the booth and prop me up on the stool inside of it. I pulled the folding door shut. Their faces floated outside the wired glass like bulbous fishes, a dark one and a fair one, nosing around a bathyscaph on the deep ocean floor.
모모
모모Technically Dr Sponti was my principal, but it was Ralph Hillman's number I asked Information for. I had a dime in my pocket, fortunately, and Hillman was there. He answered the phone himself on the first ring: 'Yes?'
모모
모모'This is Archer.' He groaned.
모모
모모'Have you heard anything from Tom?' I said.
모모
모모'No. I followed instructions to the letter, and when I came up from the beach the money was gone. He's double-crossed me,' he said bitterly.
모모
모모'Did you see him?'
모모
모모'No. I made no attempt to.'
모모
모모'I did.'
모모
모모I told Hillman what had happened, to me and to Mrs. Brown.
모모
모모His voice came thin and bleak over the wire. 'And you think these are the same people?'
모모
모모'I think Brown's your man. Brown is probably an alias. Does the name Harold Harley mean anything to you?'
모모
모모'What was that again?'
모모
모모'Harold or "Har" Harley. He's a photographer.'
모모
모모'I never heard of him.'
모모
모모I wasn't surprised. Harley's yellow card was the kind that businessmen distributed by the hundred, and had no necessary connection with Brown.
모모
모모'Is that all you wanted?' Hillman said. 'I'm trying to keep this line open.'
모모
모모'I haven't got to the main thing. The police are on my back. I can't explain what I was doing at the auto court without dragging in the extortion bit, and your son.'
모모
모모'Can't you give them a story?'
모모
모모'It wouldn't be wise. This is a capital case, a double one.'
모모
모모'Are you trying to tell me that Tom is dead?'
모모
모모'I meant that kidnapping is a capital crime. But you are dealing with a killer. I think at this point you should level with the police, and get their help. Sooner or later I'm going to have to level with them.'
모모
모모'I forbid-' He changed his tone, and started the sentence over: 'I beg of you, please hold off: Give him until morning to come home. He's my only son.'
모모
모모'All right. Till morning. We can't bottle it up any longer than that, and we shouldn't.'
모모
모모I hung up and stepped out into the corridor. Instead of taking me back to the emergency ward, my escort took me up in an elevator to a special room with heavy screens on the windows. They let me lie down on the bed, and took turns questioning me. It would be tedious to recount the dialogue. It was tedious at the time, and I didn't listen to all of it.
모모
모모Some time around midnight a sheriffs lieutenant named Bastian came into the room and ordered the deputies out into the hall. He was a tall man, with iron-gray hair clipped short. The vertical grooves in his cheeks looked like the scars inflicted by a personal discipline harsher than saber cuts.
모모
모모He stood over me frowning. 'Dr Murphy says you're feeling critical of law enforcement in this county.'
모모
모모'I've had reason.'
모모
모모'It isn't easy recruiting men at the salaries the supervisors are willing to pay. We can hardly compete with the wages for unskilled labor. And this is a tough job.'
모모
모모'It has its little extra compensations.'
모모
모모'What does that mean?'
모모
모모'I seem to be missing my wallet.'
모모
모모Bastian's face went grim. He marched out into the hall, made some remarks in a voice that buzzed like a hornet, and came back carrying my wallet. I counted the money in it, rather obtrusively.
모모
모모'It was used to check you out,' Bastian said. 'LA County gives you a good rating, and I'm sorry if you weren't treated right.'
모모
모모'Think nothing of it. I'm used to being pushed around by unskilled labor.'
모모
모모'You heard me apologize,' he said, in a tone that closed the subject.
모모
모모Bastian asked me a number of questions about Mrs. Brown and the reason for my interest in her. I told him I'd have to check with my client in the morning, before I could open up. Then he wanted everything I could give him about Brown's appearance and car.
모모
모모The moments before and after the shot were vague in my memory. I dredged up what I could. Brown was a man of better than medium size, physically powerful, not young, not old. He was wearing a dark gray or blue jacket and a wide-brimmed grayish hat which shadowed his eyes. The lower part of his face was heavy jawed. His voice was rough, with a slight wheeze in it. The car was a dirty white or tan two-door sedan, probably a Ford, about eight years old.
모모
모모I learned two facts from Lieutenant Bastian: the car had an Idaho license, according to other tenants of the court, and Stanislaus was in trouble for keeping no record of the license number. I think Bastian gave me these facts in the hope of loosening my tongue. But he finally agreed to wait till morning.
모모
모모They shifted me to another room on the same floor, unguarded. I spent a good part of the night, waking and sleeping, watching a turning wheel of faces. The faces were interspersed from time to time with brilliant visions of Dack's Auto Court. Its green ugliness was held in the selective sunset glare, as if it was under a judgment, and so was I.
모모
모모10
모모
모모MORNING WAS WELCOME, in spite of the pain in my head. I couldn't remember eating anything but Mrs. Perez's cheese sandwich since the previous morning. The tepid coffee and over-scrambled eggs tasted like nectar and ambrosia.
모모
모모I was finishing breakfast when Dr Sponti arrived, breathing rapidly and audibly. His plump face bore the marks of a bad night. He had sleepless bruises around the eyes, and a gash in his upper lip where his razor had slipped. The chilly hand he offered me reminded me of the dead woman's, and I dropped it.
모모
모모'I'm surprised you knew I was here.'
모모
모모'I found out in a rather circuitous way. A Lieutenant Bastian phoned me in the middle of the night. Evidently he saw the check I gave you yesterday morning. He asked me a great many questions.'
모모
모모'About me?'
모모
모모'About the whole situation involving you and Tom Hillman.'
모모
모모'You told him about Tom Hillman?'
모모
모모'I had no choice, really.'
모모
모모He picked at the fresh scab on his lip. 'A woman has been murdered in Ocean View. I was honor bound to provide the authorities with all the information I could. After all-' 
모모
모모'Does this include the business of the ransom money?'
모모
모모'Naturally it does. Lieutenant Bastian considered it highly important. He thanked me effusively, and promised that the name of the school would be kept out of the papers.'
모모
모모'Which is the main thing.'
모모
모모'It is to me,' Sponti said. 'I'm in the school business.'
모모
모모It was frustrating to have held out for nothing, and to have no secrets to trade with Bastian. But it was relieving, too, that the thing was out in the open. Hillman's imposition of silence had made it hard for me to do my job. I said: 'Have you had any repercussions from Ralph Hillman?'
모모
모모'He phoned early this morning. The boy is still on the missing list.'
모모
모모Sponti's voice was lugubrious, and his eyes rolled heavily toward me. 'The parents are naturally quite frantic by this time. Mr. Hillman said things I'm sure he'll regret later.'
모모
모모'Is he still blaming you for the kidnapping?'
모모
모모'Yes, and he blames me for bringing you into the case. He seems to feel you broke faith with him, shall we say.'
모모
모모'By going to the auto court and getting myself shot?'
모모
모모'You frightened off the kidnappers, in his opinion, and prevented them from returning his son to him. I'm very much afraid he wants nothing more to do with you, Mr. Archer.'
모모
모모'And neither do you?'
모모
모모Dr Sponti pursed his lips and brought his ten fingers together in the air. They made a Norman arch and then a Gothic one. 'I'm sure you understand the pressures I'm under. I'm virtually obliged to do as Mr. Hillman wishes in his extremity.'
모모
모모'Sure.'
모모
모모'And I'm not going to ask you to refund any part of your check. The entire two hundred and fifty dollars is yours, even though you've been in my employ'-he looked at his watch-'considerably less than twenty-four hours. The unearned surplus will take care of your medical expenses, I'm sure.'
모모
모모He was backing toward the door. 'Well, I have to run.'
모모
모모'Go to hell,' I said as he went out.
모모
모모He poked his head in again: 'You may regret saying that. I'm tempted to stop payment on that check after all.'
모모
모모I made an obscene suggestion as to the disposition of the check. Dr Sponti turned as blue as a Santa Clara plum and went away. I lay and enjoyed my anger for a while. It went so nicely with the reciprocating ache in my head. And it helped to cover over the fact that I had let myself in for this. I shouldn't have gone the second time to Dack's Auto Court, at least not when I did.
모모
모모A nurse's aide came in and took away my tray. Later a doctor palpated my skull, looked into my eyes with a tiny light, and told me I probably had a slight concussion but so had a lot of other people walking around. I borrowed a safety razor from an orderly, shaved and dressed, and went down to the cashier's window and paid my bill with Sponti's check.
모모
모모I got over two hundred dollars change. Riding downtown in a taxi, I decided I could afford to spend another day on the case, whether Dr Sponti liked it or not. I told the driver to let me off at the Telephone Company.
모모
모모'You said the courthouse.'
모모
모모'The telephone company. We've had a change of plan.'
모모
모모'You should have said so in the first place.'
모모
모모'Forgive my failure of leadership.'
모모
모모I was feeling bitter and bright. It had to do with the weather, which had turned sunny, but more to do with my decision to spend my own time on a boy I'd never seen. I didn't tip the driver.
모모
모모One end of the main public room in the telephone building was lined with long-distance booths and shelves of out-of-town directories. Only the main cities in Idaho, like Boise and Pocatello and Idaho Falls, were represented. I looked through their directories, for a photographer named Harold Harley. He wasn't listed. Robert Brown was, by the legion, but the name was almost certainly an alias.
모모
모모I installed myself in one of the booths and placed a long-distance call to Arnie Walters, a Reno detective who often worked with me. I had no Idaho contact, and Reno was on the fastest route to Idaho. Reno itself had a powerful attraction for thieves with sudden money.
모모
모모'Walters Agency,' Arnie said.
모모
모모'This is Lew.'
모모
모모I told him where I was calling from, and why.
모모
모모'You come up with some dillies. Murder and kidnapping, eh?'
모모
모모'The kidnapping may be a phony. Tom Hillman, the supposed victim, has been palling around with the murdered woman for a couple of weeks.'
모모
모모'How old did you say he was?'
모모
모모'Seventeen. He's big for his age.'
모모
모모I described Tom Hillman in detail. 'He may be traveling with Brown either voluntarily or involuntarily.'
모모
모모'Or not traveling at all?' Arnie said.
모모
모모'Or not traveling at all.'
모모
모모'You know this boy?'
모모
모모'No.'
모모
모모'I thought maybe you knew him. Okay. Where does this photographer Harold Harley come in?'
모모
모모'Harley may be Brown himself, or he may know Brown. His card is the only real lead I have so far. That and the Idaho license. I want you to do two things. Check Idaho and adjoining states for Harley. You have the business directories, don't you?'
모모
모모'Yeah, I'll get Phyllis on them.'
모모
모모She was his wife and partner.
모모
모모'The other thing, I want you to look out for Brown and the boy, you and your informers in Tahoe and Vegas.'
모모
모모'What makes you think they're headed in this direction?'
모모
모모'It's a hunch. The woman had a silver dollar and a loaded dice in her purse.'
모모
모모'And no identification?'
모모
모모'Whoever did her in got rid of everything she had in that line. But we'll identify her. We have her.'
모모
모모'Let me know when you do.'
모모
모모I walked across down to the courthouse, under a sky that yesterday's rain had washed clean. I asked the deputy on duty in the sheriff's department where to find Lieutenant Bastian. He directed me to the identification laboratory on the second floor.
모모
모모It was more office than laboratory, a spacious room with pigeons murmuring on the window ledges. The walls were crowded with filing cabinets and hung with maps of the city and county and state. A large adjacent closet was fitted out as a darkroom, with drying racks and a long metal sink.
모모
모모Bastian got up smiling. His smile wasn't greatly different from last night's frown. He laid down a rectangular magnifying glass on top of the photograph he had been studying. Leaning across the desk to take his outstretched hand, I could see that it was a picture of Mrs. Brown in death.
모모
모모'What killed her, Lieutenant?' I said when we were seated.
모모
모모'This.'
모모
모모He held up his right hand and clenched it. His face clenched with it. 'The human hand.'
모모
모모'Robert Brown's?'
모모
모모'It looks like it. He gave her a beating early yesterday afternoon, according to Stanislaus. The deputy coroner says she's been dead that long.'
모모
모모'Stanislaus told me they quarreled over a telephone call she made.'
모모
모모'That's right. We haven't been able to trace the call, which means it was probably local. She used the phone in Stanislaus's office, but he claims to know nothing more about it.'
모모
모모'How does he know Brown gave her a beating?'
모모
모모I said.
모모
모모'He says a neighbor woman told him. That checks out.'
모모
모모Bastian wiped his left hand across his tense angry face, without really changing his expression. 'It's terrible the way some people live, that a woman could be killed within a neighbor's hearing and nobody knows or cares.'
모모
모모'Not even Brown,' I said. 'He thought she was alive at nine-thirty last night. He talked to her through the door, trying to get her to open up. Or he may have been trying to con himself into thinking he hadn't killed her after all. I don't think he's too stable.'
모모
모모Bastian looked up sharply. 'Were you in the cottage when Brown was talking through the door?'
모모
모모'I was. Incidentally, I recognized his voice. He's the same man who extorted twenty-five thousand dollars from Ralph Hillman last night. I listened in on a phone call he made to Hillman yesterday.'
모모
모모Bastian's right fist was still clenched. He used it to strike the desk top, savagely. The pigeons on the window ledge flew away.
모모
모모'It's too damn bad,' he said, 'you didn't bring us in on this yesterday. You might have saved a life, not to mention twenty-five thousand dollars.'
모모
모모'Tell that to Hillman.'
모모
모모'I intend to. This morning. Right now I'm telling you.'
모모
모모'The decision wasn't mine. I tried to change it. Anyway, I entered the case after the woman was killed.'
모모
모모'That's a good place to begin,' Bastian said after a pause. 'Go on from there. I want the full record.'
모모
모모He reached down beside his desk and turned on a recorder. For an hour or more the tape slithered quietly from wheel to wheel as I talked into it. I was client-less and free and I didn't suppress anything. Not even the possibility that Tom Hillman had cooperated with Brown in extorting money from his father.
모모
모모'I'd almost like to think that that was true,' Bastian said. 'It would mean that the kid is still alive, anyway. But it isn't likely.'
모모
모모'Which isn't likely?'
모모
모모'Both things. I doubt that he hoaxed his old man, and I doubt that he's still alive. It looks as if the woman was used as a decoy to get him in position for the kill. We'll probably find his body in the ocean week after next.'
모모
모모His words had the weight of experience behind them. Kidnap victims were poor actuarial risks. But I said: 'I'm working on the assumption that he's alive.'
모모
모모Bastian raised his eyebrows. 'I thought Dr Sponti took you off the case.'
모모
모모'I still have some of his money.'
모모
모모Bastian gave me a long cool appraising look. 'LA was right. You're not the usual peeper.'
모모
모모'I hope not.'
모모
모모'If you're staying with it, you can do something for me, as well as for yourself. Help me to get this woman identified.'
모모
모모He slid the picture of Mrs. Brown out from under the magnifying glass. 'This postmortem photo is too rough to circularize. But you could show it around in the right circles. I'm having a police artist make a composite portrait, but that takes time.'
모모
모모'What about fingerprints?'
모모
모모'We're trying that, too, but a lot of women have never been fingerprinted. Meantime, will you try and get an identification? You're a Hollywood man, and the woman claimed that she was in pictures at one time.'
모모
모모'That doesn't mean a thing.'
모모
모모'It might.'
모모
모모'But I was planning to try and pick up Brown's trail in Nevada. If the boy's alive, Brown knows where to find him.'
모모
모모'The Nevada police already have our APB on Brown. And you have a private operative on the spot. Frankly, I'd appreciate it if you'll take this picture to Hollywood with you. I don't have a man I can spare. By the way, I had your car brought into the county garage.'
모모
모모Cooperation breeds cooperation. Besides, the woman's identity was important, if only because the killer had tried to hide it. I accepted the picture, along with several others taken from various angles, and put them in the same pocket as my picture of Tom.
모모
모모'You can reverse any telephone charges,' Bastian said in farewell.
모모
모모Halfway down the stairs I ran into Ralph Hillman. At first glance he looked fresher than he had the previous evening. But it was an illusory freshness. The color in his cheeks was hectic, and the sparkle in his eyes was the glint of desperation. He sort of reared back when he saw me, like a spooked horse.
모모
모모'Can you give me a minute, Mr. Hillman?'
모모
모모'Sorry. I have an appointment.'
모모
모모'The lieutenant can wait. I want to say this. I admit I made a mistake last night. But you made a mistake in getting Sponti to drop me.'
모모
모모He looked at me down his patrician nose. 'You'd naturally think so. It's costing you money.'
모모
모모'Look, I'm sorry about last night. I was overeager. That's the defect of a virtue. I want to carry on with the search for your son.'
모모
모모'What's the use? He's probably dead. Thanks to you.'
모모
모모'That's a fairly massive accusation, Mr. Hillman.'
모모
모모'Take it. It's yours. And please get out of my way.'
모모
모모He looked compulsively at his wristwatch. 'I'm already late.'
모모
모모He brushed past me and ran upstairs as if I might pursue him. It wasn't a pleasant interview. The unpleasantness stuck in my crop all the way to Los Angeles.
모모
모모11
모모
