모모I BOUGHT A hat a size too large, to accommodate my bandages, and paid a brief visit to the Hollywood division of the LAPD. None of the detective-sergeants in the upstairs offices recognized Mrs. Brown in her deathly disguise. I went from the station to the newsroom of the Hollywood Reporter. Most of the people at work there resented being shown such pictures. The ones who gave them an honest examination failed to identify Mrs. Brown.
모모
모모I tried a number of flesh peddlers long the Strip, with the same lack of success and the same effect. The photographs made me unpopular. These guys and dolls pursuing the rapid buck hated to be reminded of what was waiting on the far side of the last dollar. The violence of the woman's death only made it worse. It could happen to anybody, any time.
모모
모모I started back to my office. I intended to call Bastian and ask him to rush me a Xerox copy of the composite sketch as soon as his artist had completed it. Then I thought of Joey Sylvester.
모모
모모Joey was an old agent who maintained an office of sorts two blocks off Sunset and two flights up. He hadn't been able to adapt to the shift of economic power from the major studios to the independent producers. He lived mainly on his share of residuals from old television movies, and on his memories.
모모
모모I knocked on the door of his cubbyhole and heard him hiding his bottle, as if I might be the ghost of Louis B. Mayer or an emissary from J. Arthur Rank. Joey looked a little disappointed when he opened the door and it was only me. But he resurrected the bottle and offered me a drink in a paper cup. He had a glass tumbler for his own use, and I happened to know that nearly every day he sat at his desk and absorbed a quart of bourbon and sometimes a quart and a half.
모모
모모He was a baby-faced old man with innocent white hair and crafty eyes. His mind was like an old-fashioned lamp with its wick in alcohol, focused so as to light up the past and its chauffeur-driven Packard, and cast the third-floor-walkup present into cool shadow.
모모
모모It wasn't long past noon, and Joey was still in fair shape. 'It's good to see you, Lew boy. I drink to your health.'
모모
모모He did so, with one fatherly hand on my shoulder.
모모
모모'I drink to yours.'
모모
모모The hand on my shoulder reached up and took my hat off. 'What did you do to your head?'
모모
모모'I was slightly shot last night.'
모모
모모'You mean you got drunk and fell down?'
모모
모모'Shot with a gun,' I said.
모모
모모He clucked. 'You shouldn't expose yourself the way you do. Know what you ought to do, Lew boy? Retire and write your memoirs. The unvarnished sensational truth about Hollywood.'
모모
모모'It's been done a thousand times, Joey. Now they're even doing it in the fan mags.'
모모
모모'Not the way you could do it. Give'em the worm's-eye view. There's a title!'
모모
모모He snapped his fingers. 'I bet I could sell your story for twenty-five G's, make it part of a package with Steve McQueen. Give some thought to it, Lew boy. I could open up a lovely jar of olives for you.'
모모
모모'I just opened a can of peas, Joey, and I wonder if you can help me with it. How is your tolerance for pictures of dead people?'
모모
모모'I've seen a lot of them die.'
모모
모모His free hand fluttered toward the wall above his desk. It was prepared with inscribed photographs of vanished players. His other hand raised his tumbler. 'I drink to them.'
모모
모모I cluttered his desk top with the angry pictures. He looked them over mournfully. 'Ach!' he said. 'What the human animal does to itself? Am I supposed to know her?'
모모
모모'She's supposed to have worked in pictures. You know more actors than anybody.'
모모
모모'I did at one time. No more.'
모모
모모'I doubt that she's done any acting recently. She was on the skids.'
모모
모모'It can happen overnight.'
모모
모모In a sense, it had happened to him. He put on his glasses, turned on a desk lamp, and studied the pictures intensively. After a while he said: 'Carol?'
모모
모모'You know her.'
모모
모모He looked at me over his glasses. 'I couldn't swear to it in court. I once knew a little blonde girl, natural blonde, with ears like that. Notice that they're small and close to the head and rather pointed. Unusual ears for a girl.'
모모
모모'Carol who?'
모모
모모'I can't remember. It was a long time ago, back in the forties. I don't think she was using her own name, anyway.'
모모
모모'Why not?'
모모
모모'She had a very stuffy family back in Podunk. They disapproved of the acting bit. I seem to remember she told me she ran away from home.'
모모
모모'In Podunk?'
모모
모모'I didn't mean that literally. Matter of fact, I think she came from some place in Idaho.'
모모
모모'Say that again.'
모모
모모'Idaho. Is your dead lady from Idaho?'
모모
모모'Her husband drives a car with an Idaho license. Tell me more about Carol. When and where did you know her?'
모모
모모'Right here in Hollywood. A friend of mine took an interest in the girl and brought her to me. She was a lovely child. Untouched.'
모모
모모His hands flew apart in the air, un-touching her. 'All she had was high-school acting experience, but I got her a little work. It wasn't hard in those days, with the war still going on. And I had a personal in with all the casting directors on all the lots.'
모모
모모'What year was it, Joey?'
모모
모모He took off his glasses and squinted into the past. 'She came to me in the spring of '45, the last year of the war.'
모모
모모Mrs. Brown, if she was Carol, had been around longer than I'd thought. 'How old was she then?'
모모
모모'Very young. Just a child, like I said. Maybe sixteen.'
모모
모모'And who was the friend who took an interest in her?'
모모
모모'It isn't like you think. It was a woman, one of the girls in the story department at Warner's. She's producing a series now at Television City. But she was just a script girl back in the days I'm talking about.'
모모
모모'You wouldn't be talking about Susanna Drew?'
모모
모모'Yeah. Do you know Susanna?'
모모
모모'Thanks to you. I met her at a party at your house, when you were living in Beverly Hills.'
모모
모모Joey looked startled, as though the shift from one level of the past to another had caught him unawares. 'I remember. That must have been ten years ago.'
모모
모모He sat and thought about ten years ago, and so did I. I had taken Susanna home from Joey's party, and we met at other parties, by agreement. We had things to talk about. She picked my brains for what I knew about people, and I picked hers for what she knew about books. I was crazy about her insane sense of humor.
모모
모모The physical thing came more slowly, as it often does when it promises to be real. I think we tried to force it. We'd both been drinking, and a lot of stuff boiled up from Susanna's childhood. Her father had been a professor at UCLA, who lost his wife young, and he had supervised Susanna's studies. Her father was dead, but she could still feel his breath on the back of her neck.
모모
모모We had a bad passage, and Susanna stopped going to parties, at least the ones I went to. I heard she had a marriage which didn't take. Then she had a career, which took.
모모
모모'How did she happen to know Carol?' I said to Joey.
모모
모모'You'll have to ask her yourself. She told me at the time, but I don't remember. My memory isn't what it was.'
모모
모모The present was depressing him. He poured himself a drink.
모모
모모I refused the offer of one. 'What happened to Carol?'
모모
모모'She dropped out of sight. I think she ran off with a sailor, or something like that. She didn't have what it takes, anyway, after the first bloom.'
모모
모모Joey sighed deeply. 'If you see Susanna, mention my name, will you, Lew? I mean, if you can do it gracefully.'
모모
모모He moved one hand in an undulating horizontal curve. 'She acts like she thought I was dead.'
모모
모모I used Joey's phone to make a call to Susanna Drew's office. Her secretary let me talk to her: 'This is Lew Archer, Susanna.'
모모
모모'How nice to hear from you.'
모모
모모'The occasion isn't so nice,' I said bluntly. 'I'm investigating a murder. The victim may or may not be a girl you knew back in the forties, named Carol.'
모모
모모'Not Carol Harley?'
모모
모모'I'm afraid she's the one.'
모모
모모Her voice roughened. 'And you say she's dead?'
모모
모모'Yes. She was murdered yesterday in a place called Ocean View.'
모모
모모She was silent for a moment. When she spoke again, her voice was softer and younger. 'What can I do?'
모모
모모'Tell me about your friend Carol.'
모모
모모'Not on the telephone, please. The telephone dehumanizes everything.'
모모
모모'A personal meeting would suit me much better,' I said rather formally. 'I have some pictures to show you, to make the identification positive. It should be soon. We're twenty-four hours behind-' 
모모
모모'Come over now. I'll send your name out front.'
모모
모모I thanked Joey and drove to Television City. A guard from the front office escorted me through the building to Susanna Drew's office. It was large and bright, with flowers on the desk and explosive-looking abstract expressionist paintings on the walls. Susanna was standing at the window, crying. She was a slim woman with short straight hair so black the eye stayed on it. She kept her back to me for some time after her secretary went out and closed the door. Finally she turned to face me, still dabbing at her wet cheeks with a piece of yellow Kleenex.
모모
모모She was fortyish now, and not exactly pretty, but neither did she look like anybody else. Her black eyes, even in sorrow, were furiously alive. She had style, and intelligence in the lines and contours of her face. Legs still good. Mouth still good. It said: 'I don't know why I'm carrying on like this. I haven't seen or heard from Carol in seventeen or eighteen years.'
모모
모모She paused. 'I really do know, though. "It is Margaret I mourn for." Do you know Hopkins's poem?'
모모
모모'You know I don't. Who's Margaret?'
모모
모모'The girl in the poem,' she said. 'She's grieving over the fallen autumn leaves. And Hopkins tells her she's grieving for herself. Which is what I'm doing.'
모모
모모She breathed deeply. 'I used to be so young.'
모모
모모'You're not exactly over the hill now.'
모모
모모'Don't flatter me. I'm old old old. I was twenty in 1945 when I knew Carol. Back in the pre-atomic era.'
모모
모모On the way to her desk she paused in front of one of the abstract paintings, as if it represented what had happened to the world since. She sat down with an air of great efficiency. 'Well, let me look at your pictures.'
모모
모모'You won't like them. She was beaten to death.'
모모
모모'God. Who would do that?'
모모
모모'Her husband is the prime suspect.'
모모
모모'Harley? Is she-was she still with that schmo?'
모모
모모'Evidently she still was.'
모모
모모'I knew he'd do her in sooner or later.'
모모
모모I leaned on the end of her desk. 'How did you know that?'
모모
모모'It was one of those fated things. Elective affinities with a reverse twist. She was a really nice child, as tender as a soft-boiled egg, and he was a psychopathic personality. He just couldn't leave her alone.'
모모
모모'How do you know he was a psychopathic personality?'
모모
모모'I know a psychopathic personality when I see one,' she said, lifting her chin. 'I was married to one, briefly, back in the fabulous fifties. Which constitutes me an authority. If you want a definition, a psychopathic personality is a man you can't depend on for anything except trouble.'
모모
모모'And that's the way Harley was?'
모모
모모'Oh yes.'
모모
모모'What was his first name?'
모모
모모'Mike. He was a sailor, a sailor in the Navy.'
모모
모모'And what was the name of his ship?'
모모
모모She opened her mouth to tell me, I believe. But something shifted rapidly and heavily in her mind, and closed off communication. 'I'm afraid I don't remember.'
모모
모모She looked up at me with black opaque eyes.
모모
모모'What did he do before he went into the Navy? Was he a photographer?'
모모
모모She looked back over the years. 'I think he had been a boxer, a professional boxer, not a very successful one. He may have been a photographer, too. He was the sort of person who had been a number of things, none of them successfully.'
모모
모모'Are you sure his name wasn't Harold?'
모모
모모'Everybody called him Mike. It may have been a nom de guerre.'
모모
모모'A what?'
모모
모모'A fighting name. You know.'
모모
모모She breathed deeply. 'You were going to show me some pictures, Lew.'
모모
모모'They can wait. You could help me most right now by telling me what you can remember about Carol and Harley and how you got to know them.'
모모
모모Tensely, she looked at her watch. 'I'm due in a story conference in one minute.'
모모
모모'This is a more important story conference.'
모모
모모She breathed in and out. 'I suppose it is. Well, I'll make it short and simple. It's a simple story, anyway, so simple I couldn't use it in my series. Carol was a country girl from Idaho. She ran away from home with an AWOL sailor. Mike Harley came from the same hick town, I think, but he'd already been in the Navy for a couple of years and seen the world. He promised to take her out to the coast and get her into the movies. She was about sixteen and so naive it made you want to weep or burst out laughing.'
모모
모모'I can hear you laughing. When and where did you happen to meet her?'
모모
모모'In the early spring of 1945.1 was working at Warner's in Burbank and spending weekends in various places. You know the old Barcelona Hotel near Santa Monica? Carol and Harley were staying there, and it's where I-well, I got interested in her.'
모모
모모'Were they married?'
모모
모모'Carol and Harley? I think they'd gone through some sort of ceremony in Tia Juana. At least Carol thought they were married. She was also thought Harley was on extended leave, until the Shore Patrol picked him up. They whisked him back to his ship and Carol was left with nothing to live on, literally nothing. Harley hadn't bothered to make an allotment or anything. So I took her under my wing.'
모모
모모'And brought her to see Joe Sylvester.'
모모
모모'Why not? She was pretty enough, and she wasn't a stupid girl.
모모
모모Joey got her a couple of jobs, and I spent a lot of time with her on grooming and diction and posture. I'd just been through an unhappy love affair, in my blue period, and I was glad to have somebody to occupy my mind with. I let Carol share my apartment, and I actually think I could have made something out of her. A really wholesome Marilyn, perhaps.'
모모
모모She caught herself going into Hollywood patter, and stopped abruptly. 'But it all went blah.'
모모
모모'What happened?'
모모
모모'Harley had left her pregnant, and it began to show. Instead of grooming a starlet, I found myself nursing a pregnant teen-ager with a bad case of homesickness. But she refused to go home. She said her father would kill her.'
모모
모모'Do you remember her father's name?'
모모
모모'I'm afraid not. She was using the name Carol Cooper for professional purposes, but that wasn't her true surname. I think her father lived in Pocatello, if that's any help.'
모모
모모'It may be. You say she was pregnant. What happened to the baby?'
모모
모모'I don't know. Harley turned up before the baby was born. The Navy had finally kicked him out, I believe-and she went back to him. This was in spite of everything I could say or do. They were elective affinities, as I said. The Patient Griselda and the nothing man. So seventeen years later he had to kill her.'
모모
모모'Was he violent when you knew him?'
모모
모모'Was he not.'
모모
모모She crossed her arms over her breast. 'He knocked me down when I tried to prevent her from going back to him. I went out to find help. When I got back to my apartment with a policeman they were both gone, with all the money in my purse. I didn't press charges, and that was the last I saw of them.'
모모
모모'But you still care about Carol.'
모모
모모'She was nice to have around. I never had a sister, or a daughter. In fact, when I think back, feel back, I never had a happier time than that spring and summer in Burbank when Carol was pregnant. We didn't know how lucky we were.'
모모
모모'How so?'
모모
모모'Well, it was a terribly hot summer and the refrigerator kept breaking down and we only had the one bedroom and Carol got bigger and bigger and we had no men in our lives. We thought we were suffering many deprivations. Actually all the deprivations came later.'
모모
모모She looked around her fairly lavish office as if it was a jail cell, then at her watch. 'I really have to go now. My writers and director will be committing mayhem on each other.'
모모
모모'Speaking of mayhem,' I said, 'I'll ask you to look at these pictures if you can stand it. The identification should be nailed down.'
모모
모모'Yes.'
모모
모모I spread the photographs out for her. She looked them over carefully.
모모
모모'Yes. It's Carol. The poor child.'
모모
모모She had become very pale. Her black eyes stood out like the coal eyes of a snowgirl. She got to her feet and walked rather blindly into an adjoining room, shutting the door behind her.
모모
모모I sat at her desk, pinched by her contour chair, and used the phone to ask her secretary to get me Lieutenant Bastian. He was on the line in less than a minute. I told him everything Susanna Drew had told me.
모모
모모She came out of the next room and listened to the end of the conversation. 'You don't waste any time,' she said when I hung up.
모모
모모'Your evidence is important.'
모모
모모'That's good. I'm afraid it's taken all I've got.'
모모
모모She was still very pale. She moved toward me as if the floor under her feet was teetering. 'Will you drive me home?'
모모
모모Home was an apartment on Beverly Glen Boulevard. It had a mezzanine and a patio and African masks on the walls. She invited me to make us both a drink, and we sat and talked about Carol and then about Tom Hillman. She seemed to be very interested in Tom Hillman.
모모
모모I was becoming interested in Susanna. Something about her dark intensity bit into me as deep as memory. Sitting close beside her, looking into her face, I began to ask myself whether, in my present physical and financial and moral condition, I could take on a woman with all those African masks.
모모
모모The damn telephone rang in the next room. She got up, using my knee as a place to rest her hand. I heard her say: 'So it's you. What do you want from me now?'
모모
모모That was all I heard. She closed the door. Five minutes later, when she came out, her face had changed again. A kind of angry fear had taken the place of sorrow in her eyes, as if they had learned of something worse than death.
모모
모모'Who was that, Susanna?'
모모
모모'You'll never know.'
모모
모모I drove downtown in a bitter mood and bully ragged my friend Colton, the DA's investigator, into asking Sacramento for Harold or Mike Harley's record, if any. While I was waiting for an answer I went downstairs to the newsstand and bought an early evening paper.
모모
모모The murder and the kidnapping were front-page news, but there was nothing in the newspaper story I didn't already know, except that Ralph Hillman had had a distinguished combat record as a naval aviator and later (after Newport Line School) as a line officer. He was also described as a millionaire.
모모
모모I sat in Colton's outer office trying to argue away my feeling that Bastian had shoved me onto the fringes of the case. The feeling deepened when the word came back from Sacramento that neither a Harold nor a Mike Harley had a California record, not even for a traffic violation. I began to wonder if I was on the track of the right man.
모모
모모I drove back to the Strip through late afternoon traffic. It was nearly dusk when I reached my office. I didn't bother turning on the light for a while, but sat and watched the green sky at the window lose its color. Stars and neons came out. A plane like a moving group of stars circled far out beyond Santa Monica.
모모
모모I closed the venetian blind, to foil snipers, and turned on the desk lamp and went through the day's mail. It consisted of three bills, and a proposition from the Motel Institute of St Louis. The Institute offered me, in effect, a job at twenty thousand a year managing a million-dollar convention motel. All I had to do was fill out a registration form for the Institute's mail-order course in motel management and send it to the Institute's registrar. If I had a wife, we could register as a couple.
모모
모모I sat toying with the idea of filling out the form, but decided to go out for dinner first. I was making very incisive decisions. I decided to call Susanna Drew and ask her to have dinner with me, telling myself that it was in line of business. I could even deduct the tab from my income tax.
모모
모모She wasn't in the telephone book. I tried Information. Unlisted number. I couldn't afford her anyway.
모모
모모Before I went out for dinner by myself, I checked my answering service. Susanna Drew had left her number for me.
모모
모모'I've been trying to get you,' I said to her.
모모
모모'I've been right here in my apartment.'
모모
모모'I mean before I knew you left your number.'
모모
모모'Oh? What did you have in mind?'
모모
모모'The Motel Institute of St Louis is making a very nice offer to couples who want to register for their course in motel management.'
모모
모모'It sounds inviting. I've always wanted to go out to sunny California and manage a motel.'
모모
모모'Good. We'll have dinner and talk strategy. Television won't last, you know that in your heart. None of these avant-garde movements last.'
모모
모모'Sorry, Lew. I'd love dinner, another night. Tonight I'm not up to it. But I did want to thank you for looking after me this afternoon. I was in a bad way for a while.'
모모
모모'I'm afraid I did it to you.'
모모
모모'No. My whole lousy life reared up and did it to me. You and your pictures were just the catalytic agent.'
모모
모모'Could you stand a visit from a slightly catalytic agent? I'll bring dinner from the delicatessen. I'll buy you a gardenia.'
모모
모모'No. I don't want to see you tonight.'
모모
모모'And you haven't changed your mind about that telephone call you wouldn't tell me about?'
모모
모모'No. There are things about me you needn't know.'
모모
모모'I suppose that's encouraging in a way. Why did you leave your number for me, then?'
모모
모모'I found something that might help you-a picture of Carol taken in 1945.'
모모
모모'I'll come and get it. You haven't really told me how you met her, you know.'
모모
모모'Please don't come. I'll send a messenger with it.'
모모
모모'If you insist. I'll wait in my office.'
모모
모모I gave her the address.
모모
모모'Lew?'
모모
모모Her voice was lighter and sweeter, almost poignant. 'You're not just putting on an act, are you? To try and pry out my personal secrets, I mean?'
모모
모모'It's no act,' I said.
모모
모모'Likewise,' she said. 'Thank you.'
모모
모모I sat in the echoing silence thinking that she had been badly treated by a man or men. It made me angry to think of it. I didn't go out for dinner after all. I sat and nursed my anger until Susanna's messenger arrived.
모모
모모He was a young Negro in uniform who talked like a college graduate. He handed me a sealed manila envelope, which I ripped open.
모모
모모It contained a single glossy print, preserved between two sheets of corrugated cardboard, of a young blonde girl wearing a pageboy bob and a bathing suit. You couldn't pin down the reason for her beauty. It was partly in her clear low forehead, the high curve of her cheek, her perfect round chin; partly in the absolute femaleness that looked out of her eyes and informed her body.
모모
모모Wondering idly who had taken the photograph, I turned it over. Rubber-stamped in purple ink on the back was the legend: 'Photo Credit: Harold "Har" Harley, Barcelona Hotel.'
모모
모모'Will that be all?' the messenger said at the door.
모모
모모'No.'
모모
모모I gave him ten dollars.
모모
모모'This is too much, sir. I've already been paid.'
모모
모모'I know. But I want you to buy a gardenia and deliver it back to Miss Drew.'
모모
모모He said he would.
모모
모모12
모모
모모1945 WAS A long time ago, as time went in California. The Barcelona Hotel was still standing, but I seemed to remember hearing that it was closed. I took the long drive down Sunset to the coastal highway on the off-chance of developing my lead to Harold Harley. Also I wanted to take another look at the building where Harley and Carol had lived.
모모
모모It was a huge old building, Early Hollywood Byzantine, with stucco domes and minarets, and curved verandahs where famous faces of the silent days had sipped their bootleg rum. Now it stood abandoned under the bluff: The bright lights of a service station across the highway showed that its white paint was flaking off and some of the windows were broken.
모모
모모I parked on the weed-ruptured concrete of the driveway and walked up to the front door. Taped to the glass was a notice of bankruptcy, with an announcement that the building was going to be sold at public auction in September.
모모
모모I flashed my light through the glass into the lobby. It was still completely furnished, but the furnishings looked as though they hadn't been replaced in a generation. The carpet was worn threadbare, the chairs were gutted. But the place still had atmosphere, enough of it to summon up a flock of ghosts.
모모
모모I moved along the curving verandah, picking my way among the rain-warped wicker furniture, and shone my light through a french window into the dining room. The tables were set, complete with cocked-hat napkins, but there was dust lying thick on the napery. A good place for ghosts to feed, I thought, but not for me.
모모
모모Just for the hell of it, though, and as a way of asserting myself against the numerous past, I went back to the front door and tapped loudly with my flashlight on the glass. Deep inside the building, at the far end of a corridor, a light showed itself. It was a moving light, which came toward me.
모모
모모The man who was carrying it was big, and he walked as if he had sore feet or legs. I could see his face now in the upward glow of his electric lantern. A crude upturned nose, a bulging forehead, a thirsty mouth. It was the face of a horribly ravaged baby who had never been weaned from the bottle. I could also see that he had a revolver in his other hand.
모모
모모He pointed it at me and flashed the light in my eyes. 'This place is closed. Can't you read?' he shouted through the glass.
모모
모모'I want to talk to you.'
모모
모모'I don't want to talk to you. Beat it. Amscray.'
모모
모모He waved the gun at me. I could tell from his voice and look that he had been drinking hard. A drunk with a gun and an excuse to use it can be murder, literally. I made one more attempt: 'Do you know a photographer named Harold Harley who used to be here?'
모모
모모'Never heard of him. Now you get out of here before I blow a hole in you. You're trespashing.'
모모
모모He lifted the heavy revolver. I withdrew, as far as the service station across the street. A quick-moving man in stained white coveralls came out from under a car on a hoist and offered to sell me gas.
모모
모모'It ought to take ten,' I said. 'Who's the character in the Barcelona Hotel? He acts like he was bitten by a bear.'
모모
모모The man gave me a one-sided smile. 'You run into Otto Sipe?'
모모
모모'If that's the watchman's name.'
모모
모모'Yeah. He worked there so long he thinks he owns the place.'
모모
모모'How long?'
모모
모모'Twenty years or more. I been here since the war myself, and he goes back before me. He was their dick.'
모모
모모'Hotel detective?'
모모
모모'Yeah. He told me once he used to be an officer of the law. If he was, he didn't learn much. Check your oil?'
모모
모모'Don't bother, I just had it changed. Were you here in 1945?'
모모
모모'That's the year I opened. I went into the service early and got out early. Why?'
모모
모모'I'm a private detective. The name is Archer.'
모모
모모I offered him my hand.
모모
모모He wiped his on his coveralls before he took it. 'Daly. Ben Daly.'
모모
모모'A man named Harold Harley used to stay at the Barcelona in 1945. He was a photographer.'
모모
모모Daly's face opened. 'Yeah. I remember him. He took a picture of me and the wife to pay for his gas bill once. We still have it in the house.'
모모
모모'You wouldn't know where he is now?'
모모
모모'Sorry, I haven't seen him in ten years.'
모모
모모'What was the last you saw of him?'
모모
모모'He had a little studio in Pacific Palisades. I dropped in once or twice to say hello. I don't think he's there any more.'
모모
모모'I gather you liked him.'
모모
모모'Sure. There's no harm in Harold.'
모모
모모Men could change. I showed Carol's picture to Daly. He didn't know her.
모모
모모'You couldn't pin down the address in Pacific Palisades for me?'
모모
모모He rubbed the side of his face. It needed retreading, but it was a good face. 'I can tell you where it is.'
모모
모모He told me where it was, on a side street just off Sunset, next door to a short-order restaurant. I thanked him, and paid him for the gas.
모모
모모The short-order restaurant was easy to find, but the building next door to it was occupied by a paperback bookstore. A young woman wearing pink stockings and a ponytail presided over the cash register. She looked at me pensively through her eye makeup when I asked her about Harold Harley.
모모
모모'It seems to me I heard there was a photographer in here at one time.'
모모
모모'Where would he be now?'
모모
모모'I haven't the slightest idea, honestly. We've only been here less than a year ourselves-a year in September.'
모모
모모'How are you doing?'
모모
모모'We're making the rent, at least.'
모모
모모'Who do you pay it to?'
모모
모모'The man who runs the lunch counter. Mr. Vernon. He ought to give us free meals for what he charges. Only don't quote me if you talk to him. We're a month behind now on the rent.'
모모
모모I bought a book and went next door for dinner. It was a place where I could eat with my hat on. While I was waiting for my steak, I asked the waitress for Mr. Vernon. She turned to the white-hatted short-order cook who had just tossed my steak onto the grill.
모모
모모'Mr. Vernon, gentleman wants to speak to you.'
모모
모모He came over to the counter, an unsmiling thin-faced man with glints of gray beard showing on his chin. 'You said you wanted it bloody. You'll get it bloody.'
모모
모모He brandished his spatula.
모모
모모'Good. I understand you own the store next door.'
모모
모모'That and the next one to it.'
모모
모모The thought encouraged him a little. 'You looking for a place to rent?'
모모
모모'I'm looking for a man, a photographer named Harold Harley.'
모모
모모'He rented that store for a long time. But he couldn't quite make a go of it. There's too many photographers in this town. He held on for seven or eight years after the war and then gave up on it.'
모모
모모'You don't know where he is now?'
모모
모모'No sir, I do not.'
모모
모모The sizzling of my steak reached a certain intensity, and he heard it. He went and flipped it with his spatula and came back to me. 'You want french frieds?'
모모
모모'All right. What's the last you saw of Harley?'
모모
모모'The last I heard of him he moved out to the Valley. That was a good ten years ago. He was trying to run his business out of the front room of his house in Van Nuys. He's a pretty good photographer-he took a fine picture of my boy's christening party-but he's got no head for business. I ought to know, he still owes me three months' rent.'
모모
모모Six young people came in and lined up along the counter. They had wind in their hair, sand in their ears, and the word 'Surfbirds' stenciled across the backs of their identical yellow sweatshirts. All of them, girls and boys, ordered two hamburgers apiece.
모모
모모One of the boys put a quarter in the jukebox and played 'Surfin' ain't no sin.'
모모
모모Mr. Vernon got twelve hamburger patties out of the refrigerator and lined them up on the grill. He put my steak on a plate with a handful of fried potatoes and brought it to me personally.
모모
모모'I could look up that Van Nuys address if it's important. I kept it on account of the rent he owed me.'
모모
모모'It's important.'
모모
모모I showed him Carol's picture, the young one Harley had taken. 'Do you recognize his wife?'
모모
모모'I didn't even know he had a wife. I didn't think he'd rate a girl like that.'
모모
모모'Why not?'
모모
모모'He's no ladies' man. He never was. Harold's the quiet type.'
모모
모모Doubt was slipping in again that I was on the right track. It made my head ache. 'Can you describe him to me?'
모모
모모'He's just an ordinary-looking fellow, about my size, five foot ten. Kind of a long nose. Blue eyes. Sandy hair. There's nothing special about him. Of course he'd be older now.'
모모
모모'How old?'
모모
모모'Fifty at least. I'm fifty-nine myself, due to retire next year. Excuse me, mister.'
모모
모모He flipped the twelve hamburger patties over, distributed twelve half-buns on top of them and went out through a swinging door at the back. I ate my steak. Mr. Vernon returned with a slip of paper on which he had written Harley's Van Nuys address: 956 Elmhurst.
모모
모모The waitress delivered the hamburgers to the surfbirds. They munched them in time to the music. The song the jukebox was playing as I went out had a refrain about 'the day that I caught the big wave and made you my slave.'
모모
모모I drove up Sunset onto the San Diego Freeway headed north.
모모
모모Elmhurst was a working-class street of prewar bungalows built too close together. It was a warm night in the Valley, and some people were still out on their porches and lawns. A fat man drinking beer on the porch of 956 told me that Harley had sold him the house in 1960. He had his present address because he was still paying Harley monthly installments on a second trust deed.
모모
모모That didn't sound like the Harley I knew. I asked him for a description.
모모
모모'He's kind of a sad character,' the fat man said. 'One of these guys that wouldn't say "boo" to a goose. He's had his troubles, I guess.'
모모
모모'What kind of troubles?'
모모

모모
모모He gave me Harley's address in Long Beach, which is a long way from Van Nuys. It was close to midnight when I found the house, a tract house near Long Beach Boulevard. It had brown weeds in the front yard, and was lightless, like most of the houses in the street.
모모
모모I drove past a street light to the end of the block and walked back. The all-night traffic on the boulevard filled the air with a kind of excitement, rough and forlorn. I was raised in Long Beach, and I used to cruise its boulevards in a model-A Ford. Their sound, whining, threatening, rising, fading, spoke to something deep in my mind which I loved and hated. I didn't want to knock on Harley's door. I was almost certain I had the wrong man.
모모
모모The overhead door of the attached garage was closed but not locked. I opened it quietly. The street light down the block shone on the rear of a dirty white Ford sedan with an Idaho license plate.
모모
모모I went to the left-hand door of the car and opened it. The dome light came on. The car was registered in the name of Robert Brown with an address in Pocatello. My heart was pounding so hard I could scarcely breathe.
모모
모모.. The door from the garage into the house was suddenly outlined up light. The door sprung open. The light slapped me across the eyes and drenched me.
모모
모모'Mike?' said the voice of a man I didn't know. He looked around the corner of the door frame. 'Is that you, Mike?'
모모
모모'I saw Mike yesterday.'

모모
모모'A friend.'
모모

모모

모모
모모'My brother didn't tell me about a partner.'
모모

모모

'He didn't tell me a thing about you or anything. I don't know why you come to me. That car is mine. I traded him my crate for it.'

모모'Was that wise?'
모모
모모'I dunno, maybe not.'
모모

모모

He said.'

I remembered Sam Jackman telling me that Tom had been wearing a black sweater on Sunday. I jerked the yarn loose, angrily, and put it away in an envelope in my pocket. I slammed the turtleback down on the possibility which the black yarn suggested to my mind.
모모
모모13
모모

He was holding a .22 rifle upright between his knees. He didn't point it at me. I took it away from him and unloaded the single shell.

모모
모모'It's in the front room.'

Old Man, Old Woman, Young Woman, Sunset, Wild flowers, Mountain, Seascape; and Lila. Most of them had beer hand-tinted, and three portraits of Lila smiled at me from various angles, so that I felt surrounded by toothy, flesh-colored face.

모모

모모
모모'Before I sat down with the telephone I looked more closely at the portrait: a grizzled farmer with flat angry eyes and a mouth like a bear trap. Then I called Arnie Walters in Reno and gave him a rundown on the old man's son, Mike Harley, ex-sailor, ex-fighter, ex-bartender, gambler, kidnapper, wife-beater, putative murderer and driver of a 1958 Plymouth two-door, California license number IKT 449.

모모

모모
모모Arnie whistled. 'What you're doing isn't ethical.'
모모
모모'Yes it is. I'm temporarily an investigator for the local sheriff's office.'

모모
모모'I know that. You'll be paid.'

모모
모모'If you say so.'

I had about three hundred dollars in the bank, about two hundred in cash. I owned an equity in the car and some clothes and furniture. My total net worth, after nearly twenty years in the detective business, was in the neighborhood of thirty-five hundred dollars. And Ralph Hillman, with his money, was letting me finance my own search for his son.
모모
모모On the other hand, I answered my self-pity; I was doing what I wanted to be doing. I wanted to take the man who had taken me.
모모
모모I wanted to find Tom. I couldn't drop the case just as it was breaking. And I needed Arnie to backstop me in Nevada. Carry on.
모모
모모I made a second collect call, to Lieutenant Bastian. It was long past midnight, but he was still on duty in his office. I told him I was bringing in a witness, and I gave him a capsule summary of what the witness was going to say. Bastian expressed a proper degree of surprise and delight.

모모

모모
모모He fingered the tie rack distraughtly.
모모
모모I chose one for him, a dark blue tie with a conservative pattern, the kind you wear to the funerals of friends. We closed up the house and garage and drove south out of Long Beach.
모모
모모It was less than an hour's drive to Pacific Point. Harold was intermittently talkative, but his silences grew longer. I asked him about his and his brother's early life in Idaho. It had been a hard life, in an area subject to blizzards in the winter and floods in the spring and extreme heat in the summer. Their father believed that boys were a kind of domestic animal that ought to be put to work soon after weaning. They were hoeing corn and digging potatoes when they were six, and milking the cows at eight or nine.
모모
모모They could have stood the work, if it hadn't been for the punishment that went with it. I'd seen Harold's scars. The old man used a piece of knotted wire on them. Mike was the first to run away. He lived in Pocatello for a couple of years with a man named Robert Brown, a high-school coach and counselor who took him in and tried to give him a chance.
모모
모모Robert Brown was Carol's father. Mike paid him back for his kindness eventually by running away with his daughter.
모모
모모'How old was Mike then?'
모모
모모'Twenty or so. Let's see, it was about a year after they drafted him into the Navy. Yeah, he'd be about twenty. Carol was only sixteen.'
모모
모모'Where were you at the time?'
모모
모모'Working here in Los Angeles. I was 4-F. I had a job taking pictures for a hotel.'
모모
모모'The Barcelona Hotel,' I said.
모모
모모'That's right.'
모모

모모
모모'I understand that Carol and your brother stayed there, too.'
모모
모모'For a little. That was when he was AWOL and hiding out. I let them use my room for a couple of weeks.'
모모
모모'You've done a lot for your brother in your time.'
모모
모모'Yeah. He paid me back by trying to frame me for stealing a Navy camera. There's one extra thing I could have done for him.'
모모
모모'What was that?'
모모
모모'I could have drowned him in the river when he was a kid. That's all the use he's been to anybody. Especially Carol.'
모모
모모'Why did she stick to him?'
모모
모모He groaned. 'She wanted to, I guess.'
모모
모모'Were they married?'
모모
모모He answered slowly. 'I think so. She thought so. But I never saw any papers to prove it.'
모모
모모'Lately,' I said, 'they've been calling themselves Mr. and Mrs. Robert Brown. The car he left with you is registered to Robert Brown.'
모모
모모'I wondered where he got it. Now I suppose I'll have to give it back to the old guy.'
모모
모모'First the police will be wanting it.'
모모
모모'Yeah. I guess they will at that.'
모모
모모The thought of the police seemed to depress him profoundly. He sat without speaking for a while. I caught a glimpse of him in the headlights of a passing car. He was sitting with his chin on his chest. His body appeared to be resisting the movement which was carrying him toward his meeting with the police.
모모
모모'Do you know Carol's father?'
모모
모모I asked him finally.
모모
모모'I've met Mr. Brown. Naturally he holds Mike against me. God knows what he'll think of me now, with Carol dead and all.'
모모
모모'You're not your brother, Harold. You can't go on blaming yourself for what he's done.'
모모
모모'It's my fault, though.'
모모
모모'Carol's death?'
모모
모모'That, too, but I meant the kidnapping. I set it up for Mike without meaning to. I gave him the idea of the whole thing.'
모모
모모'How did you do that?'
모모
모모'I don't want to talk about it.'
모모
모모'You brought it up, Harold. You seem to want to get it off your chest.'
모모
모모'I've changed my mind.'
모모
모모I couldn't get him to change it back. He had a passive stubbornness which wouldn't be moved. We drove in complete silence the rest of the way.
모모
모모I delivered Harold and the five hundred dollars to Lieutenant Bastian, who was waiting in his office in the courthouse, and checked in at the first hotel I came to.
모모
모모14
모모
모모AT NINE O'CLOCK in the morning, with the taste of coffee still fresh in my mouth, I was back at the door of the lieutenant's office. He was waiting for me.
모모
모모'Did you get any sleep at all?'
모모
모모I said.
모모
모모'Not much.'
모모
모모The loss of sleep had affected him hardly at all, except that his voice and bearing were less personal and more official. 'You've had an active twenty-four hours. I have to thank you for turning up the brother. His evidence is important, especially if this case ever gets into court.'
모모
모모'I have some other evidence to show you.'
모모
모모But Bastian hadn't finished what he was saying: 'I talked the sheriff into paying you twenty-five dollars per diem plus ten cents a mile, if you will submit a statement.'
모모
모모'Thanks, but it can wait. You could do me a bigger favor by talking Ralph Hillman into bankrolling me.'
모모
모모'I can't do that, Archer.'
모모
모모'You could tell him the facts. I've spent several hundred dollars of my own money, and I've been getting results.'
모모
모모'Maybe, if I have an opportunity.'
모모
모모He changed the subject abruptly: 'The pathologist who did the autopsy on Mrs. Brown has come up with something that will interest you. Actual cause of death was a stab wound in the heart. It wasn't noticed at first because it was under the breast.'
모모
모모'That does interest me. It could let Harley out.'
모모
모모'I don't see it that way. He beat her and then stabbed her.'
모모
모모'Do you have the weapon?'
모모
모모'No. The doctor says it was a good-sized blade, thin but quite broad, and very sharp, with a sharp point. It went into her like butter, the doctor says.'
모모
모모He took no pleasure in the image. His face was saturnine. 'Now what was the evidence you referred to just now?'
모모
모모I showed Bastian the piece of black yarn and told him where I had found it. He picked up the implication right away: 'The trunk, eh? I'm afraid it doesn't look too promising far the boy. He was last seen wearing a black sweater. I believe his mother knitted it for him.'
모모
모모He studied the scrap of wool under his magnifying glass. 'This looks like knitting yarn to me, too. Mrs. Hillman ought to be shown this.'
모모
모모He put the piece of yarn under glass on an evidence board. Then he picked up the phone and made an appointment with the Hillmans at their house in El Rancho, an appointment for both of us. We drove out through morning fog in two cars. At the foot of the Hillmans' driveway a man in plain clothes came out of the fog-webbed bushes and waved us on.
모모
모모Mrs. Perez, wearing black shiny Sunday clothes, admitted us to the reception hall. Hillman came out of the room where the bar was. His movements were somnambulistic and precise, as if they were controlled by some external power. His eyes were still too bright.
모모
모모He shook hands with Bastian and, after some hesitation, with me. 'Come into the sitting room, gentlemen. It's good of you to make the trip out here. Elaine simply wasn't up to going downtown. If I could only get her to eat,' he said.
모모
모모She was sitting on the chesterfield near the front window. The morning light was unkind to her parched blonde face. It was two full days and nights since the first telephone call on Monday morning. She looked as if all the minutes in those forty-eight hours had passed through her body like knots in wire. The red piece of knitting on the seat beside her hadn't grown since I'd seen it last.
모모
모모She managed a rather wizened smile and extended her hand to Bastian. 'Ralph says you have something to show me.'
모모
모모'Yes. It's a piece of yarn, which may or may not have come off your boy's sweater.'
모모
모모'The black one I knitted for him?'
모모
모모'It may be. We want to know if you recognize the yarn.'
모모
모모Bastian handed her the evidence board. She put on reading glasses and examined it. Then she put it aside, rose abruptly, and left the room. Hillman made a move to follow her. He stopped with his hands out in a helpless pose which he was still in when she returned.
모모
모모She was carrying a large, figured linen knitting bag. Crouching on the chesterfield, she rummaged among its contents and tossed out balls of wool of various colors. Her furiously active hand came to rest holding a half-used ball of black wool.
모모
모모'This is what I had left over from Tom's sweater. I think it's the same. Can you tell?'
모모
모모Bastian broke off a piece of yarn from the ball and compared it under a glass with my piece. He turned from the window: 'The specimens appear identical to me. If they are, we can establish it under the microscope.'
모모
모모'What does it mean if they are identical?' Ralph Hillman said.
모모
모모'I prefer not to say until we have microscopic confirmation.'
모모
모모Hillman took hold of Bastian's arm and shook him. 'Don't double-talk me, Lieutenant.'
모모
모모Bastian broke loose and stepped back. There were white frozen-looking patches around his nose and mouth. His eyes were somber.
모모
모모'Very well, I'll tell you what I know. This piece of yarn was found by Mr. Archer here, caught in the lock of a car trunk. The car was one driven by the alleged kidnapper, Harley.'
모모
모모'You mean that Tom was riding in the trunk?'
모모
모모'He may have been, yes.'
모모
모모'But he wouldn't do that if-' Hillman's mouth worked. 'You mean Tom is dead?'
모모
모모'He may be. We won't jump to any conclusions.'
모모
모모Elaine Hillman produced a noise, a strangled gasp, which made her the center of attention. She spoke in a thin voice, halfway between a child's and an old woman's: 'I wish I had never recognized the yarn.'
모모
모모'It wouldn't change the facts, Mrs. Hillman.'
모모
모모'Well, I don't want any more of your dreadful facts. The waiting is bad enough, without these refinements of torment.'
모모
모모Hillman bent over and tried to quiet her. 'That isn't fair, Elaine. Lieutenant Bastian is trying to help us.'
모모
모모He had said the same thing about me. It gave me the queer feeling that time was repeating itself and would go on endlessly repeating itself, as it does in hell.
모모
모모She said: 'He's going about it in a strange way. Look what he's made me do. All my balls of yarn are spilled on the floor.'
모모
모모She kicked at them with her tiny slippered feet.
모모
모모Hillman got down on his knees to pick them up. She kicked at him, without quite touching him. 'Get away, you're no help, either. If you'd been a decent father, this would never have happened.'
모모
모모Bastian picked up the evidence board and turned to me. 'We'd better go.'
모모
모모Nobody asked us not to. But Hillman followed us out into the hall.
모모
모모'Please forgive us, we're not ourselves. You haven't really told me anything.'
모모
모모Bastian answered him coolly: 'We have no definite conclusions to report.'
모모
모모'But you think Tom's dead.'
모모
모모'I'm afraid he may be. We'll learn more from an analysis of the contents of that car trunk. If you'll excuse me, Mr. Hillman, I don't have time for further explanations now.'
모모
모모'I do,' I said.
모모
모모For the first time that morning Hillman looked at me as if I might be good for something more than a scapegoat. 'Are you willing to tell me what's been going on?'
모모
모모'So far as I understand it.'
모모
모모'I'll leave you men together then,' Bastian said. He went out, and a minute later I heard his car go down the drive.
모모
모모Hillman deputed Mrs. Perez to stay with his wife. He led me into a wing of the house I hadn't visited, down an arching corridor like a tunnel carved through chalk, to a spacious study. Two of the oak-paneled walls were lined with books, most of them in calf-bound sets, as if Hillman had bought or inherited a library. A third wall was broken by a large deep window overlooking the distant sea.
모모
모모The fourth wall was hung with a number of framed photographs. One was a blownup snapshot of Dick Leandro crouching in the cockpit of a racing yacht with his hand on the helm and the white wake boiling at his back. One showed a group of Navy fliers posing together on a flight deck. I recognized a younger Hillman on the far right of the group. There were other similar pictures, taken ashore and afloat; one of a torpedo-bomber squadron flying formation in old World War II Devastators; one of an escort carrier photographed from far overhead, so that it lay like a shingle on the bright, scarified water.
모모
모모It seemed to me that Hillman had brought me to this specific room, this wall, for a purpose. The somnambulistic precision of his movements was probably controlled by the deep unconscious. At any rate, we were developing the same idea at the same time, and the photographer of the escort carrier was the catalyst.
모모
모모'That was my last ship,' Hillman said. 'The fact is, for a few weeks at the end I commanded her.'
모모
모모'A few weeks at the end of the war?'
모모
모모'A few weeks at her end. The war was long since over. We took her from Dago through the Canal to Boston and put her in mothballs.'
모모
모모His voice was tender and regretful. He might have been talking about the death of a woman.
모모
모모'She wasn't the Perry Bay by any chance?'
모모
모모'Yes.'
모모
모모He swung around to look at me. 'You've heard of her?'
모모
모모'Just last night. This whole things is coming together, Mr. Hillman. Does the name Mike Harley mean anything to you?'
모모
모모His eyes blurred. 'I'm afraid you're confusing me. The name you mentioned earlier was Harold Harley.'
모모
모모'I had the wrong name. Harold is Mike's brother, and he's the one I talked to last night. He told me Mike served on the Perry Bay.'
모모
모모Hillman nodded slowly. 'I remember Mike Harley. I have reason to. He caused me a lot of trouble. In the end I had to recommend an undesirable discharge.'
모모
모모'For stealing a Navy camera.'
모모
모모He gave me a swift responsive look. 'You do your homework thoroughly, Mr. Archer. Actually we let him off easy, because he wasn't quite responsible. He could have been sent to Portsmouth for stealing that expensive camera.'
모모
모모He backed up into a chair and sat down suddenly, as if he'd been struck by the full impact of the past. 'So eighteen years later he has to steal my son.'
모모
모모I stood by the window and waited for him to master the immense coincidence. It was no coincidence in the usual sense, of course. Hillman had been in authority over Harley, and had given Harley reason to hate him. I had heard the hatred speak on the telephone Monday.
모모
모모The fog over the sea was burning off. Ragged blue holes opened and closed in the grayness. Hillman came to the window and stood beside me. His face was more composed, except for the fierce glitter of his eyes.
모모
모모'When I think of what that man has done to me,' he said. 'Tell me the rest of it, Archer. All you know.'
모모
모모I told him the rest of it. He listened as if I was an oracle telling him the story of his future life. He seemed particularly interested in the murdered woman, Carol, and I asked him if he had ever met her.
모모
모모He shook his head. 'I didn't know Harley was married.'
모모
모모'The marriage may not have been legal. But it lasted.'
모모
모모'Did Harley have children?'
모모
모모'One at least.'
모모
모모'How could a man with a child of his own-?'
모모
모모He didn't finish the thought. Another thought rushed in on his excited mind: 'At any rate this disposes of the notion that Tom was mixed up with the woman.'
모모
모모'Not necessarily. Harley could have been using her as bait.'
모모
모모'But that's fantastic. The woman must be-have been old enough to be his mother.'
모모
모모'Still, she wasn't an old woman. She was born about 1930.'
모모
모모'And you're seriously suggesting that Tom had an affair with her?'
모모
모모'It's an academic question under the circumstances, Mr. Hillman.'
모모
모모His patrician head turned slowly toward me, catching the light on its flat handsome planes. The days were carving him like sculpture. 'You mean the fact that Tom is dead.'
모모
모모'It isn't a fact yet. It is a strong possibility.'
모모
모모'If my boy were alive, wouldn't he have come home by now?'
모모
모모'Not if he's deliberately staying away.'
모모
모모'Do you have reason to believe that he is?'
모모
모모'Nothing conclusive, but several facts suggest the possibility. He was seen with the woman on Sunday, under his own power. And he did run away in the first place.'
모모
모모'From Laguna Perdida School. Not from us.'
모모
모모'He may expect to be clapped back into the school if he returns.'
모모
모모'Good Lord, I'd never do that.'
모모
모모'You did it once.'
모모
모모'I was forced to by circumstances.'
모모
모모'What were the circumstances, Mr. Hillman?'
모모
모모'There's no need to go into them. As you would say, the question is academic.'
모모
모모'Did he attempt suicide?'
모모
모모'No.'
모모
모모'Homicide?'
모모
모모His eyes flickered. 'Certainly not.'
모모
모모He changed the subject hurriedly: 'We shouldn't be standing here talking. If Thomas is alive, he's got to be found. Harley is the one man who must know where he is, and you tell me Harley is probably on his way to Nevada.'
모모
모모'He's probably there by now.'
모모
모모'Why aren't you? I'd fly you myself if I could leave my wife. But you can charter a plane.'
모모
모모I explained that this took money, of which I'd already spent a fair amount in his behalf.
모모
모모'I'm sorry, I didn't realize.'
모모
모모He produced the two-thousand-dollar check that Dr Sponti and Mr. Squerry had given him on Monday, and endorsed it to me. I was back in business.
모모
모모15
모모
모모STELLA, IN HER hooded bluejacket, was waiting for me part way down the driveway. The girl had a heavy pair of binoculars hung around her neck on a strap. Her face was bloodless and thin, as if it had provided sustenance for her eyes.
모모
모모When I stopped the car, she climbed uninvited into the seat beside me. 'I've been watching for you.'
모모
모모'Is that what the field glasses are for?'
모모
모모She nodded gravely. 'I watch everybody who comes in or goes out of Tommy's house. Mother thinks I'm bird-watching, which she lets me do because it's a status-symbol activity. Actually I am doing a bird study for next year's biology class, on the nesting habits of the acorn woodpeckers. Only they all look so much alike they're hard to keep track of.'
모모
모모'So are people.'
모모
모모'I'm finding that out.'
모모
모모She leaned toward me. Her small breast brushed my shoulder like a gift of trust. 'But you know what, Mr. Archer? Tommy tried to call me this morning, I'm almost certain.'
모모
모모'Tell me about it.'
모모
모모'There isn't much to tell, really. It was one of those calls with nobody on the other end of the line. My mother answered the phone, and that's why Tommy didn't speak. He wanted me to answer it.'
모모
모모Her eyes were luminous with hope.
모모
모모'What makes you think it was Tommy?'
모모
모모'I just know it was. Besides, he called at five to eight, which is the exact same time he always used to call me in the morning. He used to pick me up and drive me to school.'
모모
모모'That isn't too much to go on, Stella. More likely it was a wrong number.'
모모
모모'No. I believe it was Tommy. And he'll be trying again.'
모모
모모'Why would he call you instead of his parents?'
모모
모모'He's probably afraid to call them. He must be in serious trouble.'
모모
모모'You can be sure of that, one way or another.'
모모
모모I was only trying to moderate her hopefulness, but I frightened her. She said in a hushed voice: 'You've found out something.'
모모
모모'Nothing definite. We're on the track of the kidnapper. And incidentally, I have to be on my way.'
모모
모모She held me with her eyes. 'He really was kidnapped then? He didn't go to them of his own accord or anything like that?'
모모
모모'He may have in the first place. After that, I don't know. Did Tommy ever mention a woman named Carol?'
모모
모모'The woman who was killed?'
모모
모모'Yes.'
모모
모모'He never did. Why? Did he know her?'
모모
모모'He knew her very well.'
모모
모모She caught my implication and shook her head. 'I don't believe it.'
모모
모모'That doesn't prevent it from being true, Stella. Didn't you ever see them together?'
모모
모모I got out my collection of pictures and selected the one that Harold Harley had taken of Carol in 1945. The girl studied it. She said with something like awe in her voice: 'She's-she was very beautiful. She couldn't have been much older than I am.'
모모
모모'She wasn't, when the picture was taken. But that was a long time ago, and you should make allowances for that.'
모모
모모'I've never seen her. I'm sure. And Tommy never said a word about her.'
모모
모모She looked at me glumly. 'People are hard to keep track of.'
모모
모모She handed me the picture as if it was heavy and hot and would spill if it was tilted.
모모
모모At this point a female moose deprived of her calf, or something closely resembling her, came crashing through the oak woods. It was Stella's mother. Her handsome red head was tousled and her face was brutalized by anxiety. She spotted Stella and charged around to her side of the car. Stella turned up the window and snapped the lock.
모모
모모Rhea Carlson rapped on the glass with her fist. 'Come out of there. What do you think you're doing?'
모모
모모'Talking to Mr. Archer.'
모모
모모'You must be crazy. Are you trying to ruin yourself?'
모모
모모'I don't care what happens to me, that's true.'
모모
모모'You have no right to talk like that. You're ungrateful!'
모모
모모'Ungrateful for what?'
모모
모모'I gave you life, didn't I? Your father and I have given you everything.'
모모
모모'I don't want everything. I just want to be alone, Mother.'
모모
모모'No! You come out of there.'
모모
모모'I don't have to.'
모모
모모'Yes you do,' I said.
모모
모모Stella looked at me as if I had betrayed her to the enemy.
모모
모모'She's your mother,' I said, 'and you're a minor, and if you don't obey her you're out of control, and I'm contributing to the delinquency of a minor.'
모모
모모'You are?'
모모
모모'Reluctantly,' I said.
모모
모모The word persuaded her. She even gave me a little half-smile. Then she unlocked the door and climbed out of the car. I got out and walked around to their side. Rhea Carlson looked at me as if I might be on the point of assaulting her.
모모
모모'Calm down, Mrs. Carlson. Nothing's happened.'
모모
모모'Oh? Would you know?'
모모
모모'I know that no harm will ever come to Stella if I'm around. May I ask you a question?'
모모
모모She hesitated. 'I won't promise to answer it.'
모모
모모'You received a phone call this morning at five to eight. Was it local or long distance?'
모모
모모'I don't know. Most of our long-distance calls are dialed direct.'
모모
모모'Was anything said?'
모모
모모'I said hello.'
모모
모모'I mean on the other end of the line.'
모모
모모'No. Not a word.'
모모
모모'Did whoever it was hang up?'
모모
모모'Yes, and I'm sure it wasn't the Hillman boy. It was just another stupid mistake in dialing. We get them all the time.'
모모
모모'It was so Tommy,' Stella said. 'I know it.'
모모
모모'Don't believe her. She's always making things up.'
모모
모모'I am not.'
모모
모모Stella looked ready to cry.
모모
모모'Don't contradict me, Stella. Why do you always have to contradict me?'
모모
모모'I don't.'
모모
모모'You do.'
모모
모모I stepped between them. 'Your daughter's a good girl, and she's almost a woman. Please try to bear that in mind, and treat her gently.'
모모
모모Mrs. Carlson said in scornful desperation: 'What do you know about mothers and daughters? Who are you, anyway?'
모모
모모'I've been a private detective since the war. In the course of time you pick up a few primitive ideas about people, and you develop an instinct for the good ones. Like Stella.'
모모
모모Stella blushed. Her mother peered at me without understanding. In my rear-view mirror, as I started away, I saw them walking down the driveway, far apart. It seemed a pity. For all I knew, Rhea Carlson was a good girl, too.
모모
모모I drove downtown, and took Sponti's two-thousand-dollar check to the bank it was drawn on. I endorsed it, under Ralph Hillman's signature: 'With many thanks, Lew Archer.'
모모
모모It was a weak riposte for being fired, but it gave me some satisfaction to think that it might bring out the purple in Dr Sponti's face.
모모
모모The transfusion of cash made me feel mobile and imaginative. Just on a hunch, I drove back to Harold Harley's place in Long Beach. It was a good hunch. Lila answered the door.
모모
모모She had on an apron and a dusting cap, and she pushed a strand of black hair up under the cap. Her breast rose with the gesture. Lila wasn't a pretty woman, but she had vitality.
모모
모모'Are you another one of them?' she said.
모모
모모'Yes. I thought you left Harold.'
모모
모모'So did I. But I decided to come back.'
모모
모모'I'm glad you did. He needs your support.'
모모
모모'Yeah.'
모모
모모Her voice softened. 'What's going to happen to Harold? Are they going to lock him up and throw the key away?'
모모
모모'Not if I can help it.'
모모
모모'Are you with the FBI?'
모모
모모'I'm more of a free lance.'
모모
모모'I was wondering. They came this morning and took the car away. No Harold. Now no car. Next they'll be taking the house from over my head. All on account of that lousy brother of his. It isn't fair.'
모모
모모'It'll be straightened out. I'll tell you the same thing I told Harold. His best chance of getting free and clear is to tell the truth.'
모모
모모'The truth is, he let his brother take advantage of him. He always has. Mike is still-' She clapped her hand to her mouth and looked at me over it with alarm in her brown eyes.
모모
모모'What is Mike still doing, Mrs. Harley?'
모모
모모She glanced up and down the dingy street. A few young children were playing in the yards, with their mothers watching them. Lila plucked at my sleeve.
모모
모모'Come inside, will you? Maybe we can make some kind of a deal.'
모모
모모The front door opened directly into the living room. I stepped over a vacuum-cleaner hose just inside the door.
모모
모모'I've been cleaning the house,' she said. 'I had to do something and that was all I could think of.'
모모
모모'I hope Harold will be coming home to appreciate it soon.'
모모
모모'Yeah. It would help him, wouldn't it, if I helped you to nail his brother?'
모모
모모'It certainly would.'
모모
모모'Would you let him go if you got Mike in his place?'
모모
모모'I can't promise that. I think it would probably happen.'
모모
모모'Why can't you promise?'
모모
모모'I'm just a local investigator. But Mike is the one we really want. Do you know where he is, Mrs. Harley?'
모모
모모For a long moment she stood perfectly still, her face as unchanging as one of her photographs hanging on the wall. Then she nodded slightly.
모모
모모'I know where he was at three A.M. this morning.'
모모
모모She jabbed a thumb toward the telephone. 'He called here from Las Vegas at three A.M. He wanted Harold. I told him I didn't know where Harold was-he was gone when I came home last night.'
모모
모모'You're sure it was Mike who called?'
모모
모모'It couldn't have been anybody else. I know his voice. And it isn't the first time he called here, whining and wheedling for some of our hard-earned money.'
모모
모모'He wanted money?'
모모
모모'That's right. I was to wire him five hundred dollars to the Western Union office in Las Vegas.'
모모
모모'But he was carrying over twenty thousand.'
모모
모모Her face closed, and became impassive. 'I wouldn't know about that. All I know is what he said. He needed money bad, and I was to wire him five hundred, which he would pay back double in twenty-four hours. I told him I'd see him in the hot place first. He was gambling.'
모모
모모'It sounds like it, doesn't it?'
모모
모모'He's a crazy gambler,' she said. 'I hate a gambler.'
모모
모모I called the Waiters Agency in Reno. Arnie's wife and partner Phyllis told me that Arnie had taken an early plane to Vegas. Harold Harley's two-tone Plymouth had been spotted at a motel on the Vegas Strip.
모모
모모Not more than two hours later, after a plane ride of my own, I was sitting in a room of the motel talking with Arnie and the Plymouth's new owner. He was a man named Fletcher who said he was from Phoenix, Arizona, although his accent sounded more like Texas. He was dressed up in a western dude costume, with high-heeled boots, a matching belt with a fancy silver buckle, and an amethyst instead of a tie. His Stetson lay on one of the twin beds, some women's clothes on the other. The woman was in the bathroom taking a bath, Arnie told me, and I never did see her.
모모
모모Mr. Fletcher was large and self-assured and very rough-looking. His face had been chopped rather carelessly from granite, then put out to weather for fifty or fifty-five years.
모모
모모'I didn't want to buy his heap,' he said. 'I have a new Cadillac in Phoenix, you can check that. He didn't even have a pink slip for it. I paid him five hundred for the heap because he was broke, desperate to stay in the game.'
모모
모모'What game was that?' I asked him.
모모
모모'Poker.'
모모
모모'It was a floating game,' Arnie said, 'in one of the big hotels. Mr. Fletcher refuses to name the hotel, or the other players. It went on all day yesterday and most of last night. There's no telling how much Harley lost, but he lost everything he had.'
모모
모모'Over twenty thousand, probably. Was the game rigged?'
모모
모모Fletcher turned his head and looked at me the way a statue looks at a man. 'It was an honest game, friend. It had to be. I was the big winner.'
모모
모모'I wasn't questioning your honesty.'
모모
모모'No sir. Some of the finest people in Phoenix visit the little woman and I in our residence and we visit them in their residences. Honest Jack Fletcher, they call me.'
모모
모모There was a silence in which the three of us sat and listened to the air-conditioner. I said: 'That's Fine, Mr. Fletcher. How much did you win?'
모모
모모'That's between I and the tax collector, friend. I won a bundle. Which is why I gave him five hundred for his heap. I have no use for the heap. You can take it away.'
모모
모모He lifted his arm in an imperial gesture.
모모
모모'We'll be doing just that,' Arnie said.
모모
모모'You're welcome to it. Anything I can do to cooperate.'
모모
모모'You can answer a few more questions, Mr. Fletcher.'
모모
모모I got out my picture of Tom. 'Did you see this boy with Harley at any time?'
모모
모모He examined the picture as if it was a card he had drawn, then passed it back to me. 'I did not.'
모모
모모'Hear any mention of him?'
모모
모모'I never did. Harley came and went by himself and he didn't talk. You could see he didn't belong in a high-stakes game, but he had the money, and he wanted to lose it.'
모모
모모'He wanted to lose it?'
모모
모모Arnie said.
모모
모모'That's right, the same way I wanted to win. He's a born loser, I'm a born winner.'
모모
모모Fletcher got up and strutted back and forth across the room. He lit a Brazilian cigar, not offering any around. As fast as he blew it out, the smoke disappeared in the draft from the air conditioner. 'What time did the game break up this morning?'
모모
모모I said.
모모
모모'Around three, when I took my last big pot.'
모모
모모His mouth savored the recollection. 'I was willing to stay, but the other people weren't. Harley wanted to stay, naturally, but he didn't have the money to back it up. He isn't much of a poker player, frankly.'
모모
모모'Did he give you any trouble?'
모모
모모'No sir. The gentleman who runs the game discourages that sort of thing. No trouble. Harley did put the bite on me at the end. I gave him a hundred dollars ding money to get home.'
모모
모모'Home where?'
모모
모모'He said he came from Idaho.'
모모
모모I took a taxi back to the airport and made a reservation on a plane that stopped in Pocatello. Before sundown I was driving a rented car out of Pocatello along Rural Route Seven, where the elder Harleys lived.
모모
모모16
모모
모모THEIR FARM, GREEN and golden in the slanting light, lay in a curve of the river. I drove down a dusty lane to the farmhouse. It was built of white brick, without ornament of any kind. The barn, unpainted, was weathered gray and in poor repair.
모모
모모The late afternoon was windless. The trees surrounding the fenced yard were as still as watercolors. The heat was oppressive, in spite of the river nearby, even worse than it had been in Vegas.
모모
모모It was a far cry from Vegas to here, and difficult to believe that Harley had come home, or ever would. But the possibility had to be checked out.
모모
모모A black and white farm collie with just one eye barked at me through the yard fence when I stepped out of the car. I tried to calm him down by talking to him, but he was afraid of me and he wouldn't be calmed. Eventually an old woman wearing an apron came out of the house and silenced the dog with a word. She called to me: 'Mr. Harley's in the barn.'
모모
모모I let myself in through the wire gate. 'May I talk to you?'
모모
모모'That depends what the talk's about.'
모모
모모'Family matters.'
모모
모모'If that's another way to sell insurance, Mr. Harley doesn't believe in insurance.'
모모
모모'I'm not selling anything. Are you Mrs. Harley?'
모모
모모'I am.'
모모
모모She was a gaunt woman of seventy, square-shouldered in a long-sleeved, striped shirtwaist. Her gray hair was drawn back severely from her face. I liked her face, in spite of the broken-ness in and around the eyes. There was humor in it, and suffering half transformed into understanding.
모모
모모'Who are you?' she said.
모모
모모'A friend of your son Harold's. My name is Archer.'
모모
모모'Isn't that nice? We're going to sit down to supper as soon as Mr. Harley finishes up the milking. Why don't you stay and have some supper with us?'
모모
모모'You're very kind.'
모모
모모But I didn't want to eat with them.
모모
모모'How is Harold?' she said. 'We don't hear from him so often since he married his wife. Lila.'
모모
모모Evidently she hadn't heard the trouble her sons were in. I hesitated to tell her, and she noticed my hesitation.
모모
모모'Is something the matter with Harold?' she said sharply.
모모
모모'The matter is with Mike. Have you seen him?'
모모
모모Her large rough hands began to wipe themselves over and over on the front of her apron. 'We haven't seen Mike in twenty years. We don't expect to see him again in this life.'
모모
모모'You may, though. He told a man he was coming home.'
모모
모모'This is not his home. It hasn't been since he was a boy. He turned his back on us then. He went off to Pocatello to live with a man named Brown, and that was his downfall.'
모모
모모'How so?'
모모
모모'That daughter of Brown was a Jezebel. She ruined my son. She taught him all the filthy ways of the world.'
모모
모모Her voice had changed. It sounded as if the voice of somebody slightly crazy was ranting ventriloquially through her. I said with deliberate intent to stop it: 
모모
모모'Carol's been paid back for whatever she did to him. She was murdered in California on Monday.'
모모
모모Her hands stopped wiping themselves and flew up in front of her. She looked at their raw ugliness with her broken eyes.
모모
모모'Did Mike do it to her?'
모모
모모'We think so. We're not sure.'
모모
모모'And you're a policeman,' she stated.
모모
모모'More or less.'
모모
모모'Why do you come to us? We did our best, but we couldn't control him. He passed out of our control long ago.'
모모
모모Her hands dropped to her sides.
모모
모모'If he gets desperate enough, he may head this way.'
모모
모모'No, he never will. Mr. Harley said he would kill him if he ever set foot on our property again. That was twenty years ago, when he ran away from the Navy. Mr. Harley meant it, too. Mr. Harley never could abide a lawbreaker. It isn't true that Mr. Harley treated him cruelly. Mr. Harley was only trying to save him from the Devil.'
모모
모모The ranting, ventriloquial note had entered her voice again. Apparently she knew nothing about her son, and if she did she couldn't talk about him in realistic terms. It was beginning to look like a dry run.
모모
모모I left her and went to the barn to find her husband. He was in the stable under the barn, sitting on a milking stool with his forehead against the black and white flank of a Holstein cow. His hands were busy at her teats, and her milk surged in the pail between his knees. Its sweet fresh smell penetrated the smell of dung that hung like corruption in the heated air.
모모
모모'Mr. Harley?'
모모
모모'I'm busy,' he said morosely. 'This is the last one, if you want to wait.'
모모
모모I backed away and looked at the other cows. There were ten or twelve of them, moving uneasily in their stanchions as I moved. Somewhere out of sight a horse blew and stamped.
모모
모모'You're disturbing the livestock,' Mr. Harley said. 'Stand still if you want to stay.'
모모
모모I stood still for five minutes. The one-eyed collie drifted into the stable and did a thorough job of smelling my shoes. But he still wouldn't let me touch him. When I reached down, he moved back.
모모
모모Mr. Harley got up and emptied his pail into a ten-gallon can; the foaming milk almost overflowed. He was a tall old man wearing overalls and a straw hat which almost brushed the low rafters. His eyes were as flat and angry and his mouth as sternly righteous as in Harold's portrait of him. The dog retreated whining as he came near.
모모
모모'You're not from around here. Are you on the road?'
모모
모모'No.'
모모
모모I told him who I was. 'And I'll get to the point right away. Your son Mike's in very serious trouble.'
모모
모모'Mike is not my son,' he intoned solemnly, 'and I have no wish to hear about him or his trouble.'
모모
모모'But he may be coming here. He said he was. If he does, you'll have to inform the police.'
모모
모모'You don't have to instruct me in what I ought to do. I get my instructions from a higher power. He gives me my instructions direct in my heart.'
모모
모모He thumped his chest with a gnarled fist.
모모
모모'That must be convenient.'
모모
모모'Don't blaspheme or make mock, or you'll regret it. I can call down the punishment.'
모모
모모He reached for a pitchfork leaning against the wall. The dog ran out of the stable with his tail down. I became aware suddenly that my shirt was sticking to my back and I was intensely uncomfortable. The three tines of the pitchfork were sharp and gleaming, and they were pointed at my stomach.
모모
모모'Get out of here,' the old man said. 'I've been fighting the Devil all my life, and I know one of his cohorts when I see one.'
모모
모모So do I, I said, but not out loud. I backed as far as the door, stumbled on the high threshold, and went out. Mrs. Harley was standing near my car, just inside the wire gate. Her hands were quiet on her meager breast.
모모
모모'I'm sorry,' she said to me. 'I'm sorry for Carol Brown. She wasn't a bad little girl, but I hardened my heart against her.'
모모
모모'It doesn't matter now. She's dead.'
모모
모모'It matters in the sight of heaven.'
모모
모모She raised her eyes to the arching sky as if she imagined a literal heaven like a second story above it. Just now it was easier for me to imagine a literal hell, just over the horizon, where the sunset fires were burning.
모모
모모'I've done so many wrong things,' she said, 'and closed my eyes to so many others. But don't you see, I had to make a choice.'
모모
모모'I don't understand you.'
모모
모모'A choice between Mr. Harley and my sons. I knew that he was a hard man. A cruel man, maybe not quite right in the head. But what could I do? I had to stick with my husband. And I wasn't strong enough to stand up to him. Nobody is. I had to stand by while he drove our sons out of our home. Harold was the soft one, he forgave us in the end. But Mike never did. He's like his father. I never even got to see my grandson.'
모모
모모Tears ran in the gullies of her face. Her husband came out of the barn carrying the ten-gallon can in his left hand and the pitchfork in his right.
모모
모모'Go in the house, Martha. This man is a cohort of the Devil. I won't allow you to talk to him.'
모모
모모'Don't hurt him. Please.'
모모
모모'Go in the house,' he repeated.
모모
모모She went, with her gray head down and her feet dragging.
모모
모모'As for you, cohort,' he said, 'you get off my farm or I'll call down the punishment on you.'
모모
모모He shook his pitchfork at the reddening sky. I was already in the car and turning up the windows.
모모
모모I turned them down again as soon as I got a few hundred yards up the lane. My shirt was wet through now, and I could feel sweat running down my legs. Looking back, I caught a glimpse of the river, flowing sleek and solid in the failing light, and it refreshed me.
모모
모모17
모모
모모BEFORE DRIVING OUT to the Harley farm, I had made an evening appointment with Robert Brown and his wife. They already knew what had happened to their daughter. I didn't have to tell them.
모모
모모I found their house in the north end of the city, on a pleasant, tree-lined street parallel to Arthur Street. Night had fallen almost completely, and the street-lights were shining under the clotted masses of the trees. It was still very warm. The earth itself seemed to exude heat like a hot-blooded animal.
모모
모모Robert Brown had been watching for me. He hailed me from his front porch and came out to the curb. A big man with short hair, vigorous in his movements, he still seemed to be wading in some invisible substance, age or sorrow. We shook hands solemnly.
모모
모모He spoke with more apparent gentleness than force: 'I was planning to fly out to California tomorrow. It might have saved you a trip if you had known.'
모모
모모'I wanted to talk to the Harleys, anyway.'
모모
모모'I see.'
모모
모모He cocked his head on one side in a birdlike movement which seemed odd in such a big man. 'Did you get any sense out of them?'
모모
모모'Mrs. Harley made a good deal of sense. Harley didn't.'
모모
모모'I'm not surprised. He's a pretty good farmer, they say, but he's been in and out of the mental hospital. I took-my wife and I took care of his son Mike during one of his bouts. We took him into our home.'
모모
모모He sounded ashamed of the act.
모모
모모'That was a generous thing to do.'
모모
모모'I'm afraid it was misguided generosity. But who can prophesy the future? Anyway, it's over now. All over.'
모모
모모He forgot about me completely for a moment, then came to himself with a start. 'Come in, Mr. Archer. My wife will want to talk to you.'
모모
모모He took me into the living room. It had group and family photos on the walls, and a claustrophobic wallpaper, which lent it some of the stuffiness of an old-fashioned country parlor. The room was sedately furnished with well-cared-for maple pieces. Across the mantel marched a phalanx of sports trophies gleaming gold and silver in the harsh overhead light.
모모
모모Mrs. Brown was sitting in an armchair under the light. She was a strikingly handsome woman a few years younger than her husband, maybe fifty-five. She had chosen to disguise herself in a stiff and rather dowdy black dress. Her too precisely marcelled brown hair had specks of gray in it. Her fine eyes were confused, and surrounded by dark patches. When she gave me her hand, the gesture seemed less like a greeting than a bid for help.
모모
모모She made me sit down on a footstool near her. 'Tell us all about poor Carol, Mr. Archer.'
모모
모모All about Carol. I glanced around the safe, middle-class room, with the pictures of Carol's ancestors on the walls, and back at her parents' living faces. Where did Carol come in? I could see the source of her beauty in her mother's un-disguisable good looks. But I couldn't see how one life led to the other, or why Carol's life had ended as it had.
모모
모모Brown said: 'We know she's dead, murdered, and that Mike probably did it, and that's about all.'
모모
모모His face was like a Roman general's, a late Roman general's, after a long series of defeats by barbarian hordes.
모모
