모모'It's about all I know. Mike seems to have been using her as a decoy in an extortion attempt. You know about the Hillman boy?'
모모
모모He nodded. 'I read about it before I knew that my daughter-' His voice receded.
모모
모모'They say he may be dead, too,' his wife said.
모모
모모'He may be, Mrs. Brown.'
모모
모모'And Mike did these things? I knew he was far gone, but I didn't know he was a monster.'
모모
모모'He's not a monster,' Brown said wearily. 'He's a sick man. His father was a sick man. He still is, after all the mental hospital could do for him.'
모모
모모'If Mike was so sick, why did you bring him into this house and expose your daughter to him?'
모모
모모'She's your daughter, too.'
모모
모모'I know that. I'm not allowed to forget it. But I'm not the one that ruined her for life.'
모모
모모'You certainly had a hand in it. You were the one, for instance, who encouraged her to enter that beauty contest.'
모모
모모'She didn't win, did she?'
모모
모모'That was the trouble.'
모모
모모'Was it? The trouble was the way you felt about that Harley boy.'
모모
모모'I wanted to help him. He needed help, and he had talent.'
모모
모모'Talent?'
모모
모모'As an athlete. I thought I could develop him.'
모모
모모'You developed him all right.'
모모
모모They were talking across me, not really oblivious of me, using me as a fulcrum for leverage, or a kind of stand-in for reality. I guessed that the argument had been going on for twenty years.
모모
모모'I wanted a son,' Brown said.
모모
모모'Well, you got a son. A fine upstanding son.'
모모
모모He looked as if he was about to strike her. He didn't, though. He turned to me: 'Forgive us. We shouldn't do this. It's embarrassing.'
모모
모모His wife stared at him in unforgiving silence. I tried to think of something that would break or at least soften the tension between them: 'I didn't come here to start a quarrel.'
모모
모모'You didn't start it, let me assure you.'
모모
모모Brown snickered remorsefully. 'It started the day Carol ran off with Mike. It was something I didn't foresee-' His wife's bitter voice cut in: 'It started when she was born, Rob. You wanted a son. You didn't want a daughter. You rejected her and you rejected me.'
모모
모모'I did nothing of the sort.'
모모
모모'He doesn't remember,' she said to me. 'He has one of these convenient memories that men have. You blot out anything that doesn't suit your upright idea of yourself. My husband is a very dishonest man.'
모모
모모She had a peculiar angry gnawing smile.
모모
모모'That's nonsense,' he protested. 'I've been faithful to you all my life.'
모모
모모'Except in ways I couldn't cope with. Like when you brought the Harley boy into our home. The great altruist. The noble counselor.'
모모
모모'You have no right to jeer at me,' he said. 'I wanted to help him. I had no way of knowing that he couldn't be reached.'
모모
모모'Go on. You wanted a son any way you could get one.'
모모
모모He said stubbornly: 'You don't understand. A man gets natural pleasure from raising a boy, teaching him what he knows.'
모모
모모'All you succeeded in teaching Mike was your dishonesty.'
모모
모모He turned to me with a helpless gesture, his hands swinging out. 'She blames me for everything.'
모모
모모Walking rather aimlessly, he went out to the back part of the house.
모모
모모I felt as if I'd been left alone with a far from toothless lioness. She stirred in her chair: 'I blame myself as well for being a fool. I married a man who has the feelings of a little boy. He still gets excited about his high-school football teams. The boys adore him. Everybody adores him. They talk about him as if he was some kind of a plaster saint. And he couldn't even keep his own daughter out of trouble.'
모모
모모'You and your husband should be pulling together.'
모모
모모'It's a little late to start, isn't it?'
모모
모모Her glance came up to my face, probed at it for a moment, moved restlessly from side to side.
모모
모모'It may be that you'll kill him if you go on like this.'
모모
모모'No. He'll live to be eighty, like his father.'
모모
모모She jerked her marcelled head toward one of the pictures on the wall. Seen from varying angles, her head was such a handsome object I could hardly take my eyes off it. It was hard to believe that such a finely shaped container could be full of cold boiling trouble.
모모
모모I said, partly because I wanted to, and partly to appease her: 'You must have been a very beautiful girl.'
모모
모모'Yes. I was.'
모모
모모She seemed to take no pleasure even from her vanity. I began to suspect that she didn't relate to men. It happened sometimes to girls who were too good-looking. They were treated as beautiful objects until they felt like that and nothing more.
모모
모모'I could have married anybody,' she said, 'any man I went to college with. Some of them are bank presidents and big corporation executives now. But I had to fall in love with a football player.'
모모
모모'Your husband is a little more than that.'
모모
모모'Don't sell him to me,' she said. 'I know what he is, and I know what my life has been. I've been defrauded. I gave everything I had to marriage and motherhood, and what have I got to show for it? Do you know I never even saw my grandson?'
모모
모모Mrs. Harley had said the same thing. I didn't mention the coincidence.
모모
모모'What happened to your grandson?'
모모
모모'Carol put him out for adoption, can you imagine? Actually I know why she did it. She didn't trust her husband not to harm the baby. That's the kind of a man she married.'
모모
모모'Did she tell you this?'
모모
모모'More or less. Mike is a sadist, among other things. He used to swing cats by their tails. He lived in this house for over a year and all the time I was afraid of him. He was terribly strong, and I never was certain what he was going to do.'
모모
모모'Did he ever attack you?'
모모
모모'No. He never dared to.'
모모
모모'How old was he when he left?'
모모
모모'Let me see, Carol was fifteen at the time. That would make him seventeen or eighteen.'
모모
모모'And he left to join the Navy, is that correct?'
모모
모모'He didn't go into the Navy right away. He left town with an older man, a policeman who used to be on the local force. I forgot his name. Anyway, this man lost his position on the force through bribery, and left town, taking Mike with him. He said he was going to make a boxer out of him. They went out to the west coast. I think Mike joined the Navy a few months later. Carol could-' She stopped in dismay.
모모
모모'What about Carol?'
모모
모모'I was going to say that Carol could tell you.'
모모
모모The angry smile twisted and insulted her mouth. 'I must be losing my mind.'
모모
모모'I doubt that, Mrs. Brown. It takes time to get used to these shocks and changes.'
모모
모모'More time than I have. More time than I'll ever have.'
모모
모모She rose impatiently and went to the mantelpiece. One of the trophies standing on it was out of line with the others. She reached up and adjusted its position. 'I wonder what Rob thinks he's doing in the kitchen.'
모모
모모She didn't go and find out what he was doing. She stood in an awkward position, one hip out, in front of the empty fireplace. Under the dowdy black dress, the slopes and masses of her body were angry. But nothing that she could do with her body, or her face, could change the essential beauty of the structure. She was trapped in it, as her daughter had been.
모모
모모'I wish you'd go on with your story, Mrs. Brown.'
모모
모모'It hardly qualifies as a story.'
모모
모모'Whatever you want to call it, then. I'm very grateful for the chance to talk to you. It's the first decent chance I've had to get any information about the background of this case.'
모모
모모'The background hardly matters now, or the foreground either.'
모모
모모'It does, though. You may tell me something that will help me to find Harley. I take it you've seen him and Carol from time to time over the years.'
모모
모모'I saw him just once more-after that, I wouldn't give him house room when he came home from the Navy in the winter 1944-45. He claimed to be on leave. Actually he was absent without leave. He talked himself back into Rob's good graces. Rob had been terribly let down when he left town with that ex-policeman, the bribery artist. But my gullible husband fell for his line all over again. He even gave him money. Which Mike used to elope with my only daughter.'
모모
모모'Why did Carol go with him?'
모모
모모She scratched at her forehead, leaving faint weals in the clear skin. 'I asked her that, the last time she was home, just a couple of months ago. I asked her why she went and why she stuck with him. She didn't really know. Of course she wanted to get out of Pocatello. She hated Pocatello. She wanted to go out to the coast and break into the movies. I'm afraid my daughter had very childish dreams.'
모모
모모'Girls of fifteen do.'
모모
모모With a pang, I thought of Stella. The pang became a vaguely formed idea in an unattended area of my mind. Generation after generation had to start from scratch and learn the world over again. It changed so rapidly that children couldn't learn from their parents or parents from their children. The generations were like alien tribes islanded in time.
모모
모모'The fact is,' I said, 'Carol did make it into the movies.'
모모
모모'Really? She told me that once, but I didn't believe her.'
모모
모모'Was she a chronic liar?'
모모
모모'No. Mike was the chronic liar. I simply didn't believe that she could succeed at anything. She never had.'
모모
모모The woman's bitterness was getting me down. She seemed to have an inexhaustible reservoir of the stuff. If she had been like this twenty years before, I could understand why Carol had left home at the first opportunity, and stayed away.
모모
모모'You say you saw Carol just a couple of months ago.'
모모
모모'Yes. She rode the bus from Lake Tahoe in June. I hadn't seen her for quite a long time. She was looking pretty bedraggled. God knows what kind of a life he was leading her. She didn't talk much.'
모모
모모'It was a chancy life. Harley seems to have lost his job, and they were on their uppers.'
모모
모모'So she told me. There was the usual plea for money. I guess Rob gave her money. He always did. He tried to pretend afterwards, to me, that he gave her the car, too, but I know better. She took it. Apparently their old car had broken down, and they couldn't live at Tahoe without a car.'
모모
모모'How do you know she took it if your husband says she didn't?'
모모
모모She showed signs of embarrassment. 'It doesn't matter. They were welcome to the car.'
모모
모모It was her first generous word. She half-spoiled it: 'We needed a new one, anyway, and I'm sure she did it on the spur of the moment. Carol always was a very impulsive girl.
모모
모모'The point is,' she said, 'she left without saying goodbye. She took the car to go downtown to the movies and simply never came back. She even left her suitcase in her room.'
모모
모모'Had there been trouble?'
모모
모모'No more than the usual trouble. We did have an argument at supper.'
모모
모모'What about?'
모모
모모'My grandson. She had no right to put him out for adoption. She said he was her baby to do with as she pleased. But she had no right. If she couldn't keep him, she should have brought him to us. We could have given him opportunities, an education.'
모모
모모She breathed heavily and audibly. 'She said an unforgivable thing to me that evening. She said, did I mean the kind of opportunities she had? And she walked out. I never saw her again. Neither did her father.'
모모
모모Her head jerked forward in emphatic affirmation: 'We did give her opportunities. It's not our fault if she didn't take advantage of them. It isn't fair to blame us.'
모모
모모'You blame each other,' I said. 'You're tearing each other to pieces.
모모
모모'Don't give me that sort of talk. I've had enough of it from my husband.'
모모
모모'I'm merely calling your attention to an obvious fact. You need some kind of intermediary, a third party, to help straighten out your thinking.'
모모
모모'And you're electing yourself, are you?'
모모
모모'Far from it. You need an expert counselor.'
모모
모모'My husband is a counselor,' she said. 'What good has it done him? Anyway, I don't believe in seeking that kind of help. People should be able to handle their own problems.'
모모
모모She composed her face and sat down in the armchair again, with great calm, to show me how well she was handling hers.
모모
모모'But what if they can't, Mrs. Brown?'
모모
모모'Then they can't, that's all.'
모모
모모I made one more attempt. 'Do you go to church?'
모모
모모'Naturally I do.'
모모
모모'You could talk these problems over with your minister.'
모모
모모'What problems? I'm not aware of any outstanding problems.'
모모
모모She was in despair so deep that she wouldn't even look up toward the light. I think she was afraid it would reveal her to herself.
모모
모모I turned to other matters. 'You mentioned a suitcase that your daughter left behind. Is it still here in the house?'
모모
모모'It's up in her room. There isn't much in it. I almost threw it out with the trash, but there was always the chance that she would come back for it.'
모모
모모'May I see it?'
모모
모모'I'll go and get it.'
모모
모모'If you don't mind, I'd sooner go up to her room.'
모모
모모'I don't mind.'
모모
모모We went upstairs together, Mrs. Brown leading the way. She turned on the light in a rear bedroom and stood back to let me enter.
모모
모모The room provided the first clear evidence that she had been hit very hard by Carol's running away. It was the bedroom of a high-school girl. The flouncy yellow cover on the French provincial bed matched the yellow flounces on the dressing table, where a pair of Kewpie-doll lamps smiled vacantly at each other. A floppy cloth dog with his red felt tongue hanging out watched me from the yellow lamb's wool rug. A little bookcase, painted white like the bed, was filled with high-school texts and hospital novels and juvenile mysteries. There were college pennants tacked around the walls.
모모
모모'I kept her room as she left it,' Mrs. Brown said behind me.
모모
모모'Why?'
모모
모모'I don't know. I guess I always thought that she'd come home in the end. Well, she did a few times. The suitcase is in the closet.'
모모
모모The closet smelled faintly of sachet. It was full of skirts and dresses, the kind girls wore in high school a half-generation before. I began to suspect that the room and its contents had less to do with Carol than with some secret fantasy of her mother's. Her mother said, as if in answer to my thought: 'I spend a lot of time here in this room. I feel very close to her here. We really were quite close at one time. She used to tell me everything, all about the boys she dated and so on. It was like living my own high school days over again.'
모모
모모'Is that good?'
모모
모모'I don't know.'
모모
모모Her lips gnawed at each other. 'I guess not, because she suddenly turned against me. Suddenly she closed up completely. I didn't know what went on in her life, but I could see her changing, coarsening. She was such a pretty girl, such a pure looking girl.'
모모
모모Her mouth was wrenched far off center and it remained that way, as if the knowledge of her loss had fallen on her like a cerebral stroke.
모모
모모The suitcase was an old scuffed cowhide one with Rob Brown's initials on it. I pulled it out into the middle of the floor and opened it. Suddenly I was back in Dack's Auto Court opening Carol's other suitcase. The same sour odor of regret rose from the contents of this one and seemed to permeate the room.
모모
모모There was the same tangle of clothes, this time all of them women's skirts and dresses and under-things and stockings, a few cosmetics, a paperback book on the divination of dreams. A hand-scrawled piece of paper was stuck in this as a bookmark. I pulled it out and looked at it. It was signed 'Your Brother "Har.
모모
모모DEAR MIKE, I'm sorry you and Carole are haveing a 'tough time' and I enclose a money order for fifty which I hope will help out you have to cash it at a post-office. I would send more but things are a little 'tight' since I got married to Lila shes a good girl but does not believe that blood is thicker than water which it is. You asked me do I like bing married well in some ways I really like it in other ways I dont Lila has very strong ideas of her own. Shes no 'sinsational beauty like Carole is but we get long.
모모
모모Im sorry you lost your job Mike unskilled jobs are hard to come by in these times I know you are a good bartender and that is a skill you should be able to pick up something in that line even if they are prejudiced like you say. I did look up Mr. Sipe like you asked me to but he is in no position to do anything for anybody hes on the skids himself the Barcelona went bankrupt last winter and now old Sipe is just watchman on the place but he sent his best regards for old time sake he wanted to know if you ever developed a left.
모모
모모I saw another 'friend' of yours last week I mean Captain Hillman I know you bear a grudge there but after all he treated you pretty good he could have sent you to prison for ten years. No Im not rakeing up old recrimations because Hillman could do something for you if he wanted you ought to see the raceing yacht he has thats how I saw him went down to Newport to take some sailing pictures. I bet he has twenty-five thousand in that yacht the guy is loaded. I found out he lives with his wife and boy in Pacific Point if you want to try him for a job hes head of some kind of 'smogless industry.'
모모
모모Well thats about all for now if you deside to come out to 'sunny Cal' you know where we live and dont worry Lila will make you welcome shes a good soul 'at heart.'
모모
모모SINCERELY YOURS 
모모
모모Mrs. Brown had come out of her trance and moved toward me with a curious look. 
모모
모모'What is that?'
모모
모모'A letter to Mike from his brother Harold. May I have it?'
모모
모모'You're welcome to it.'
모모
모모'Thank you. I believe it's evidence. It seems to have started Mike thinking about the possibility of bleeding the Hillmans for money.'
모모
모모And it explained, I thought, why Harold had blamed himself for instigating the crime.
모모
모모'May I read it?'
모모
모모I handed it to her. She held it at arm's length, squinting.
모모
모모'I'm afraid I need my glasses.'
모모
모모We went downstairs to the living room, where she put on horn-rimmed reading glasses and sat in her armchair with the letter. 'Sipe,' she said when she finished reading it. 'That's the name I was trying to think of before.'
모모
모모She raised her voice and called: 'Robert! Come in here.'
모모
모모Rob Brown answered from the back of the house: 'I was just coming.'
모모
모모He appeared in the doorway carrying a clinking pitcher and three glasses on a tray. He said with a placatory look at his wife: 'I thought I'd make some fresh lemonade for the three of us. It's a warm night.'
모모
모모'That was thoughtful, Robert. Put it down on the coffee table. Now, what was the name of the ex-policeman that Mike left town with, the first time?'
모모
모모'Sipe. Otto Sipe.'
모모
모모He flushed slightly. 'That man was a bad influence, I can tell you.'
모모
모모I wondered if he still was. The question seemed so urgent that I drove right back to the airport and caught the first plane out, to Salt Lake City. A late jet from Minneapolis rescued me from a night in the Salt Lake City airport and deposited me at Los Angeles International, not many miles from the Barcelona Hotel, where a man named Sipe was watchman.
모모
모모18
모모
모모I HAD A gun in a locked desk drawer in my apartment, and one in my office. The apartment in West Los Angeles was nearer. I went there.
모모
모모It was in a fairly new, two-story building with a long roofed gallery on which the second-floor apartments opened directly. Mine was the second-floor back. I parked in the street and climbed the outside stairs.
모모
모모It was the dead dull middle of the night, the static hour when yesterday ended and tomorrow gathered its forces to begin. My own forces were running rather low, but I wasn't tired. I had slept on the planes. And my case was breaking, my beautiful terrible mess of a case was breaking.
모모
모모A light shone dimly behind my draped front window, and when I tried the door it was unlocked. I had no family, no wife, no girl. I turned the knob quietly, and slowly and tentatively opened the door.
모모
모모It seemed I had a girl after all. She was curled up on the studio couch under a blanket which came from my bed. The light from a standing lamp shone down on her sleeping face. She looked so young I felt a hundred years old.
모모
모모I closed the door. 'Hey, Stella.'
모모
모모Her body jerked under the blanket. Throwing it off, she sat up. She was wearing a blue sweater and a skirt. 'Oh,' she said. 'It's you.'
모모
모모'Who were you expecting?'
모모
모모'I don't know. But don't be cross with me. I was just dreaming something, I forget what, but it was depressing.'
모모
모모Her eyes were still dark with the dream.
모모
모모'How in the world did you get in here?'
모모
모모'The manager let me in. I told him I was a witness. He understood.'
모모
모모'I don't. A witness to what?'
모모
모모'Quite a few things,' she said with some spirit. 'If you want me to tell you, you can stop treating me like a mentally retarded delinquent. Nobody else does, except my parents.'
모모
모모I sat on the edge of the studio couch beside her. I liked the girl but at the moment she was an obstruction, and could turn into a serious embarrassment. 'Do your parents know you're here?'
모모
모모'Of course not. How could I tell them? They wouldn't have let me come, and I had to come. You ordered me to get in touch with you if I ever heard from Tommy. Your answering service couldn't find you and finally they gave me your home address.'
모모
모모'Are you telling me you've heard from him?'
모모
모모She nodded. Her eyes held steady on my face. They were brimming with complex feelings, more womanly than girlish. 'He phoned around four o'clock this afternoon. Mother was at the store, and I had a chance to answer the phone myself.'
모모
모모'Where was he, did he say?'
모모
모모'Here in-' She hesitated. 'He made me promise not to tell anyone. And I've already broken my promise once.'
모모
모모'How did you do that?'
모모
모모'I put a little note in Mr. Hillman's mailbox, before I left El Rancho. I couldn't just leave him dangling, when I knew.'
모모
모모'What did you tell him?'
모모
모모'Just that I'd heard from Tommy, and he was alive.'
모모
모모'It was a kind thing to do.'
모모
모모'But it broke my promise. He said I wasn't to tell anyone, especially not his parents.'
모모
모모'Promises have to be broken sometimes, when there are higher considerations.'
모모
모모'What do you mean?'
모모
모모'His safety. I've been afraid that Tom was dead. Are you absolutely certain you talked to him?'
모모
모모'I'm not telling a lie.'
모모
모모'I mean, you're sure it wasn't an imposter, or a tape recording?'
모모
모모'I'm sure. We talked back and forth.'
모모
모모'Where was he calling from?'
모모
모모'I don't know, but I think it was long distance.'
모모
모모'What did he say?'
모모
모모She hesitated again, with her finger raised. 'Is it all right for me to tell you, even after I promised?'
모모
모모'It would be all wrong if you didn't. You know that, don't you? You didn't come all the way here to hold it back.'
모모
모모'No.'
모모
모모She smiled a little. 'He didn't tell me too much. He didn't say a word about the kidnappers. Anyway, the fact that he's alive is the important thing. He said he was sorry I'd been worried about him, but he couldn't help it. Then he asked me to meet him and bring some money.'
모모
모모I was relieved. Tom's need for money implied that he had no part of the payoff. 'How much money?'
모모
모모'As much as I could get hold of in a hurry. He knew it wouldn't amount to a great deal. I borrowed some from the people at the beach club. The secretary of the club gave me a hundred dollars of her own money-she knows I'm honest. I took a taxi to the bus station. You know, I never rode on a bus before, except the school bus.'
모모
모모I cut in impatiently: 'Did you meet him here in Los Angeles?'
모모
모모'No. I was supposed to meet him in the Santa Monica bus station at nine o'clock. The bus was a few minutes late, and I may have missed him. He did say on the phone that he mightn't be able to make it tonight. In which case I was to meet him tomorrow night. He said he generally only goes out at night.'
모모
모모'Did he tell you where he's staying?'
모모
모모'No. That's the trouble. I hung around the bus station for about an hour and then I tried to phone you and when I couldn't I took a taxi here. I had to spend the night somewhere.'
모모
모모'So you did. It's too bad Tom didn't think of that.'
모모
모모'He probably has other things on his mind,' she said in a defensive tone. 'He's been having a terrible time.'
모모
모모'Did he say so?'
모모
모모'I could tell by the way he talked to me. He sounded-I don't know-so upset.'
모모
모모'Emotionally upset, or just plain scared?'
모모
모모Her brow knit. 'More worried than scared. But he wouldn't say what about. He wouldn't tell me anything that happened. I asked him if he was okay, you know, physically okay, and he said he was. So I asked him why he didn't come home. He said on account of his parents, only he didn't call them his parents. He called them his anti-parents. He said they could probably hardly wait to put him back in Laguna Perdida School.'
모모
모모Her eyes were very dark. 'I remember now what I was dreaming before you woke me up. Tommy was in that school and they wouldn't let him out and they wouldn't let me see him. I went around to all the doors and windows, trying to get in. All I could see was the terrible faces leering at me through the windows.'
모모
모모'The faces aren't so terrible. I was there.'
모모
모모'Yes, but you weren't locked up there. Tommy says it's a terrible place. His parents had no right to put him there. I don't blame him for staying away.'
모모
모모'Neither do I, Stella. But, under the circumstances, he has to be brought in. You understand that, don't you?'
모모
모모'I guess I do.'
모모
모모'It would be a rotten anticlimax if something happened to him now. You don't want that.'
모모
모모She shook her head.
모모
모모'Then will you help me get him?'
모모
모모'It's why I came here, really. I couldn't sic the police on him. But you're different.'
모모
모모She touched the back of my hand. 'You won't let them put him back in Laguna Perdida.'
모모
모모'It won't happen if I can possibly help it. I think I can. If Tom needs treatment, he should be able to get it as an outpatient.'
모모
모모'He isn't sick!'
모모
모모'His father must have had a reason for putting him there. Something happened that Sunday, he wouldn't tell me what.'
모모
모모'It happened long before that Sunday,' she said. 'His father turned against him, that's what happened. Tommy isn't the hairy-chested type, and he preferred music to trap-shooting and sailing and such things. So his father turned against him. It's as simple as that.'
모모
모모'Nothing ever is, but we won't argue. If you'll excuse me for a minute, Stella, I have to make a phone call.'
모모
모모The phone was on the desk under the window. I sat down there and dialed Susanna Drew's unlisted number. She answered on the first ring.
모모
모모'Hello.'
모모
모모'Lew Archer. You sound very alert for three o'clock in the morning.
모모
모모'I've been lying awake thinking, about you among other things and people. Somebody said-I think it was Scott Fitzgerald-something to the effect that in the real dark night of the soul it's always three o'clock in the morning. I have a reverse twist on that. At three o'clock in the morning it's always the real dark night of the soul.'
모모
모모'The thought of me depresses you?'
모모
모모'In certain contexts it does. In others, not.'
모모
모모'You're talking in riddles, Sphinx.'
모모
모모'I mean to be, Oedipus. But you're not the source of my depression. That goes back a long way.'
모모
모모'Do you want to tell me about it?'
모모
모모'Another time, doctor.'
모모
모모Her footwork was very skittish. 'You didn't call me at this hour for snatches of autobiography.'
모모
모모'No, though I'd still like to know who that telephone call was from the other day.'
모모
모모'And that's why you called me?'
모모
모모There was disappointment in her voice, ready to turn into anger.
모모
모모'It isn't why I called you. I need your help.'
모모
모모'Really?'
모모
모모She sounded surprised, and rather pleased. But she said guardedly: 'You mean by telling you all I know and like that?'
모모
모모'We don't have time. I think this case is breaking. Anyway I have to make a move, now. A very nice high-school girl named Stella has turned up on my doorstep.'
모모
모모I was speaking to the girl in the room as well as to the woman on the line; as I did so, I realized that they were rapidly becoming my favorite girl and woman. 'I need a safe place to keep her for the rest of the night.'
모모
모모'I'm not that safe.'
모모
모모A rough note in her voice suggested that she meant it.
모모
모모Stella said quickly behind me: 'I could stay here.'
모모
모모'She can't stay here. Her parents would probably try to hang a child-stealing rap on me.'
모모
모모'Are you serious?'
모모
모모'The situation is serious, yes.'
모모
모모'All right. Where do you live?'
모모
모모'Stella and I will come there. We're less than half an hour from you at this time of night.'
모모
모모Stella said when I hung up: 'You didn't have to do it behind my back.'
모모
모모'I did it right in front of your face. And I don't have time to argue.'
모모
모모To underline the urgency I took off my jacket, got my gun and its harness out of the drawer, and put it on in front of her. She watched me with wide eyes. The ugly ritual didn't quite silence her.
모모
모모'But I didn't want to meet anybody tonight.'
모모
모모'You'll like Susanna Drew. She's very stylish and hep.'
모모
모모'But I never do like people when adults tell me I will.'
모모
모모After the big effort of the night, she was relapsing into childishness. I said, to buck her up: 'Forget your war with the adults. You're going to be an adult pretty soon yourself. Then who will you have to blame for everything?'
모모
모모'That isn't fair.'
모모
모모It wasn't, but it held her all the way to the apartment house on Beverly Glen. Susanna came to the door in silk pajamas, not the kind anyone slept in. Her hair was brushed. She hadn't bothered with makeup. Her face was extraordinarily and nakedly handsome, with eyes as real and dark as any night.
모모
모모'Come in, Lew. It's nice to see you, Stella. I'm Susanna. I have a bed made up for you upstairs.'
모모
모모She indicated the indoor balcony which hung halfway up the wall of the big central studio, and on which an upstairs room opened. 'Do you want something to eat?'
모모
모모'No, thank you,' Stella said. 'I had a hamburger at the bus station.'
모모
모모'I'll be glad to make you a sandwich.'
모모
모모'No. Really. I'm not hungry.'
모모
모모The girl looked pale and a little sick.
모모
모모'Would you like to go to bed then?'
모모
모모'I have no choice.'
모모
모모Stella heard herself, and added: 'That was rude, wasn't it? I didn't mean it to be. It's awfully kind of you to take me in. It was Mr. Archer who gave me no choice.'
모모
모모'I had no choice, either,' I said. 'What would you do if you had one?'
모모
모모'I'd be with Tommy, wherever he is.'
모모
모모Her mouth began to work, and so did the delicate flesh around her eyes and mouth. The mask of a crying child seemed to be struggling for possession of her face. She ran away from it, or from our eyes, up the circular stairs to the balcony.
모모
모모Susanna called after her before she closed the door. 'Pajamas on the bed, new toothbrush in the bathroom.'
모모
모모'You're an efficient hostess,' I said.
모모
모모'Thank you. Have a drink before you go.'
모모
모모'It wouldn't do anything for me.'
모모
모모'Do you want to go into where you're going and what you have to do?'
모모
모모'I'm on my way to the Barcelona Hotel, but I keep running into detours.'
모모
모모She reacted more sharply than she had any apparent reason to. 'Is that what I am, a detour?'
모모
모모'Stella was the detour. You're the United States Cavalry.'
모모
모모'I love your imagery.'
모모
모모She made a face. 'What on earth are you planning to do at the old Barcelona? Isn't it closed down?'
모모
모모'There's at least one man living there, a watchman who used to be the hotel detective, named Otto Sipe.'
모모
모모'Good Lord, I think I know him. Is he a big red-faced character with a whisky breath?'
모모
모모'That's probably the man. How do you happen to know him?'
모모
모모She hesitated before she answered, in a careful voice: 'I sort of frequented the Barcelona at one time, way back at the end of the war. That was where I met Carol.'
모모
모모'And Mr. Sipe.'
모모
모모'And Mr. Sipe.'
모모
모모She wouldn't tell me any more.
모모
모모'You have no right to cross-question me,' she said finally. 'Leave me alone.'
모모
모모'I'll be glad to.'
모모
모모She followed me to the door. 'Don't leave on that note. Please.
모모
모모I'm not holding back for the fun of it. Why do you think I've been lying awake all night?'
모모
모모'Guilt?'
모모
모모'Nonsense. I'm not ashamed of anything.'
모모
모모But there was shame in her eyes, deeper than her knowledge of herself. 'Anyway, the little I know can't be of any importance.'
모모
모모'You're not being fair. You're trying to use my personal feeling for you-' 'I didn't know it existed. If it does, I ought to have a right to use it any way I need to.'
모모
모모'You don't have that right, though. My privacy is a very precious thing to me, and you have no right to violate it.'
모모
모모'Even to save a life?'
모모
모모Stella opened her door and came out on the balcony. She looked like a young, pajamaed saint in a very large niche.
모모
모모'If you adults,' she said, 'will lower your voices a few decibels, it might be possible to get a little sleep.'
모모
모모'Sorry,' I said to both of them.
모모
모모Stella retreated. Susanna said: 'Whose life is in danger, Lew?'
모모
모모'Tom Hillman's for one. Possibly others, including mine.'
모모
모모She touched the front of my jacket. 'You're wearing a shoulder holster. Is Otto Sipe one of the kidnappers?'
모모
모모I countered with a question: 'Was he a man in your life?'
모모
모모She was offended. 'Of course not. Go away now.'
모모
모모She pushed me out. 'Take care.'
모모
모모The night air was chilly on my face.
모모
모모19
모모
모모TRAFFIC WAS SPARSE on the coastal highway. Occasional night-crawling trucks went by, blazing with red and yellow lights. This stretch of highway was an ugly oil-stained place, fouled by petroleum fumes and rubbed barren by tires. Even the sea below it had a used-dishwater odor.
모모
모모Ben Daly's service station was dark, except for an inside bulb left on to discourage burglars. I left my car in his lot, beside an outside telephone booth, and crossed the highway to the Barcelona Hotel.
모모
모모It was as dead as Nineveh. In the gardens behind the main building a mockingbird tried a few throbbing notes, like a tiny heart of sound attempting to beat, and then subsided. The intermittent mechanical movement of the highway was the only life in the inert black night.
모모
모모I went up to the front door where the bankruptcy notice was posted and knocked on the glass with my flashlight. I knocked repeatedly, and got no answer. I was about to punch out a pane of glass and let myself in. Then I noticed that the door was unlocked. It opened under my hand.
모모
모모I entered the lobby, jostling a couple of ghosts. They were Susanna, twenty years old, and a man without a face. I told them to get the hell out of my way.
모모
모모I went down the corridor where Mr. Sipe had first appeared with his light, past the closed, numbered doors, to a door at the end which was standing slightly ajar. I could hear breathing inside the dark room, the heavy sighing breathing of a man in sleep or stupor. The odor of whisky was strong.
모모
모모I reached inside the door and found the light switch with my right hand. I turned it on and shifted my hand to my gun butt. There was no need. Sipe was lying on the bed, fully clothed, with his ugly nostrils glaring and his loose mouth sighing at the ceiling. He was alone.
모모
모모There was hardly space for anyone else. The room had never been large, and it was jammed with stuff which looked as if it had been accumulating for decades. Cartons and packing cases, piles of rugs, magazines and newspapers, suitcases and footlockers, were heaped at the back of the room almost to the ceiling. On the visible parts of the walls were pictures of young men in boxing stance, interspersed with a few girlie pictures.
모모
모모Empty whisky bottles were ranged along the wall beside the door. A half-full bottle stood by the bed where Sipe was lying. I turned the key that was in the lock of the door and took a closer look at the sleeping man.
모모
모모He wasn't just sleeping. He was out, far out and possibly far gone. If I had put a match to his lips, his breath would have ignited like an alcohol burner. Even the front of his shirt seemed to be saturated with whisky, as though he'd poured it over himself in one last wild libation before he passed out.
모모
모모His gun was stuck in the greasy waistband of his trousers. I transferred it to my jacket pocket before I tried to rouse him. He wouldn't wake up. I shook him. He was inert as a side of beef, and his big head rolled loosely on the pillow. I slapped his pitted red cheeks. He didn't even groan.
모모
모모I went into the adjoining bathroom-it was also a kind of kitchen fitted out with an electric plate and a percolator that smelled of burned coffee, and filled the percolator with cold water from the bathtub faucet. This I poured over Sipe's head and face, being careful not to drown him. He didn't wake up.
모모
모모I was getting a little worried, not so much about Sipe as about the possibility that he might never be able to give me his story. There was no way of telling how many of the bottles in the room had been emptied recently. I felt his pulse: laboriously slow. I lifted one of his eyelids. It was like looking down into a red oyster.
모모
모모I had noticed that the bathroom was one of those with two doors, serving two rooms, that you find in older hotels. I went through it into the adjoining bedroom and shone my light around. It was a room similar in shape and size to the other, but almost bare. A brass double bed with a single blanket covering the mattress was just about the only furniture. The blanket lay in the tumbled folds that a man, or a boy, leaves behind when he gets up.
모모
모모Hung over the head of the bed, like a limp truncated shadow of a boy, was a black sweater. It was a knitted sweater, and it had a raveled sleeve. Where the yarn was snarled and broken I could see traces of light-colored grease, the kind they use on the locks of automobile trunks. In the wastebasket I found several cardboard baskets containing the remains of hamburgers and french frieds.
모모
모모My heart was beating in my ears. The sweater was pretty good physical evidence that Stella had not been conned. Tom was alive.
모모
모모I found Sipe's keys and locked him in his room and went through every other room in the building. There were nearly a hundred guest and service rooms, and it took a long time. I felt like an archaeologist exploring the interior of a pyramid. The Barcelona's palmy days seemed that long ago.
모모
모모All I got for my efforts was a noseful of dust. If Tom was in the building, he was hiding. I had a feeling that he wasn't there, that he had left the Barcelona for good. Anybody would if he had the chance.
모모
모모I went back across the highway to Daly's station. My flashlight found a notice pasted to the lower right-hand corner of the front door. 'In case of emergency call owner,' with Daly's home number. I called it from the outside booth, and after a while got an answer: 'Daly here.'
모모
모모'Lew Archer. I'm the detective who was looking for Harold Harley.'
모모
모모'This is a heck of a time to be looking for anybody.'
모모
모모'I found Harley, thanks to you. Now I need your help in some more important business.'
모모
모모'What's the business?'
모모
모모'I'll tell you when you get here. I'm at your station.'
모모
모모Daly had the habit of serviceability. 'Okay. I'll be there in fifteen minutes.'
모모
모모I waited for him in my car, trying to put the case together in my mind. It was fairly clear that Sipe and Mike Harley had been working together, and had used the Barcelona as a hideout. It didn't look as if Tom had been a prisoner; more likely a willing guest, as Harley had said from the start. Even with Laguna Perdida School in the background, it was hard to figure out why a boy would do this to his parents and himself.
모모
모모Daly came off the highway with a flourish and parked his pickup beside me. He got out and slammed the door, which had his name on it. He gave me a frowzy sardonic pre-dawn look.
모모
모모'What's on your mind, Mr. Archer?'
모모
모모'Get in. I'll show you a picture.'
모모
모모He climbed in beside me. I turned on the dome light and got out Tom's photograph. Every time I looked at it it had changed, gathering ambiguities on the mouth and in the eyes.
모모
모모I put it in Daly's oil-grained hands. 'Have you seen him?'
모모
모모'Yeah. I have. I saw him two or three times over the last couple of days. He made some telephone calls from the booth there. He made one yesterday afternoon.'
모모
모모'What time?'
모모
모모'I didn't notice, I was busy. It was along toward the end of the afternoon. Then I saw him again last night waiting for the bus.'
모모
모모He pointed down the road toward Santa Monica. 'The bus stops at the intersection if you flag it down. Otherwise it don't.'
모모
모모'Which bus is that?'
모모
모모'Any of the intercity buses, expecting the express ones.'
모모
모모'Did you see him get on a bus?'
모모
모모'No. I was getting ready to close up. Next time I looked he was gone.'
모모
모모'What time was this?'
모모
모모'Around eight-thirty last night.'
모모
모모'What was he wearing?'
모모
모모'White shirt, dark slacks.'
모모
모모'What made you interested enough to watch him?'
모모
모모Daly fidgeted. 'I dunno. I didn't watch him exactly. I saw him come out of the grounds of the Barcelona and I wondered what he was doing there, naturally. I'd hate to see such a nice-looking boy mixed up with a man like Sipe.'
모모
모모He glanced at the photograph and handed it back to me, as if to relieve himself of the responsibility of explaining Tom.
모모
모모'What's the matter with Sipe?'
모모
모모'What isn't? I've got boys of my own, and I hate to see a man like Sipe teaching the boys to drink and other things. He ought to be in jail, if you want my opinion.'
모모
모모'I agree. Let's put him there.'
모모
모모'You're kidding.'
모모
모모'I'm serious, Ben. Right now Sipe is in his hotel room, passed out. He probably won't wake up for a long time. Just in case he does, will you stay here and watch for him to come out?'
모모
모모'What do I do if he comes out?'
모모
모모'Call the police and tell them to arrest him.'
모모
모모'I can't do that,' he said uneasily. 'I know he's a bad actor, but I got nothing definite to go on.'
모모
모모'I have. If you're forced to call the police, tell them Sipe is wanted in Pacific Point on suspicion of kidnapping. But don't call them unless you have to. Sipe is my best witness, and once he's arrested I'll never see him again.'
모모
모모'Where are you going?'
모모
모모'To see if I can trace the boy.'
모모
모모His eyes brightened. 'Is he the one that's been in all the papers? What's his name? Hillman?'
모모
모모'He's the one.'
모모
모모'I should have recognized him. I dunno, I don't pay too much attention to people's faces. But I can tell you what kind of a car they drive.'
모모
모모'Does Sipe have a car?'
모모
모모'Yeah. It's a '53 Ford with a cracked engine. I put some goop in it for him, but it's due to die any day.'
모모
모모Before I left, I asked Daly if he had seen anyone else around the hotel. He had, and he remembered. Mike Harley had been there Monday morning, driving the car with the Idaho license. I guessed that Tom had been riding in the trunk.
모모
모모'And just last night,' he said, 'there was this other young fellow driving a brand-new Chevvy. I think he had a girl with him, or maybe a smaller fellow. I was just closed up, and my bright lights were off.'
모모
모모'Did you get a good look at the driver?'
모모
모모'Not so good, no. I think he was dark-haired, a nice-looking boy. What he was doing with that crumb-bum-.' Shaking his head some more, Ben started to get out of my car. He froze in mid-action: 'Come to think of it, what's the Hillman boy been doing walking around? I thought he was a prisoner and everybody in Southern California was looking for him.'
모모
모모'We are.'
모모
모모It took me a couple of hours, with the help of several bus company employees, to sort out the driver who had picked Tom up last night. His name was Albertson and he lived far out on La Cienaga in an apartment over a bakery. The sweet yeasty smell of freshly made bread permeated his small front room.
모모
모모It was still very early in the morning. Albertson had pulled on trousers over his pajamas. He was a square-shouldered man of about forty, with alert eyes. He nodded briskly over the picture: 'Yessir. I remember him. He got on my bus at the Barcelona intersection and bought a ticket into Santa Monica. He didn't get off at Santa Monica, though.'
모모
모모'Why not?'
모모
모모He rubbed his heavily bearded chin. The sound rasped on my nerves. 'Would he be wanted for something?' Albertson said.
모모
모모'He would.'
모모
모모'That's what I thought at the time. He started to get off and then he saw somebody inside the station and the kid went back to his seat. I got off for a rest stop and it turned out there was a cop inside. When I came back the boy was still on the bus. I told him this was as far as his fare would take him. So he asked me to sell him a ticket to LA I was all set to go and I didn't make an issue. If the kid was in trouble, it wasn't up to me to turn him in. I've been in trouble myself. Did I do wrong?'
모모
모모'You'll find out on judgment Day.'
모모
모모He smiled. 'That's a long time to wait. What's the pitch on the kid?'
모모
모모'Read it in the papers, Mr. Albertson. Did he ride all the way downtown?'
모모
모모'Yeah. I'm sure he did. He was one of the last ones to get off the bus.'
모모
모모I went downtown and did some bird-dogging in and around the bus station. Nobody remembered seeing the boy. Of course the wrong people were on duty at this time in the morning. I'd stand a better chance if I tried again in the evening. And it was time I got back to Otto Sipe.
모모
모모Ben Daly said he hadn't come out of the hotel. But when we went to Sipe's room the door was standing open and he was gone before he left he had finished the bottle of whisky by his bed.
모모
모모'He must have had a master key, Ben. Is there any way out of here except the front?'
모모
모모'No sir. He has to be on the grounds some place.'
모모
모모We went around to the back of the sprawling building, past a dry swimming pool with a drift of brown leaves in the deep end Under the raw bluff which rose a couple of hundred feet behind the hotel were the employees' dormitories, garages, and other outbuildings. The two rear wings of the hotel contained a forma garden whose clipped shrubs and box hedges were growing back into natural shapes. Swaying on the topmost spray of a blue plumbago bush, a mockingbird was scolding like a jay.
모모
모모I stood still and made a silencing gesture to Daly. Someone was digging on the far side of the bush. I could see some of his movements and hear the scrape of the spade, the thump of earth. I took out the gun and showed myself.
모모
모모Otto Sipe looked up from his work. He was standing in a shallow hole about five feet long and two feet wide. There was dirt on his clothes. His face was muddy with sweat.
모모
모모In the grass beside the hole a man in a gray jacket was lying on his back. The striped handle of a knife protruded from his chest. The man looked like Mike Harley, and he lay as if the knife had nailed him permanently to the earth.
모모
모모'What are you doing, Otto?'
모모
모모'Planting petunias.'
모모
모모He bared his teeth in a doggish grin. The man seemed to be in that detached state of drunkenness where everything appears surreal or funny.
모모
모모'Planting dead men, you mean.'
모모

모모
모모'You know who he is. You and Mike have been buddies ever since he left Pocatello with you in the early forties.'
모모
모모'All right, I got a right to give a buddy a decent burial. You just can't leave them lying around in the open for the vultures.'
모모
모모'The only vultures I see around here are human ones. Did you kill him?'
모모
모모'Naw. Why would I kill my buddy?'
모모
모모'Who did?'
모모

모모

모모
모모'I'm gonna save my talk for when it counts.'

모모
모모'Hell no, I was only at Guadal.'
모모
모모'Hold this on him.'
모모
모모I handed him my revolver and went to look at Harley. His face when I touched it was cold as the night had been. This and the advanced coagulation of the blood that stained his shirt front told me he had been dead for many hours, probably all night.
모모
모모I didn't try to pull the knife out of his ribs. I examined it closely without touching it. The handle was padded with rubber, striped black and white, and molded to fit the hand. It looked new and fairly expensive.
모모
모모The knife was the only thing of any value that had attached itself to Mike Harley. I went through his pockets and found the stub of a Las Vegas to Los Angeles plane ticket issued the day before, and three dollars and forty-two cents.
모모
모모Ben Daly let out a yell. Several things happened at once. At the edge of my vision metal flashed and the mockingbird flew up out of the bush. The gun went off. A gash opened in the side of Daly's head where Otto Sipe had hit him with the spade. Otto Sipe's face became contorted. He clutched at his abdomen and fell forward, with the lower part of his body in the grave.
모모
모모Ben Daly said: 'I didn't mean to shoot him. The gun went off when he swung the spade at me. After the war I never wanted to shoot anything.'
모모
모모The gash in the side of his head was beginning to bleed. I tied my handkerchief around it and told him to go and call the police and an ambulance. He ran. He was surprisingly light on his feet for a man of middle age.
모모
모모I was feeling surprisingly heavy on mine. I went to Sipe and turned him onto his back and opened his clothes. The wound in his belly was just below the umbilicus. It wasn't bleeding much, externally, but he must have been bleeding inside. The life was draining visibly from his face.
모모
모모It was Archer I mourned for. It had been a hard three days. All I had to show for them was a dead man and a man who was probably dying. The fact that the bullet in Sipe had come from my gun made it worse.
모모
모모Compunction didn't prevent me from going through Sipe's pockets. His wallet was fat with bills, all of them twenties. But his share of the Hillman payoff wasn't going to do him any good. He was dead before the ambulance came shrieking down the highway.

모모'It's all right. You've been the best help I've had.'
모모
모모Jay Carlson, whom I hadn't met and wasn't looking forward to meeting, was standing out in front of his house when we got there. He was a well-fed, youngish man with sensitive blue eyes resembling Stella's. At the moment he looked sick with anger, gray and shuddering with it.
모모
모모Rhea Carlson, her red hair flaring like a danger signal, came out of the house and rushed up to the car, with her husband trudging behind her. He acted like a man who disliked trouble and couldn't handle it well. The woman spoke first: 'What have you been doing with my daughter?'
모모
모모'Protecting her as well as I could. She spent the night with a woman friend of mine. This morning I talked her into coming home.'
모모
모모'I intend to check that story very carefully,' Carlson said. 'What was the name of this alleged woman friend?'
모모
모모'Susanna Drew.'
모모
모모'Is he telling the truth, Stella?' She nodded.
모모
모모'Can't you talk?' he cried. 'You've been gone all night and you won't even speak to us.'
모모
모모'Don't get so excited, Daddy. He's telling the truth. I'm sorry I went to Los Angeles but-' 
모모
모모He couldn't wait for her to finish. 'I've got a right to get excited, after what you've done. We didn't even know if you were alive.'
모모
모모Stella bowed her head. 'I'm sorry, Daddy.'
모모
모모'You're a cruel, unfeeling girl,' her mother said. 'And I'll never be able to believe you again. Never.'
모모
모모'You know better than that, Mrs. Carlson.'
모모
모모Her husband turned on me fiercely. 'You stay out of it.'
모모
모모He probably wanted to hit me. In lieu of this, he grasped Stella by the shoulders and shook her. 'Are you out of your mind, to do a thing like this?'
모모
모모'Lay off her, Carlson.'
모모
모모'She's my daughter!'
모모
모모'Treat her like one. Stella's had a rough night-' 
모모
모모'She's had a rough night, has she? What happened?'
모모
모모'She's been trying to grow up, under difficulties, and you're not giving her much help.'
모모
모모'What she needs is discipline. And I know where she can get it.'
모모
모모'If you're thinking of Laguna Perdida, your thinking is way out of line. Stella is one of the good ones, one of the best-' 
모모
모모'I'm not interested in your opinion. I suggest you get off my place before I call the police.'
모모
모모I left them together, three well-intentioned people who couldn't seem to stop hurting each other. Stella had the courage to lift her hand to me in farewell.
모모
모모21
모모
모모I WENT NEXT door to the Hillmans. Turning in past their mailbox, I heard the noise of a sports car coming down the driveway. I stopped in the middle of the narrow blacktop so that Dick Leandro had to stop, too.
모모
모모He sat there looking at me rather sulkily from under his hair, as if I'd halted him in the middle of a Grand Prix. I got out and walked over to the side of his car and patted the hood.
모모
모모'Nice car.'
모모
모모'I like it.'
모모
모모'You have any other cars?'
모모
모모'Just this one,' he said. 'Listen, I hear they f-found Tom, is that the true word?'
모모
모모'He hasn't been found yet, but he's running free.'
모모
모모'Hey, that's great,' he said without enthusiasm. 'Listen, do you know where Skipper is? Mrs. Hillman says he hasn't been home all night.'
모모
모모He looked up at me with puzzled anxiety.
모모
모모'I wouldn't worry about him. He can look after himself.'
모모
모모'Yeah, sure, but do you know where he is? I want to ta-talk to him.'
모모
모모'What about?'
모모
모모'That's between him and I. It's a personal matter.'
모모
모모I said unpleasantly: 'Do you and Mr. Hillman share a lot of secrets?'
모모
모모'I w-wouldn't say that. He advises me. He gives me g-good advice.'
모모
모모The young man was almost babbling with fear and hostility. I let him go and drove up to the house. Elaine Hillman was the one I wanted to see, and she let me in herself.
모모
모모She looked better than she had the last time I'd seen her. She was well groomed and well dressed, in a tailored sharkskin suit which concealed the shrinkage of her body. She was even able to smile at me.
모모
모모'I got your good news, Mr. Archer.'
모모
모모'Good news?' I couldn't think of any.
모모
모모'That Tom is definitely alive. Lieutenant Bastian passed the word to me. Come in and tell me more.'
모모
모모She led me across the reception hall, making a detour to avoid the area under the chandelier, and into the sitting room. She said almost brightly, as if she was determined to be cheerful: 'I call this the waiting room. It's like a dentist's waiting room. But the waiting is almost over, don't you think?'
모모
모모Her voice curled up thinly at the end, betraying her tension.
모모
모모'Yes. I really think so.'
모모
모모'Good. I couldn't stand much more of this. None of us could. These days have been very difficult.'
모모
모모'I know. I'm sorry.'
모모
모모'Don't be sorry. You've brought us good news.'
모모
모모She perched on the chesterfield. 'Now sit down and tell me the rest of it.'
모모
모모I sat beside her. 'There isn't much more, and not all of it is good. But Tom is alive, and free, and very likely still in Los Angeles. I traced him from the Barcelona Hotel, where he was hiding, to downtown Los Angeles. He was seen getting off a bus in the main station around ten o'clock last night. I'm going back there this afternoon to see if I can find him.'
모모
모모'I wish my husband was here to share this,' she said. 'I'm a little worried about him. He left the house early last evening and hasn't been back since.'
모모
모모She looked around the room as if it felt strange without him.
모모
모모I said: 'He probably got word that Tom was alive.'
모모
모모'From whom?'
모모
모모I left the question unanswered.
모모
모모'But he wouldn't go without telling me.'
모모
모모'Not unless he had a reason.'
모모
모모'What possible reason could he have for keeping me in the dark?'
모모
모모'I don't know, Mrs. Hillman.'
모모
모모'Is he going out of his mind, do you think?'
모모
모모'I doubt it. He probably spent the night in Los Angeles searching for Tom. I know he had breakfast this morning with Susanna Drew.'
모모
모모I'd dropped the name deliberately, without preparation, and got the reaction I was looking for. Elaine's delicate blonde face crumpled like tissue paper. 'Good Lord,' she said, 'is that still going on? Even in the midst of these horrors?'
모모
모모'I don't know exactly what is going on.'
모모
모모'They're lovers,' she said bitterly, 'for twenty years. He swore to me it was over long ago. He begged me to stay with him, and gave me his word of honor that he would never go near her again. But he has no honor.'
모모
모모She raised her eyes to mine. 'My husband is a man without honor.'
모모
모모'He didn't strike me that way.'
모모
모모'Perhaps men can trust him. I know a woman can't. I'm rather an expert on the subject. I've been married to him for over twenty-five years. It wasn't loyalty that kept him with me. I know that. It was my family's money, which has been useful to him in his business, and in his hobbies. Including,' she added in a disgusted tone, 'his dirty little bed-hopping hobby.'
모모
모모She covered her mouth with her hand, as if to hide the anguish twisting it. 'I shouldn't be talking this way. It isn't like me. It's very much against my New England grain. My mother, who had a similar problem with my father, taught me by precept and example always to sulkier in silence. And I have. Except for Ralph himself, you're the only person I've spoken to about it.'
모모
모모'You haven't told me much. It might be a good idea to ventilate it.'
모모
모모'Do you believe it may be connected in some way with-all this?'
모모
모모She flung out her arm, with the fingers spread at the end of it.
모모
모모'Very likely it is. I think that's why your husband and Miss Drew got together this morning. He probably phoned her early in the week. Tuesday afternoon.'
모모
모모'He did! I remember now. He was phoning from the bar, and I came into the room. He cut it short. But I heard him say something to the effect that they must absolutely keep quiet. It must have been that Drew woman he was talking to.'
모모
모모The scornful phrase made me wince. It was a painful, strange colloquy, but we were both engrossed in it. The intimacy of the people we were talking about forced intimacy on us.
모모
모모'It probably was her,' I said. 'I'd just told Lieutenant Bastian that she was a witness, and Bastian must have passed it on to your husband.'
모모
모모'You're right again, Mr. Archer. My husband had just heard from the lieutenant. How can you possibly know so much about the details of other people's lives?'
모모
모모'Other people's lives are my business.'
모모
모모'And your passion?'
모모
모모'And my passion. And my obsession, too, I guess. I've never been able to see much in the world besides the people in it.'
모모
모모'But how could you possibly find out about that phone call? You weren't here. My husband wouldn't tell you.'
모모
모모'I was in Miss Drew's apartment when the call came. I didn't hear what was said, but it shook her up.'
모모
모모'I hope so.'
모모
모모She glanced at my face, and her eyes softened. She reached out and touched my arm with gentle fingers. 'She isn't a friend of yours?'
모모
모모'She is, in a way.'
모모
모모'You're not in love with her?'
모모
모모'Not if I can help it.'
모모
모모'That's a puzzling answer.'
모모
모모'It puzzles me, too. If she's still in love with your husband it would tend to chill one's interest. But I don't think she is.'
모모
모모'Then what are they trying to conceal?'
모모
모모'Something in the past.'
모모
모모I hoped it was entirely in the past. Susanna, I had learned in the course of the morning, could still hurt me where I lived. 
모모
모모'It would help if you'd go into it a little deeper. I know it will also hurt,' I said to myself and her.
모모
모모'I can stand pain if there's any purpose in it. It's the meaningless pain I can't stand. The pain for Tom, for instance.'
모모
모모She didn't explain what she meant, but she touched her blue-veined temple with her fingertips.
모모
모모'I'll try to make it short, Mrs. Hillman. You said the affair has been going on for twenty years. That would take it back to around the end of the war.'
모모
모모'Yes. The spring of 1945. I was living alone, or rather with a woman companion, in a house in Brentwood. My husband was in the Navy. He had been a squadron commander, but at the time I'm talking about he was executive officer of an escort carrier. Later they made him captain of the same ship.'
모모
모모She spoke with a kind of forlorn pride, and very carefully, as if the precise facts of the past were all she had to hold on to.
모모
모모'In January or February of 1945 my husband's ship was damaged by a kamikaze plane. They had to bring it back to San Diego for repairs. Ralph had some days of leave, of course, and of course he visited me. But I didn't see as much of him as I wanted to, or expected to. I found out later why. He was spending some of his nights, whole weekends, with Susanna Drew.'
모모
모모'In the Barcelona Hotel?'
모모
모모'Did she tell you?'
모모
모모'In a way.'
모모
모모She had given me Harold Harley's picture of Carol, and the printing on the back of the picture had sent me to the Barcelona Hotel. 'About herself she told me, not about your husband. She's a loyal girl, anyway.'
모모
모모'I don't want to hear her praised. She's caused me too much suffering.'
모모
모모'I'm sorry. But she was only twenty, remember.'
모모
모모'She's closer to forty now. The fact that she was twenty then only made it worse. I was still in my twenties myself, but my husband had already discarded me. Do you have any idea how a woman feels when her husband leaves her for a younger woman? Can you imagine the crawling of the flesh?'
모모
모모She was suffering intense remembered pain. Her eyes were bright and dry, as if there was fire behind them. The cheerfullest thing I could think of to say was: 'But he didn't leave you.'
모모
모모'No. He came back. It wasn't me he cared for. There was the money, you see, and his postwar plans for his engineering firm. He was quite frank on the subject, and quite impenitent. In fact, he seemed to feel that he was doing me an enormous favor. He felt that any couple who couldn't have a child-'Her hand went to her mouth again.
모모
모모I prompted her: 'But you had Tom.'
모모
모모'Tom came later,' she said, 'too late to save us.'
모모
모모Her voice broke into a deeper range. 'Too late to save my husband. He's a tragically unhappy man. But I can't find it in my heart to pity him.'
모모
모모Her hand touched her thin breast and lingered there.
모모
모모'What's the source of the trouble between him and Tom?'
모모
모모'The falsity,' she said in her deeper voice.
모모
모모'The falsity?'
모모
모모'I might as well tell you, Mr. Archer. You're going to find out about it sooner or later, anyway. And it may be important. Certainly it's psychologically important.'
모모
모모'Was Tom-is Tom an adopted son?'
모모
모모She nodded slowly. 'It may have to come out publicly, I don't know. For the present I'll ask you not to divulge it to anyone. No one in town here knows it. Tom doesn't know it himself. We adopted him in Los Angeles shortly after my husband left the Navy and before we moved here.'
모모
모모'But he resembles your husband.'
모모
모모'Ralph chose him for that reason. He's a very vain man, Mr. Archer. He's ashamed to admit even to our friends that we were incapable of producing a child of our own. Actually Ralph is the one who is sterile. I'm telling you this so you'll understand why he has insisted from the beginning on the great pretense. His desire, to have a son was so powerful, I think he has actually believed at times that Tom is his own flesh and blood.'
모모
모모'And he hasn't told Tom he isn't?'
모모
모모'No. Neither have I. Ralph wouldn't let me.'
모모
모모'Isn't that supposed to be a poor idea, with an adopted child?'
모모
모모'I told my husband that from the beginning. He had to be honest with Tom, or Tom would not be honest with him. There would be falsity at the center of the household.'
모모
모모Her voice trembled, and she looked down at the carpet as if there was no floor under it. 'Well, you see what the consequence has been. A ruined boyhood for Tom and a breakdown of the family and now this tragedy.'
모모
모모'This almost-tragedy. He's still alive and we're going to get him back.'
모모
모모'But can we ever put the family back together?'
모모
모모'That will depend on all three of you. I've seen worse fractures mended, but not without competent help. I don't mean Laguna Perdida. And I don't mean just help for Tom.'
모모
모모'I know. I've been wretchedly unhappy, and my husband has been quite quite irrational on this subject for many years. Actually I think it goes back to Midway. Ralph's squadron was virtually massacred in that dreadful battle. Of course he blamed himself, since he was leading them. He felt as though he had lost a dozen sons.'
모모
모모'How do you know?'
모모
모모'He was still writing to me then,' she said, 'freely and fully, as one human being to another. He wrote me a number of very poignant letters about our having children, sons of our own. I know the thought was connected with his lost (tiers, although he never said so. And when he found out he couldn't have a son of his own, and decided to adopt Tom, well-' 
모모
모모She dropped her hands in her lap. Her hands seemed restless without knitting to occupy them.
모모
모모'What were you going to say, Mrs. Hillman?'
모모
모모'I hardly know. I'm not a psychologist, though I once had some training in philosophy-. I've felt that Ralph was trying to live out some sort of a fantasy with Tom-perhaps relive those terrible war years and make good his losses somehow. But you can't use people in that way, as figures in a fantasy. The whole thing broke down between Tom and his father.'
모모
모모'And Tom caught on that your husband wasn't his father.'
모모
모모She looked at me nervously. 'You think he did?'
모모
모모'I'm reasonably certain of it,' I said, remembering what Fred Tyndal had told me. 'Mrs. Hillman, what happened on the Sunday morning that you put Tom in Laguna Perdida?'
모모
모모She said quickly: 'It was Ralph's doing, not mine.'
모모
모모'Had they quarreled?'
모모
모모'Yes. Ralph was horribly angry with him.'
모모
모모'What about?'
모모
모모She bowed her head. 'My husband has forbidden me to speak of it.'
모모
모모'Did Tom say something or do something very wrong?'
모모
모모She sat with her head bowed and wouldn't answer me. 'I've told you more than I should have,' she said eventually, 'in the hope of getting to the bottom of this mess. Now will you tell me something? You mentioned a hotel called the Barcelona, and you said that Tom had been hiding there. You used the word "hiding."
모모
모모'Yes.'
모모
모모'Wasn't he being held?'
모모
모모'I don't know. There may have been some duress, possibly psychological duress. But I doubt that he was held in the ordinary sense.'
모모
모모She looked at me with distaste. I'd brought her some very tough pieces of information to chew on, and probably this was the hardest one of all. 
모모
모모'You've hinted from the beginning that Tom cooperated willingly with the kidnappers.'
모모
모모'It was a possibility that had to be considered. It still is.'
모모
모모'Please don't sidestep the question. I can stand a direct answer.'
모모
모모She smiled dimly. 'At this point I couldn't stand anything else.'
모모
모모'All right. I think Tom went with Harley of his own free will, rode in the trunk of Harley's car to the Barcelona Hotel, and stayed there without anybody having to hold a gun on him. I don't understand his reasons, and I won't until I talk to him. But he probably didn't know about the extortion angle. There's no evidence that he profited from it, anyway. He's broke.'
모모
모모'How do you know? Have you seen him?'
모모
모모'I've talked to somebody who talked to him. Tom said he needed money.'
모모
모모'I suppose that's good news in a way.'
모모
모모'I thought it was.'
모모
모모I made a move to go, but she detained me. There was more on her mind: 'This Barcelona Hotel you speak of, is it the big old rundown place on the coast highway?'
모모
모모'Yes. It's closed up now.'
모모
모모'And Tom hid, or was hidden, there?'
모모
모모I nodded. 'The watchman at the hotel, a man named Sipe, was a partner in the extortion. He may have been the brains behind it, to the extent that it took any brains. He was shot to death this morning. The other partner, Harley, was stabbed to death last night.'
모모
모모Her face was open, uncomprehending, as if she couldn't quite take in these terrible events.
모모
모모'How extraordinary,' she breathed.
모모
모모'Not so very. They were heavy thieves, and they came to a heavy end.'
모모
모모'I don't mean those violent deaths, although they're part of it. I mean the deep connections you get in life, the coming together of the past and the present.'
모모
모모'What do you have in mind?'
모모
모모She grimaced. 'Something ugly, but I'm afraid it has to be said. You see, the Barcelona Hotel, where my son, my adopted son, has been staying with criminals, apparently'-she took a shuddering breath-'that very place was the scene of Ralph's affair with Susanna Drew. And did you say that the watchman's name was Sipe, the one who was shot?'
모모
모모'Yes. Otto Sipe.'
모모
모모'Did he once work as a detective in the hotel?'
모모
모모'Yes. He was the kind of detective who gives our trade a bad name.'
모모
모모'I have reason to know that,' she said. 'I knew Mr. Sipe. That is, I talked to him once, and he left an impression that I haven't been able to wipe out of my memory. He was a gross, corrupt man. He came to my house in Brentwood in the spring of 1945.
모모
모모He was the one who told me about Ralph's affair with Miss Drew.'
모모
모모'He wanted money, of course.'
모모
모모'Yes, and I gave him money. Two hundred dollars, he asked for, and when he saw that I was willing to pay he raised it to five hundred, all the cash I had on hand. Well, the money part is unimportant. It always is,' she said, reminding me that she had never needed money.
모모
모모'What did Sipe have to say to you?'
모모
모모'That my husband was committing adultery-he had a snapshot to prove it-and it was his duty under the law to arrest him. I don't know now if there was ever such a law on the books-' 
모모
모모'There was. I don't think it's been enforced lately, or an awful lot of people would be in jail.'
모모
모모'He mentioned jail, and the effect it would have on my husband's reputation. This was just about the time when Ralph began to believe he could make Captain. I know from this height and distance it sounds childish, but it was the biggest thing in his life at that time. He came from an undistinguished family, you see-his father was just an unsuccessful small businessman-and he felt he had to shine in so many ways to match my family's distinction.'
모모
모모She looked at me with sad intelligence. 'We all need something to buttress our pride, don't we, fragments to shore against our ruins.'
모모
모모'You were telling me about your interview with Otto Sipe.'
모모
모모'So I was. My mind tends to veer away from scenes like that. In spite of the pain and shock I felt-it was my first inkling that Ralph was unfaithful to me-I didn't want to see all his bright ambitions wrecked. So I paid the dreadful man his dirty money, and he gave me his filthy snapshot.'
모모
모모'Did you hear from him again?'
모모
모모'No.'
모모
모모'I'm surprised he didn't attach himself to you for life.'
모모
모모'Perhaps he intended to. But Ralph stopped him. I told Ralph about his visit, naturally.'
모모
모모She added: 'I didn't show him the snapshot. That I destroyed.'
모모
모모'How did Ralph stop him?'
모모
모모'I believe he knocked him down and frightened him off: I didn't get a very clear account from Ralph. By then we weren't communicating freely. I went home to Boston and I didn't see Ralph again until the end of the year, when he brought his ship to Boston harbor. We had a reconciliation of sorts. It was then we decided to adopt a child.'
모모
모모I wasn't listening too closely. The meanings of the case were emerging. Ralph Hillman had had earlier transactions with both of the extortionists. He had been Mike Harley's superior officer, and had thrown him out of the Navy. He had knocked down Otto Sipe. And they had made him pay for his superiority and his power.
모모
모모Elaine was thinking along the same lines. She said in a soft, despondent voice: 'Mr. Sipe would never have entered our lives if Ralph hadn't used that crummy hotel for his crummy little purposes.'
모모
모모'You mustn't blame your husband for everything. No doubt he did wrong. We all do. But the things he did nineteen or twenty years ago aren't solely responsible for this kidnapping, or whatever it was. It isn't that simple.'
모모
모모'I know. I don't blame him for everything.'
모모
모모'Sipe, for instance, would probably have been involved anyway. His partner Mike Harley knew your husband and had a grievance against him.'
모모
모모'But why did Tom, my poor dear Tom, end up at that same hotel? Isn't there a fatality in it?'
모모
모모'Maybe there is. To Sipe and Harley it was simply a convenient place to keep him.'
모모
모모'Why would Tom stay with them? They must be-have been outrageous creatures.'
모모
모모'Teen-age boys sometimes go for the outrageous.'
모모
모모'Do they not,' she said. 'But I can't really blame Tom for anything he's done. Ralph and I have given him little enough reality to hold on to. Tom's a sensitive, artistic, introverted boy. My husband didn't want him to be those things-perhaps they reminded Ralph that he wasn't our son, really. So he kept trying to change him. And when he couldn't, he withdrew his interest. Not his love, I'm sure. He's still profoundly concerned with Tom.'
모모
모모'But he spends his time with Dick Leandro.'
모모
모모One corner of her mouth lifted, wrinkling her cheek and eye, as if age and disillusion had taken sudden possession of that side of her face. 
모모
모모'You're quite a noticer, Mr. Archer.'
모모
모모'You have to be, in my job. Not that Dick Leandro makes any secret of his role. I met him coming, out of your driveway.'
모모
모모'Yes. He was looking for Ralph. He's very dependent on Ralph,' she added dryly.
모모
모모'How would you describe the relationship, Mrs. Hillman? Substitute son?'
모모
모모'I suppose I would. Dick's mother and father broke up some years ago. His father left town, and of course his mother got custody of Dick. He needed a substitute father. And Ralph needed someone to crew for him on the sloop-I sometimes think it's the most urgent need he has, or had. Someone to share the lusty gusty things he likes to do, and would like a son to do.'
모모
모모'He could do better than Dick, couldn't he?'
모모
모모She was silent for a while. 'Perhaps he could. But when you have an urgent need, you tend to hook up with people who have urgent needs of their own. Poor Dick has a great many urgent needs.'
모모
모모'Some of which have been met. He told me that your husband put him through college.'
모모
모모'He did. But don't forget that Dick's father used to work for Ralph's firm. Ralph is very strong on loyalty, up and down.'
모모
모모'Is Dick?'
모모
모모'He's fanatically loyal to Ralph,' she said with emphasis.
모모
모모'Let me ask you a hypothetical question, without prejudice, as they say in court. If your husband disinherited Tom, would Dick be a likely heir?'
모모
모모'That's excessively hypothetical, isn't it?'
모모
모모'But the answer might have practical consequences. What's your answer?'
모모
모모'Dick might be left something. He probably will be in any case. But please don't imagine that poor stupid Dick, with his curly hair and his muscles, is capable of plotting-' 
모모
모모'I wasn't suggesting that.'
모모
모모'And you mustn't embarrass Dick. He's come through nobly in this crisis. Both of us have leaned on him.'
모모
모모'I know. I'll leave him alone.'
모모
모모I got up to go. 'Thank you for being frank with me.'
모모
모모'There's not much point in pretending at this late date. If there's anything else you need to know-' 
모모
모모'There is one thing that might help. If you could give me the name of the agency through which you adopted Tom?'
모모
모모'It wasn't done through an agency. It was handled privately.'
모모
모모'Through a lawyer, or a doctor?'
모모
모모'A doctor,' she said. 'I don't recall his name, but he delivered Tom at Cedars of Lebanon. We paid the expenses, you understand, as part of the bargain that we made with the mother.'
모모
모모'Who was she?'
모모
모모'Some poor woman who'd got herself in trouble. I didn't actually meet her, nor did I want to. I wanted to feel that Tom was my own son.'
모모
모모'I understand.'
모모
모모'Does it matter who his parents were? I mean, in the current situation?'
모모
모모'It does if he's wandering around Los Angeles looking for them. Which I have reason to think he may be doing. You should have a record somewhere of that doctor's name.'
모모
모모'My husband could tell you.'
모모
모모'But he isn't available.'
모모
모모'It may be in his desk in the library.'
모모
모모I followed her to the library and while she rummaged in the desk I looked at the pictures on the wall again. The group photo taken on the flight deck must have been Hillman's squadron. I looked closely at their faces, wondering which of the young men had died at Midway.
모모
모모Next I studied the yachting picture of Dick Leandro. His handsome, healthy, empty face told me nothing. Perhaps it would have meaning for somebody else. I took it off the wall and slipped it into the side pocket of my jacket.
모모
모모Elaine Hillman didn't notice. She had found the name she was looking for.
모모
모모'Elijah Weintraub,' she said, 'was the doctor's name.'
모모
모모22
모모
모모I PHONED DR Weintraub long-distance. He confirmed the fact that he had arranged for Thomas Hillman's adoption, but he refused to discuss it over the phone. I made an appointment to see him in his office that afternoon.
모모
모모Before I drove back to Los Angeles I checked in with Lieutenant Bastian. He'd been working on the case for nearly three days, and the experience hadn't improved his disposition. The scarlike lines in his face seemed to have deepened. His voice was hoarse and harsh, made harsher by irony: 'It's nice of you to drop by every few days.'
모모
모모'I'm working for Ralph Hillman now.'
모모
모모'I know that, and it gives you certain advantages. Which you seize. But you and I are working on the same case, and we're supposed to be cooperating. That means periodic exchanges of information.'
모모
모모'Why do you think I'm here?'
모모
모모His eyes flared down. 'Fine. What have you found out about the Hillman boy?'
모모
모모I told Bastian nearly all of it, enough to satisfy both him and my conscience. I left out the adoption and Dr Weintraub, and the possibility that Tom might turn up at the Santa Monica bus station at nine that night. About his other movements, and the fact that he had probably been a voluntary captive in the Barcelona Hotel, I was quite frank.
모모
모모'It's too bad Otto Sipe had to die,' Bastian grumbled. 'He could have cleared up a lot of things.'
모모
모모I agreed.
모모
모모'Exactly what happened to Sipe? You were a witness.'
모모
모모'He attacked Ben Daly with a spade. Daly was holding my gun while I examined Harley's body. The gun went off.' 
모모
모모Bastian made a disgusted noise with his lips. 'What do you know about Daly?'
모모
모모'Not much. He has a service station across from the Barcelona. He struck me as dependable. He's a war veteran-' 
모모

모모
모모'That would be natural enough. Daly ran the nearest service station to where Sipe worked.'
모모
모모'So you don't think Daly killed him to shut him up?'
모모

모모
모모'Not yet. I have a description.'
모모

모모

모모

모모
모모'That's what I was going to suggest.'
모모

모모

모모

모모

모모

모모

모모

모모

모모

모모

모모
모모'I treat 'em all gently. Harold Harley called me a few minutes ago. He's taking his brother's death hard.'
모모

모모

모모
모모He was needling me about the death of Otto Sipe. On that note I left.
모모
모모I made a detour up the coast highway on the way to my appointment with Dr Weintraub, and stopped at Ben Daly's service station. Ben was there by the pump, with a bandage around his head. When he saw me he went into the office and didn't come out. A boy who looked like a teen-aged version of Ben emerged after a while. He asked in an unfriendly way if there was anything he could do for me.
모모
모모'I'd like to talk to Mr. Daly for a minute.'
모모

모모
