모모
모모The boy went into the office, closing the door behind him. Across the roaring highway, the Barcelona Hotel asserted itself in the sunlight like a monument of a dead civilization. In the driveway I could see a number of county cars, and a man in deputy's uniform keeping back a crowd of onlookers.
모모

모모
모모'Tell him I'm sorry.'
모모
모모The boy retreated formally, like an emissary. He didn't show himself again, and neither did his father. I gave up on Daly for the present.
모모
모모Dr Weintraub's office was in one of the new medical buildings on Wilshire, near Cedars of Lebanon Hospital. I went up in a self-service elevator to a waiting room on the fifth floor. This was handsomely furnished in California Danish and had soothing music piped in, which got on my nerves before I had time to sit down. Two pregnant women on opposite sides of the room caught me, a mere man, in a crossfire of pitying glances.
모모
모모The highly made-up girl behind the counter in one corner said: 'Mr. Archer?'
모모
모모'Yes.'
모모
모모'Dr Weintraub will see you in a few minutes. You're not a patient, are you? So we needn't bother taking your history, need we?'
모모
모모'It would give you the horrors, honey.'
모모
모모She moved her eyelashes up and down a few times, to indicate shocked surprise. Her eyelashes were long and thick and phony, and they waved clumsily in the air like tarantula legs.
모모
모모Dr Weintraub opened a door and beckoned me into his consulting room. He was a man about my age, perhaps a few years older. Like a lot of other doctors, he hadn't looked after himself. His shoulders were stooped under his white smock, and he was putting on weight. The curly black hair was retreating from his forehead.
모모
모모But the dark eyes behind his glasses were extraordinarily alive. I could practically feel their impact as we shook hands. I recognized his face, but I couldn't place it.
모모

모모
모모'Thanks. It will have to come later.'
모모
모모I didn't tell him he needed exercise.
모모
모모He sat down rather heavily at his desk, and I took the patient's chair facing him. One whole wall of the room was occupied by bookshelves. The books seemed to cover several branches of medicine, with special emphasis on psychiatry and gynecology.
모모
모모'Are you a psychiatrist, doctor?'
모모
모모'No, I am not.'
모모
모모His eyes were melancholy. 'I studied for the Boards at one time but then the war came along. Afterwards I chose another specialty, delivering babies.'
모모

모모
모모'You delivered Thomas Hillman.'
모모
모모'Yes. I told you so on the telephone.'
모모
모모'Have you refreshed your memory about the date?'
모모

모모
모모'How did his real mother feel about it?'
모모
모모'She didn't want him,' he said.
모모
모모'Wasn't she married?'
모모
모모'As a matter of fact, she was a young married woman. Neither she nor her husband wanted a child at that time.'
모모
모모'Are you willing to tell me their name?'
모모
모모'It wouldn't be professional, Mr. Archer.'
모모

모모

모모

모모
모모'Neither this week nor any other time.'
모모
모모He got up bulkily and moved past me to the door, where he waited with courteous impatience till I went out past him.

모모23
모모
모모WITH ITS PORTICO supported by fluted columns, the front of Susanna's apartment house was a cross between a Greek temple and a Southern plantation mansion. It was painted blue instead of white. Diminished by the columns, I went into the cold marble lobby. Miss Drew was out. She had been out all day.
모모
모모I looked at my watch. It was nearly five. The chances were she had gone to work after her breakfast with Hillman. I went out and sat in my car at the curb and watched the rush-hour traffic crawling by.
모모
모모Shortly after five a yellow cab veered out of the traffic stream and pulled up behind my car. Susanna got out. I went up to her as she was paying the driver. She dropped a five-dollar bill when she saw me. The driver scooped it up.
모모
모모'I've been hoping you'd come to see me, Lew,' she said without much conviction. 'Do come in.'
모모

모모

모모

모모

모모

모모

I told her so, and she was satisfied. She's quite an impressive woman, you know, at least she was then.'
모모
모모'She still is, when you get under the surface. And Ralph Hillman is an impressive man.'
모모

모모
모모I said to test her honesty: 'Didn't you have any other reason for dropping him, besides Elaine Hillman's visit?'
모모
모모'I don't know what you mean,' she said, failing the honesty test, or perhaps the memory test.
모모
모모'How did Elaine Hillman find out about you?'
모모
모모'Oh. That.'
모모
모모The shame that lay beneath her knowledge of herself came up into her face and took possession of it. She whispered: 'Mrs. Hillman told you, I suppose?'
모모
모모'She mentioned a picture.'
모모
모모'Did she show it to you?'
모모
모모'She's too much of a lady.'
모모
모모'That was a nasty crack!'
모모
모모'It wasn't intended to be. You're getting paranoid.'
모모
모모'Yes, Doctor. Shall I stretch out on this convenient couch and tell you a dream?'
모모
모모'I can think of better uses for a couch.'
모모
모모'Not now,' she said quickly.
모모
모모'No. Not now.'
모모
모모But in the darkest part of our transaction we had reached a point of intimacy, understanding at least. 'I'm sorry I have to drag all this stuff out.'
모모
모모'I know. I know that much about you. I also know you haven't finished.'
모모
모모'Who took the picture? Otto Sipe?'
모모
모모'He was there. I heard his voice.'
모모
모모'You didn't see him?'
모모
모모'I hid my face,' she said. 'A flashbulb popped. It was like reality exploding.'
모모
모모She passed her hand over her eyes. 'I think it was another man in the doorway who took the picture.'
모모
모모'Harold Harley?'
모모
모모'It must have been. I didn't see him.'
모모
모모'What was the date?'
모모
모모'It's in my memory book. April 14, 1945. Why does it matter?'
모모
모모'Because you can't explode reality. Life hangs together in one piece. Everything is connected with everything else. The problem is to find the connections.'
모모
모모She said with some irony: 'That's your mission in life, isn't it? You're not interested in people, you're only interested in the connections between them. Like a-' she searched for an insulting word-'a plumber.'
모모
모모I laughed at her. She smiled a little. Her eyes remained somber.
모모
모모'There's another connection we have to go into,' I said. 'This one involves the telephone, not the plumbing.'
모모
모모'You mean Ralph's call the other day.'
모모
모모'Yes. He wanted you to keep quiet about something. What was it?'
모모
모모She squirmed a little, and gathered her feet under her. 'I don't want to get him into trouble. I owe him that much.'
모모
모모'Spare me the warmed-over sentiment. This is for real.'
모모
모모'You needn't sound so insulting.'
모모
모모'I apologize. Now let's have it.'
모모
모모'Well, he knew you had seen me, and he said we had to keep our stories straight. It seems there was a discrepancy in the story he told you. He told you he hadn't met Carol, but actually he had. After Mike Harley was arrested, she made an appeal to him and he did what he could. I wasn't to tell you about his interest in Carol.'
모모
모모'He was interested in Carol?'
모모
모모'Not in the way you mean,' she said with a lift of her head. 'I was his girl. He simply didn't like the idea of leaving a child bride like Carol alone in the Barcelona Hotel. He asked me to take her under my wing. My slightly broken wing. Which I did, as you know.'
모모
모모'It all sounds very innocent.'
모모
모모'It was. I swear it. Besides, I liked Carol, I loved her, that summer in Burbank. I felt as if the baby in her womb belonged to both of us.'
모모
모모'Have you ever had a child?'
모모
모모She shook her head rather sadly. 'I never will have now. I was sure I was pregnant once, that very spring we've been talking about, but the doctor said it was false, caused by wistful thinking.'
모모
모모'Was Carol seeing a doctor when she lived with you?'
모모
모모'Yes, I made her go. She went to the same doctor, actually. Weintraub, his name was.'
모모
모모'Did he deliver her baby?'
모모
모모'I wouldn't know. She'd already left me, remember, and gone off with Mike Harley. And I didn't go back to Dr Weintraub on account of the unpleasant associations.'
모모
모모'Was he unpleasant to you?'
모모
모모'I mean the association with Ralph Hillman. Ralph sent me to Dr Weintraub. I think they were buddies in the Navy.'
모모
모모Dr Weintraub's plump face came into my mind. At the same time I remembered where I had seen a younger version of it, stripped of excess flesh, that very day. Weintraub was a member of the group on the flight deck, in the picture hanging on Hillman's library wall.
모모
모모'It's funny,' Susanna was saying, 'how a name you haven't heard for seventeen or eighteen years will crop up, and then a couple of hours or a couple of days later, it will crop up again. Like Weintraub.'
모모
모모'Has the name been cropping up in other contexts?'
모모
모모'Just this afternoon at the office. I had a rather peculiar caller whom I meant to tell you about, but all these other matters pushed him out of my mind. He was interested in Dr Weintraub, too.'
모모
모모'Who was he?'
모모
모모'He didn't want to say. When I pressed him, he said his name was Jackman.'
모모
모모'Sam Jackman?'
모모
모모'He didn't mention his first name.'
모모
모모'Sam Jackman is a middle-aged Negro with very light skin who looks and talks like a jazz musician on his uppers, which he is.'
모모
모모'This boy seemed to be on his uppers, all right, but he certainly isn't Sam's. Maybe he's Sam son. He can't be more than eighteen or nineteen.'
모모
모모'Describe him.'
모모
모모'Thin-faced, very good features, very intense dark eyes, so intense he scared me a little. He seemed intelligent, but he was too excited to make much sense.'
모모
모모'What was he excited about?'
모모
모모I said with a mounting excitement of my own.
모모
모모'Carol's death, I think. He didn't refer to it directly, but he asked me if I had known Carol in 1945. Apparently he'd been all the way out to Burbank trying to find me. He came across an old secretary at Warner's whom I still keep in touch with, and used her name to get past my secretary: He wanted to know what I could tell him about the Harley baby, and when I couldn't tell him anything he asked me what doctor Carol had gone to. I dredged up Weintraub's name-Elijah Weintraub isn't exactly a forgettable name-and it satisfied him. I was quite relieved to get rid of him.'
모모
모모'I'm sorry you did.'
모모
모모She looked at me curiously. 'Do you suppose he could be the Harley baby himself?'
모모
모모I didn't answer her. I got out my collection of photographs and shuffled them. There was an electric tremor in my hands, as if time was short-circuiting through me.
모모
모모Susanna whispered fearfully: 'He isn't dead, is he, Lew? I couldn't bear to look at another dead picture.'
모모
모모'He's alive. At least, I hope he is.'
모모
모모I showed her Tom Hillman's face. She said: 'That's the boy I talked to. But he's very much the worse for wear now. Is he the Harley baby?'
모모
모모'I think so. He's also the baby that Ralph and Elaine Hillman adopted through Dr Weintraub. Did you get the impression that he was on his way to see Weintraub?'
모모
모모'Yes. I did.'
모모
모모She was getting excited, too. 'It's like an ancient identity myth. He's searching for his lost parentage.'
모모
모모'The hell of it is, both of his parents are dead. What time did you see him?'
모모
모모'Around four o'clock.'
모모
모모It was nearly six now. I went to the phone and called Weintraub's office. His answering service said it was closed for the night. The switchboard girl wouldn't give me Weintraub's home address or his unlisted number, and neither would the manager of the answering service. I had to settle for leaving my name and Susanna's number and waiting for Weintraub to call me, if he was willing.
모모
모모An hour went by. Susanna broiled me a steak, and chewed un-hungrily on a piece of it. We sat at a marble table in the patio and she told me all about identity myths and how they grew. Oedipus. Hamlet. Stephen Dedalus. Her father had taught courses in such subjects. It passed the time, but it didn't relieve my anxiety for the boy. Hamlet came to a bloody end. Oedipus killed his father and married his mother, and then blinded himself.
모모
모모'Thomas Harley,' I said aloud. 'Thomas Harley Hillman Jackman. He knew he wasn't the Hillmans' son. He thought he was a changeling.'
모모
모모'You get that in the myths, too.'
모모
모모'I'm talking about real life. He turned on his foster parents and went for his real parents. It's too bloody bad they had to be the Harleys.'
모모
모모'You're very certain that he is the Harley child.'
모모
모모'It fits in with everything I know about him. Incidentally, it explains why Ralph Hillman tried to hush up the fact that he'd taken an interest in Carol. He didn't want the facts of the adoption to come out.'
모모
모모'Why, though?'
모모
모모'He's kept it a secret all these years, even from Tom. He seems to be a little crazy on the subject.'
모모
모모'I got that impression this morning.'
모모
모모She leaned across the corner of the table and touched my fingers. 'Lew? You don't think he went off his rocker and murdered Carol himself' 
모모
모모'It's a possibility, but a remote one. What was on his mind at breakfast?'
모모
모모'Him, mostly. He felt his life was collapsing around his ears. He thought I might be interested in helping him to pick up the pieces. After eighteen years he was offering me my second big break.'
모모
모모Her scorn touched herself as well as Hillman.
모모
모모'I don't quite understand.'
모모
모모'He asked me to marry him, Lew. I suppose that's in line with contemporary mores. You get your future set up ahead of time, before you terminate your present marriage.'
모모
모모'I don't like that word "terminate."
모모
모모Did he say what he intended to do with Elaine?'
모모
모모'No.'
모모
모모She looked quite pale and haunted.
모모
모모'I hope divorce was all he had in mind. What was your answer?'
모모
모모'My answer?'
모모
모모'Your response to his proposal.'
모모
모모'Oh. I told him I was waiting for a better offer.'
모모
모모Her dark meaningful eyes were on my face. I sat there trying to frame a balanced answer. The telephone rang inside before I had a chance to deliver it.
모모
모모I went in through the door we had left open and picked up the receiver. 'Archer speaking.'
모모
모모'This is Dr Weintraub.'
모모
모모His voice had lost its calmness. 'I've just had a thoroughly upsetting experience-' 
모모
모모'Have you seen the Hillman boy?'
모모
모모'Yes. He came to me just as I was leaving. He asked me essentially the same question you did.'
모모
모모'What did you tell him, Doctor?'
모모
모모'I told him the truth. He already knew it, anyway. He wanted to know if Mike and Carol Harley were his parents. They were.'
모모
모모'How did he react to the information?'
모모
모모'Violently, I'm afraid. He hit me and broke my glasses. I'm practically blind without them. He got away from me.'
모모
모모'Have you told the police?'
모모
모모'No.'
모모
모모'Tell them, now. And tell them who he is.'
모모
모모'But his father-his adoptive father wouldn't want me-' 
모모
모모'I know how it is when you're dealing with an old commander, Doctor. He was your commander at one time, wasn't he?'
모모
모모'Yes. I was his flight surgeon.'
모모
모모'You aren't any more, and you can't let Hillman do your thinking for you. Do you tell the police, or do I?'
모모
모모'I will. I realize we can't let the boy run loose in his condition.'
모모
모모Just what is his condition?'
모모
모모'He's very upset and, as I said, violently acting out.'
모모
모모With his heredity, I thought, that was hardly surprising.
모모
모모16
모모
모모I KISSED SUSANNA goodbye and drove down Wilshire through Westwood. I wanted to be at the Santa Monica bus station at nine, just in case Tom showed up, but there was still time for another crack at Ben Daly. I turned down San Vicente toward the coastal highway.
모모
모모The sun was half down on the horizon, bleeding color into the sea and the sky. Even the front of the Barcelona Hotel was touched with factitious Mediterranean pink. The crowd of onlookers in the driveway had changed and dwindled. There were still a few waiting for something more interesting than their lives to happen.
모모
모모It was a warm night, and most of them were in beach costume. One man was dressed formally in a dark gray business suit and dark gray felt hat. He looked familiar.
모모
모모I pulled up the drive on impulse and got out. The man in the dark gray suit was Harold Harley. He was wearing a black tie, which Lila had doubtless chosen for him, and a woebegone expression.
모모
모모It deepened when he saw me. 'Mr. Archer?'
모모
모모'You can't have forgotten me, Harold.'
모모
모모'No. It's just that everything looks different, even people's faces. Or that hotel there. It's just a caved-in old dump, and I used to think it was a pretty ritzy place. Even the sky looks different.'
모모
모모He raised his eyes to the red-stretched sky. 'It looks hand-tinted, phony, like there was nothing behind it.'
모모
모모The little man talked like an artist. He might have become one, I thought, with a different childhood.
모모
모모'I didn't realize you were so fond of your brother.'
모모
모모'Neither did I. But it isn't just that. I hate California. Nothing really good ever happened to me in California. Or Mike either.'
모모
모모He gestured vaguely toward the cluster of official cars. 'I wisht I was back in Idaho.'
모모
모모I drew him away from the little group of onlookers, from the women in slacks and halters which their flesh overflowed, the younger girls with haystacks of hair slipping down their foreheads into their blue-shadowed eyes, the tanned alert looking boys with bleached heads and bleached futures. We stood under a magnolia tree that needed water.
모모
모모'What happened to your brother started in Idaho, Harold.'
모모
모모And also what happened to you, or failed to happen.
모모
모모'You think I don't know that? The old man always said Mike would die on the gallows. Anyway, he cheated the gallows.'
모모
모모'I talked to your father yesterday.'
모모
모모Harold started violently, and glanced behind him. 'Is he in town?'
모모
모모'I was in Pocatello yesterday.'
모모
모모He looked both relieved and anxious. 'How is he?'
모모
모모'Much the same, I gather. You didn't tell me he was one step ahead of the butterfly nets.'
모모
모모'You didn't ask me. Anyway, he isn't like that all the time.'
모모
모모'But he had to be committed more than once.'
모모
모모'Yeah.'
모모
모모He hung his head. In the final glare of day I could see the old closet dust in the groove of his hat, and the new sweat staining the hatband.
모모
모모'It's nothing to blame yourself for,' I said. 'It explains a lot about Mike.'
모모
모모'I know. The old man was a terror when Mike was a kid. Maw finally had him committed for what he did to Mike and her. Mike left home and never came back, and who could blame him?'
모모
모모'But you stayed.'
모모
모모'For a while. I had a trick of pretending I was some place else, like here in California. I finally came out here and went to photography school.'
모모
모모I returned to the question that interested me. It was really a series of questions about the interlinked lives that brought Mike Harley and Carol Brown from their beginnings in Idaho to their ends in California. Their beginnings and ends had become clear enough. The middle still puzzled me, as well as the ultimate end that lay ahead in darkness.
모모
모모'I talked to Carol's parents, too,' I said. 'Carol was there earlier in the summer, and she left a suitcase in her room. A letter in it explained to me why you blamed yourself for the Hillman extortion.'
모모
모모'You saw my letter, eh? I should never have written a letter like that to Mike. I should have known better.'
모모
모모He was hanging his head again.
모모
모모'It's hard to see ahead and figure what the little things we do will lead to. And you weren't intending to suggest anything wrong.'
모모
모모'Gosh, no.'
모모
모모'Anyway, your letter helped me. It led me back here to Otto Sipe, and I hope eventually to the Hillman boy. The boy was holed up here with Sipe from Monday morning till Wednesday night, last night.'
모모
모모'No kidding.'
모모
모모'How well did you know Otto Sipe?'
모모
모모Harold winced away from the question. If he could, he would have disappeared entirely, leaving his dark business suit and black tie and dusty hat suspended between the crisp brown grass and the dry leaves of the magnolia. He said in a voice that didn't want to be heard: 'He was Mike's friend. I got to know him that way. He trained Mike for a boxing career.'
모모
모모'What kind of a career did he train you for, Harold?'
모모
모모'You. Didn't Sipe get you the job as hotel photographer here?'
모모
모모'On account of- I was Mike's brother.'
모모
모모'I'm sure that had something to do with it. But didn't Sipe want you to help him with his sideline?'
모모
모모'What sideline was that?'
모모
모모'Blackmail.'
모모
모모He shook his head so vehemently that his hat almost fell off. 'I never had any part of the rake-off, honest. He paid me standard rates to take those pictures, a measly buck a throw, and if I didn't do it I'd lose my job. I quit anyway, as soon as I had the chance. It was a dirty business.'
모모
모모He peered up the driveway at the bland decaying face of the hotel. It was stark white now in the twilight. 'I never took any benefit from it. I never even knew who the people were.'
모모
모모'Not even once?'
모모
모모'I don't know what you mean.'
모모
모모'Didn't you take a picture of Captain Hillman and his girl?'
모모
모모His face was pale and wet. 'I don't know. I never knew their names.'
모모
모모'Last spring at Newport you recognized Hillman.'
모모
모모'Sure, he was the exec of Mike's ship. I met him when I went aboard that time.'
모모
모모'And no other time?'
모모
모모'No sir.'
모모
모모'When were you and Mike arrested? In the spring of 1945?'
모모
모모He nodded. 'The fifth of March. I'm not likely to forget it. It was the only time I ever got arrested. After they let me go I never came back here. Until now.'
모모
모모He looked around at the place as if it had betrayed him a second time.
모모
모모'If you're telling the truth about the date, you didn't take the picture I'm interested in. It was taken in April.'
모모
모모'I'm not lying. By that time Otto Sipe had another boy.'
모모
모모'What gave him so much power around the hotel?'
모모
모모'I think he had something on the management. He hushed up something for them, long ago, something about a movie star who stayed here.'
모모
모모'Was Mike staying here at the time he was picked up?'
모모
모모'Yeah. I let him and Carol use my room, the one that went with the job. I slept in the employees' dormitory. I think Otto Sipe let Carol stay on in the room for a while after me and Mike were arrested.'
모모
모모'Was it the room next door to his, at the end of the corridor?'
모모
모모'Yeah.'
모모
모모'Did it have a brass bed in it?'
모모
모모'Yeah. Why?'
모모
모모'I was just wondering. They haven't changed the furnishings since the war. That interconnecting bathroom would have been handy for Sipe, if he liked Carol.'
모모
모모He shook his head. 'Not him. He had no use for women. And Carol had no use for him. She got out of there as soon as she could make other arrangements. She went to live with a woman friend in Burbank.'
모모
모모'Susanna.'
모모
모모Harold blinked. 'Yeah. That was her name, Susanna. I never met her, but she must have been a nice person.'
모모
모모'What kind of a girl was Carol?'
모모
모모'Carol? She was a beauty. When a girl has her looks, you don't think much about going deeper. I mean, there she was. I always thought she was an innocent young girl. But Lila says you could fill a book with what I don't know about women.'
모모
모모I looked at my watch. It was past eight, and Harold had probably taken me as far as he could. Partly to make sure of this, I asked him to come across the highway and see his old acquaintance, Ben Daly. He didn't hang back.
모모
모모Daly scowled at us from the doorway of his lighted office. Then he recognized Harold, and his brow cleared. He came out and shook hands with him, disregarding me.
모모
모모'Long time no see, Har.'
모모
모모'You can say that again.'
모모
모모They talked to each other across a distance of years, with some warmth and without embarrassment. There was no sign of guilty involvement between them. It didn't follow necessarily, but I pretty well gave up on the idea that either of them was involved in any way with the recent crimes.
모모
모모I broke in on their conversation: 'Will you give me one minute, Ben? You may be able to help me solve that murder.'
모모
모모'How? By killing somebody else?'
모모
모모'By making another identification, if you can.'
모모
모모I brought out Dick Leandro's picture and forced it into his hand. 'Have you ever seen this man?'
모모
모모He studied the picture for a minute. His hand was unsteady. 'I may have. I'm not sure.'
모모
모모'When?'
모모
모모'Last night. He may be the one who came to the hotel last night.'
모모
모모'The one with the girl, in the new blue Chevvy?'
모모
모모'Yeah. He could be the one. But I wouldn't want to swear to it in court.'
모모
모모25 
모모
모모THE SANTA MONICA bus station is on a side street off lower Wilshire. At a quarter to nine I left my car at the curb and went in. Stella, that incredible child, was there. She was sitting at the lunch counter at the rear in a position from which she could watch all the doors.
모모
모모She saw me, of course, and swung around to hide her face in a cup of coffee. I sat beside her. She put down her cup with an impatient rap. The coffee in it looked cold, and had a grayish film on it.
모모
모모She spoke without looking directly at me, like somebody in a spy movie. 'Go away. You'll frighten Tommy off' 'He doesn't know me.'
모모
모모'But I'm supposed to be alone. Besides, you look like a policeman or something.'
모모
모모'Why is Tommy allergic to policemen?'
모모
모모'You would be, too, if they locked you up the way they locked him up.'
모모
모모'If you keep running away, they'll be locking you up, Stella.'
모모
모모'They're not going to get the chance,' she said, with a sharp sideways glance at me. 'My father took me to a psychiatrist today, to see if I needed to be sent to Laguna Perdida. I told her everything, just as I've told you. She said there was nothing the matter with me at all. So when my father went in to talk to her I walked out the front door and took a taxi to the bus station, and there was a bus just leaving.'
모모
모모'I'm going to have to drive you home again.'
모모
모모She said in a very young voice: 'Don't teen-agers have any rights?'
모모
모모'Yes, including the right to adult protection.'
모모
모모'I won't go without Tommy!'
모모
모모Her voice rose and broke on his name. Half the people in the small station were looking at us. The woman behind the lunch counter came over to Stella.
모모
모모'Is he bothering you, miss?'
모모
모모She shook her head. 'He's a very good friend.'
모모
모모This only deepened the woman's suspicions, but it silenced her. I ordered a cup of coffee. When she went to draw it, I said to Stella: 'I won't go without Tommy, either. What did your psychiatrist friend think about him, by the way?'
모모
모모'She didn't tell me. Why?'
모모
모모'I was just wondering.'
모모
모모The waitress brought my coffee. I carried it to the far end of the counter and drank it slowly. It was eight minutes to nine. People were lining up at the loading door, which meant that a bus was expected.
모모
모모I went out the front, and almost walked into Tommy. He had on slacks and a dirty white shirt. His face was a dirty white, except where a fuzz of beard showed.
모모
모모'Excuse me, sir,' he said, and stepped around me.
모모
모모I didn't want to let him get inside, where taking him would create a public scene that would bring in the police. I needed a chance to talk to him before anyone else did. There wasn't much use in trying to persuade him to come with me. He was lean and quick and could certainly outrun me.
모모
모모These thoughts went through my head in the second before he reached the door of the station. I put both arms around his waist from behind, lifted him off his feet, and carried him wildly struggling to my car. I pushed him into the front seat and got in beside him. Other cars were going by in the road, but nobody stopped to ask me any questions. They never do any more.
모모
모모Tom let out a single dry sob or whimper, high in his nose. He must have known that this was the end of running.
모모
모모'My name is Lew Archer,' I said. 'I'm a private detective employed by your father.'
모모
모모'He isn't my father.'
모모
모모'An adoptive father is a father, too.'
모모
모모'Not to me he isn't. I don't want any part of Captain Hillman,' he said with the cold distance of injured youth. 'Or you either.'
모모
모모I noticed a cut on the knuckle of his right hand. It had been bleeding. He put the knuckle in his mouth and sucked it, looking at me over it. It was hard to take him seriously at that moment. But he was a very serious young man.
모모
모모'I'm not going back to my cruddy so-called parents.'
모모
모모'You have nobody else.'
모모
모모'I have myself.'
모모
모모'You haven't been handling yourself too well.'
모모
모모'Another lecture.'
모모
모모'I'm pointing out a fact. If you could look after yourself decently, you might make out a case for independence. But you've been rampaging around clobbering middle-aged doctors-' 
모모
모모'He tried to make me go home.'
모모
모모'You're going home. The alternative seems to be a life with bums and criminals.'
모모
모모'You're talking about my parents, my real parents.'
모모
모모He spoke with conscious drama, but there was also a kind of bitter awe in his voice. 'My mother wasn't a bum and she wasn't a criminal. She was-nice.'
모모
모모'I didn't mean her.'
모모
모모'And my father wasn't so bad, either,' he said without conviction.
모모
모모'Who killed them, Tom?'
모모
모모His face became blank and tight. It looked like a wooden mask used to fend off suffering.
모모
모모'I don't know anything about it,' he said in a monotone. 'I didn't know Carol was dead, even, till I saw the papers last night.
모모
모모I didn't know Mike was dead till I saw the papers today. Next question.'
모모
모모'Don't be like that, Tom. I'm not a cop, and I'm not your enemy.
모모
모모'With the so-called parents I've got, who needs enemies? All my-all Captain Hillman ever wanted was a pet boy around the house, somebody to do tricks. I'm tired to doing tricks for him.'
모모
모모'You should be tired, after this last trick. It was a honey of a trick.'
모모
모모He gave me his first direct look, half in anger and half in fear. 'I had a right to go with my real parents.'
모모
모모'Maybe. We won't argue about that. But you certainly had no right to help them extort money from your father.'
모모
모모'He's not my father.'
모모
모모'I know that. Do you have to keep saying it?'
모모
모모'Do you have to keep calling him my father?'
모모
모모He was a difficult boy. I felt good, anyway. I had him.
모모
모모'Okay,' I said. 'We'll call him Mr. X and we'll call your mother Madam X and we'll call you the Lost Dauphin of France.'
모모
모모'That isn't so funny.'
모모
모모He was right. It wasn't.
모모
모모'Getting back to the twenty-five thousand dollars you helped to take them for, I suppose you know you're an accomplice in a major felony.'
모모
모모'I didn't know about the money. They didn't tell me. I don't think Carol knew about it, either.'
모모
모모'That's hard to believe, Tom.'
모모
모모'It's true. Mike didn't tell us. He just said he had a deal cooking.'
모모
모모'If you didn't know about the extortion, why did you ride away in the trunk of his car?'
모모
모모'So I wouldn't be seen. Mike said my dad-' he swallowed the word, with disgust-'he said that Captain Hillman had all the police looking for me, to put me back in Laguna-' He became aware of his present situation. He peered around furtively, scrambled under the wheel to the far door. I pulled him back into the middle of the seat and put an armlock on him.
모모
모모'You're staying with me, Tom, if I have to use handcuffs.'
모모
모모'FUZZ!'
모모
모모The jeering word came strangely from him, like a foreign word he was trying to make his own. It bothered me. Boys, like men, have to belong to something. Tom had felt betrayed by one world, the plush deceptive world of Ralph Hillman, with schools like Laguna Perdida on the underside of the weave. He had plunged blindly into another world, and now he had lost that. His mind must be desperate for a place to rest, I thought, and I wasn't doing much of a job of providing one.
모모
모모A bus came down the street. As it turned into the loading area, I caught a glimpse of passengers at the windows, travel-drugged and blare. California here we come, right back where we started from.
모모
모모I relaxed my grip on Tom. 'I couldn't let you go,' I said, 'even if I wanted to. You're not stupid. Try for once to figure out how this looks to other people.'
모모
모모'This?'
모모
모모'The whole charade. Your running away from school-for which I certainly don't blame you-' 
모모
모모'Thanks a lot.'
모모
모모I disregarded his irony. 'And the phony kidnapping and all the rest of it. An adopted son is just as important as a real one to his parents. Yours have been worried sick about you.'
모모
모모'I bet.'
모모
모모'Neither one of them gave a damn about the money, incidentally. It's you they cared about, and care about.'
모모
모모'There's something missing,' he said.
모모
모모'What?'
모모
모모'The violin accompaniment.'
모모
모모'You're a hard boy to talk to, Tom.'
모모
모모'My friends don't think so.'
모모
모모'What's a friend? Somebody who lets you run wild?'
모모
모모'Somebody who doesn't want to throw me into the Black Hole of Calcutta, otherwise known as Laguna Perdida School.'
모모
모모'I don't.'
모모
모모'You say you don't. But you're working for Captain Hillman, and he does.'
모모
모모'Not any more.'
모모
모모The boy shook his head. 'I don't believe you, and I don't believe him. After a few things happen to you, you start to believe what people do, not what they say. People like the Hillmans would think that a person like Carol was a nothing, a nothing woman. But she wasn't to me. She liked me. She treated me well. Even my real father never raised his hand to me. The only trouble we had was about the way he treated Carol.'
모모
모모He had dropped his brittle sardonic front and was talking to me in a human voice. Stella chose this moment to come out of the loading area onto the sidewalk. Her faced was pinched with disappointment.
모모
모모Tom caught sight of her almost as soon as I did. His eyes lit up as if she was an angel from some lost paradise. He leaned across me.
모모
모모'Hey! Stell!'
모모
모모She came running. I got out of the car and let her take my place beside the boy. They didn't embrace or kiss. Perhaps their hands met briefly. I got in behind the wheel.
모모
모모Stella was saying: 'It feels as though you've been gone for ages.'
모모
모모'It does to me, too.'
모모
모모'You should have called me sooner.'
모모
모모'I did.'
모모
모모'I mean, right away.'
모모
모모'I was afraid you'd-do what you did.'
모모
모모He jerked his chin in my direction.
모모
모모'I didn't, though. Not really. It was his idea. Anyway, you have to go home. We both do.'
모모
모모'I have no home.'
모모
모모'Neither have I, then. Mine's just as bad as yours.'
모모
모모'No, it isn't.'
모모
모모'Yes, it is. Anyway,' she said to clinch the argument, 'you need a bath. I can smell you. And a shave.'
모모
모모I glanced at his face. It had a pleased silly embarrassed expression.
모모
모모The street was empty of traffic at the moment. I started the car and made a U-turn toward the south. Tom offered no objection.
모모
모모Once on the freeway, in that anonymous world of rushing lights and darkness, he began to talk in his human voice to Stella.
모모
모모Carol had phoned him, using his personal number, several weeks before. She wanted to arrange a meeting with him. That night, driving Ralph Hillman's Cadillac, he picked her up at the view-point overlooking the sea near Dack's Auto Court.
모모
모모He parked in an orange grove that smelled of weddings and listened to the story of her life. Even though he'd often doubted that he belonged to the Hillmans, it was hard for him to believe that he was Carol's son. But he was strongly drawn to her. The relationship was like an escape hatch in Captain Hillman's tight little ship. He kept going back to Carol, and eventually he believed her. He even began to love her in a way.
모모
모모'Why didn't you tell me about her?'
모모
모모Stella said. 'I would have liked to know her.'
모모
모모'No, you wouldn't.'
모모
모모His voice was rough. 'Anyway, I had to get to know her myself first. I had to get adjusted to the whole idea of my mother. And then I had to decide what to do. You see, she wanted to leave my father. He gave her a hard time, he always had. She said if she didn't get away from him soon, she'd never be able to. She wasn't good at standing up for herself, and she wanted my help. Besides, I think she knew he was up to something.'
모모
모모'You mean the kidnapping and all?' she said.
모모
모모'I think she knew it and she didn't know it. You know how women are.'
모모
모모'I know my mother,' she answered sagely.
모모
모모They had forgotten me. I was the friendly chauffeur, good old graying Lew Archer, and we would go on driving like this forever through a night so dangerous that it had to feel secure. I remembered a kind of poem or parable that Susanna had quoted to me years before. A bird came in through a window at one end of a lighted hall, flew the length of the hall, and out through another window into darkness: that was the span of a human life. The headlights that rose in the distance and swooped by and fell away behind us reminded me of Susanna's briefly lighted bird. I wished that she was with me.
모모
모모Tom was telling Stella how he first met his father. Mike had been kept in the background the first week; he was supposed to be in Los Angeles looking for work. Finally, on the Saturday night, Tom met him at the auto court.
모모
모모'That was the night you borrowed our car, wasn't it?'
모모
모모'Yeah. My fa-Ralph had me grounded, you know. Carol spilled some wine on the front seat of the car and he smelled it. He thought I was driving and drinking.'
모모
모모'Did Carol drink much?'
모모
모모'Quite a bit. She drank a lot that Saturday night. So did he. I had some wine, too.'
모모
모모'You're not old enough.'
모모
모모'It was with dinner,' he said. 'Carol cooked spaghetti. Spaghetti a la Pocatello, she called it. She sang some of the old songs for me, like "Sentimental journey."
모모
모모It was kind of fun,' he said doubtfully.
모모
모모'Is that why you didn't come home?'
모모
모모'No. I-' The word caught in his throat. 'I-' His face, which I could see in the rear-view mirror, became contorted with effort. He couldn't finish the sentence.
모모
모모'Did you want to stay with them?' Stella said after a while.
모모
모모'No. I don't know.'
모모
모모'How did you like your father?'
모모
모모'He was all right, I guess, until he got drunk. We played some gin rummy and he didn't win, so he broke up the game. He started to take it out on Carol. I almost had a fight with him. He said he used to be a boxer and I'd be crazy to try it, that his fists could kill.'
모모
모모'It sounds like a terrible evening.'
모모
모모'That part of it wasn't so good.'
모모
모모'What part of it was?'
모모
모모'When she sang the old songs. And she told me about my grandfather in Pocatello.'
모모
모모'Did that take all night?' she said a little tartly.
모모
모모'I didn't stay with them all night. I left around ten o'clock, when we almost had the fight. I-' The same word stuck in his throat again, as if it was involved with secret meanings that wouldn't let it be spoken.
모모
모모'What did you do?'
모모
모모'I went and parked on the view-point where I picked her up the first time. I sat there until nearly two o'clock, watching the stars and listening, you know, to the sea. The sea and the highway. I was trying to figure out what I should do, where I belonged. I still haven't got it figured out.'
모모
모모He added, in a voice that was conscious of me: 'Now I guess I don't have any choice. They'll put me back in the Black Hole of Calcutta.'
모모
모모'Me too,' she said with a nervous giggle. 'We can send each other secret notes. Tap out messages on the bars and stuff.'
모모
모모'It isn't funny, Stell. Everybody out there is crazy, even some of the staff. They get that way.'
모모
모모'You're changing the subject,' she said. 'What did you do at two A.M.?'
모모
모모'I went to see Sam Jackman when he got off work. I thought I could ask him what to do, but I found out that I couldn't. I just couldn't tell him that they were my parents. So I went out in the country, and drove around for a few hours. I didn't want to go home, and I didn't want to go back to the auto court.'
모모
모모'So you turned the car over and tried to kill yourself.'
모모
모모'I-' Silence set in again, and this time it lasted. He sat bolt upright, staring ahead, watching the headlights rise out of the darkness. After a time I noticed that Stella's arm was across his shoulders. His face was streaked with tears.
모모
모모26
모모
모모I DROPPED STELLA off first. She refused to get out of the car until Tom promised that he wouldn't go away again, ever, without telling her.
모모
모모Her father came out of the house, walking on his heels. He put his arms around her. With a kind of resigned affection, she laid her head neatly against his shoulder. Maybe they had learned something, or were learning. People sometimes do.
모모
모모They went inside, and I turned down the driveway.
모모
모모'He's just a fake,' Tom said. 'Stella lent me the car, and then he turned around and told the police I stole it.'
모모
모모'I believe he thought so at the time.'
모모
모모'But he found out the truth later, from Stella, and went right on claiming I stole it.'
모모
모모'Dishonesty keeps creeping in,' I said. 'We all have to watch it.'
모모
모모He thought this over, and decided that I had insulted him. 'Is that supposed to be a crack at me?'
모모
모모'No. I think you're honest, so far as you understand what you're talking about. But you only see one side, your own, and it seems to consist mainly of grievances.'
모모
모모'I have a lot of them,' he admitted. After a moment he said: 'You're wrong about me only seeing one side, though. I know how my-my adoptive parents are supposed to feel, but I know how I feel, too. I can't go on being split down the middle. That's how I felt, you know, these last few nights, like somebody took a cleaver and split me down the middle. I lay awake on that old brass bed, where Mike and Carol, you know, conceived me with old Sipe snoring in the other room, and I was there and I wasn't there. You know? I mean I couldn't believe that I was me and this was my life and those people were my parents. I never believed the Hillmans were, either. They always seemed to be putting on an act. Maybe,' he said half-seriously, 'I was dropped from another planet.'
모모
모모'You've been reading too much science fiction.'
모모
모모'I don't really believe that. I know who my parents were. Carol told me. Mike told me. The doctor told me, and that made it official. But I still have a hard time telling myself.'
모모
모모'Stop trying to force it. It doesn't matter so much who your parents were.'
모모
모모'It does to me,' he said earnestly. 'It's the most important thing in my life.'
모모
모모We were approaching the Hillmans' mailbox. I had been driving slowly, immersed in the conversation, and now I pulled into the driveway and stopped entirely.
모모
모모'I sometimes think children should be anonymous.'
모모
모모'How do you mean, Mr. Archer?'
모모
모모It was the first time he had called me by my name.
모모
모모'I have no plan. I'd just like to change the emphasis slightly. People are trying so hard to live through their children. And the children keep trying so hard to live up to their parents, or live them down. Everybody's living through or for or against somebody else. It doesn't make too much sense, and it isn't working too well.'
모모
모모I was trying to free his mind a little, before he had to face the next big change. I didn't succeed. 'It doesn't work when they lie to you,' he said. 'They lied to me. They pretended I was their own flesh and blood. I thought there was something missing in me when I couldn't feel like their son.'
모모
모모'I've talked to your mother about this-Elaine-and she bitterly regrets it.'
모모
모모'I bet.'
모모
모모'Let's not get off on that routine, Tom.'
모모
모모He was silent for a while. 'I suppose I have to go and talk to them, but I don't want to live with them, and I'm not going to put on any phony feelings.'
모모
모모No phoniness, I thought, was the code of the new generation, at least the ones who were worth anything. It was a fairly decent ideal, but it sometimes worked out cruelly in practice.
모모
모모'You can't forgive them for Laguna Perdida.'
모모
모모'Could you?'
모모
모모I had to think about my answer. 'It would depend on their reasons. I imagine some pretty desperate parents end up there as a last resort with some pretty wild sons and daughters.'
모모
모모'They're desperate, all right,' he said. 'Ralph and Elaine get desperate very easily. They can't stand trouble. Sweep it under the rug. All they wanted to do was get me out of sight, when I stopped being their performing boy. And I had all these terrible things on my mind.'
모모
모모He put his hands to his head, to calm the terrible things. He was close to breaking down.
모모
모모'I'm sorry, Tom. But didn't something crucial happen that Sunday morning?'
모모
모모He peered at me under his raised arm. 'They told you, eh?'
모모
모모'No. I'm asking you to tell me.'
모모
모모'Ask them.'
모모
모모It was all he would say.
모모
모모I drove up the winding blacktop lane to the top of the knoll. Lights were blazing outside and inside the house. The harsh white floods made the stucco walls look ugly and unreal. Black shadows lurked under the melodramatic Moorish arches.
모모
모모There was something a little melodramatic in the way Ralph Hillman stepped out from one of the arches into the light. He wasn't the wreck Susanna had described, at least not superficially. His handsome silver head was sleekly brushed. His face was tightly composed. He held himself erect, and even trotted a few steps as he came toward my side of the car. He was wearing a wine-colored jacket with a rolled collar.
모모
모모'Prodigal son returneth,' Tom was saying beside me in scared bravado. 'But they didn't kill the fatted calf, they killed the prodigal son.'
모모
모모Hillman said: 'I thought you were Lieutenant Bastian.'
모모
모모'Are you expecting him?'
모모
모모'Yes. He says he has something to show me.'
모모
모모He stooped to look in the window and saw Tom. His eyes dilated.
모모
모모'My boy!'
모모
모모His hoarse, whisky-laden voice hardly dared to believe what it was saying. 'You've come back.'
모모
모모'Yeah. I'm here.'
모모
모모Hillman trotted around to the other side of the car and opened the door. 'Come out and let me look at you.'
모모
모모With a brief, noncommittal glance in my direction, Tom climbed out. His movements were stiff and tentative, like a much older man's. Hillman put his hands on the boy's shoulders and held him at arm's length, turning him so that his face was in the light.
모모
모모'How are you, Tom?'
모모
모모'I'm okay. How are you?'
모모
모모'Wonderful, now that you're here.'
모모
모모I didn't doubt that Hillman's feeling was sincere, but his expression of it was somehow wrong. Phony. And I could see Tom wince under his hands.
모모
모모Elaine Hillman came out of the house. I went to meet her. The floodlights multiplied the lines in her face and leached it of any color it might have had. She was pared so thin that she reminded me vaguely of concentration camps. Her eyes were brilliantly alive.
모모
모모'You've brought him back, Mr. Archer. Bless you.'
모모
모모She slipped her hand through my arm and let me take her to him. He stood like a dutiful son while she stood on her toes and kissed him on his grimy tear-runneled cheek.
모모
모모Then he backed away from both of them. He stood leaning against the side of my car with his thumbs in the waistband of his slacks. I'd seen a hundred boys standing as he was standing against cars both hot and cold, on the curb of a street or the shoulder of a highway, while men in uniform questioned them. The sound of the distant highway faintly disturbed the edges of the silence I was listening to now.
모모
모모Tom said: 'I don't want to hurt anybody. I never did. Or maybe I did, I don't know. Anyway, there's no use going on pretending. You see, I know who I am. Mike and Carol Harley were my father and mother. You knew it, too, didn't you?'
모모
모모'I didn't,' Elaine said quickly.
모모
모모'But you knew you weren't my mother.'
모모
모모'Yes. Of course I knew that.'
모모
모모She glanced down at her body and then, almost wistfully, at her husband. He turned away from both of them. His face had momentarily come apart. He seemed to be in pain, which he wanted to hide.
모모
모모'One of you must have known who I really was.'
모모
모모Tom said to Hillman: 'You knew, didn't you?'
모모
모모Hillman didn't answer. Tom said in a high desperate voice: 'I can't stay here. You're both a couple of phonies. You put on a big act for all these years, and as soon as I step out of line you give me the shaft.'
모모
모모Hillman found his voice. 'I should think it was the other way around.'
모모
모모'Okay, so I did wrong. Stand me up against a wall and shoot me.'
모모
모모The boy's voice was slightly hysterical, but it wasn't that that bothered me so much. He seemed to be shifting from attitude to attitude, even from class to class, trying to find a pace where he could stand. I went and stood beside him.
모모
모모'Nobody's talking about punishing you,' Hillman said. 'But a homicidal attack is something that can't be laughed off.'
모모
모모'You're talking crap,' the boy said.
모모
모모Hillman's chin came up. 'Don't speak to me like that!'
모모
모모'Or what will you do? Lock me up with a bunch of psychos and throw away the key?'
모모
모모'I didn't say that.'
모모
모모'No. You just went ahead and did it.'
모모
모모'Perhaps I acted hastily.'
모모
모모'Yes,' Elaine put in. 'Your father acted hastily. Now let's forget the whole thing and go inside and be friends.'
모모
모모'He isn't my father,' Tom said stubbornly.
모모
모모'But we can all be friends, anyway. Can't we, Tom?'
모모
모모Her voice and look were imploring. 'Can't we forget the bad things and simply be glad they're over and that we're all together?'
모모
모모'I don't know. I'd like to go away for a while and live by myself and think things through. What would be wrong with it? I'm old enough.'
모모
모모'That's nonsense.'
모모
모모Hillman shouldn't have said it. A second later his eyes showed that he knew he shouldn't have. He stepped forward and put his hand on the boy's shoulder. 'Maybe that isn't such a bad idea, after all. We're intelligent people, we ought to be able to work something out between us. There's the lodge in Oregon, for example, where you and I were planning to go next month. We could step up our schedule and synch our watches, eh?'
모모
모모The performance was forced. Tom listened to it without interest or hope. After a bit Elaine put her hand inside her husband's arm and drew him toward the house. Tom and I followed along.
모모
모모Mrs. Perez was waiting at the door. There was warmth in her greeting, and even some in Tom's response. They had a discussion about food. Tom said he would like a hamburger sandwich with pea soup. Mrs. Perez darted jouncily away.
모모
모모Hillman surveyed the boy in the light of the chandelier. 'You'd better go up and bathe and change your clothes.'
모모
모모'Now?'
모모
모모'It's just a suggestion,' Hillman said placatingly. 'Lieutenant Bastian of the sheriffs department is on his way over. I'd like you-you should be looking more like yourself.'
모모
모모'Is he going to take me away? Is that the idea?'
모모
모모'Not if I can help it,' Hillman said. 'Look, I'll come up with you.
모모
모모'I can dress myself, Dad!'
모모
모모The word slipped out, irretrievable and undeniable.
모모
모모'But we ought to go over what you're going to say to him. There's no use putting your neck in a noose-I mean-' 
모모
모모'I'll just tell him the truth.'
모모
모모The boy walked away from him toward the stairs. Ralph and Elaine Hillman followed him with their eyes until he was out of sight, and then they followed his footsteps with their ears. The difficult god of the household had returned and the household was functioning again, in its difficult way.
모모
모모We went into the sitting room. Hillman continued across it into the bar alcove. He made himself a drink, absently, as if he was simply trying to find something to do with his hands and then with his mouth.
모모
모모When we came out with the drink in his hand, he reminded me of an actor stepping out through a proscenium arch to join the audience.
모모
모모'Ungrateful sons are like a serpent's tooth,' he said, not very conversationally.
모모
모모Elaine spoke up distinctly from the chesterfield: 'If you're attempting to quote from Icing Lear, the correct quotation is: "How sharper than a serpent's tooth it is to have a thankless child!" But it isn't terribly appropriate, since Tom is not your child. A more apt quotation from the same work would be Edmund's line, "Now, gods, stand up for bastards!" ' He knocked back his drink and moved towards her, lurching just a little. 'I resent your saying that.'
모모
모모'That's your privilege, and your habit.'
모모
모모'Tom is not a bastard. His parents were legally married.'
모모
모모'It hardly matters, considering their background. Did you and your precious Dr Weintraub have to choose the offspring of criminals?'
모모
모모Her voice was cold and bitter. She seemed, after years of silence, to be speaking out and striking back at him.
모모
모모'Look,' he said, 'he's back. I'm glad he's back. You are, too. And we want him to stay with us, don't we?'
모모
모모'I want what's best for him.'
모모
모모'I know what's best for him.'
모모
모모He spread his arms, swinging them a little from side to side, as if he was making Tom a gift of the house and the life that went on inside it.
모모
모모'You don't know what's best for anybody, Ralph. Having men under you, you got into the habit of thinking you knew. But you really don't. I'm interested in Mr. Archer's opinion. Come and sit here beside me,' she said to me, 'and tell me what you think.'
모모
모모'What exactly is the subject?'
모모
모모I said as I sat down.
모모
모모'Tom. What kind of a future should we plan for him?'
모모
모모'I don't think you can do it for him. Let him do his own planning.'
모모
모모Hillman said across the room: 'But all he wants to do is go away by himself.'
모모
모모'I admit that isn't such a good idea. We should be able to persuade him to tone it down. Let him live with another family for a year. Or send him to prep school. After that, he'll be going away to college, anyway.'
모모
모모'Good Lord, do you think he'll make it to college?'
모모
모모'Of course he will, Ralph.'
모모
모모She turned to me. 'But is he ready now for any ordinary prep school? Could he survive it?'
모모
모모'He survived the last two weeks.'
모모
모모'Yes. We have to thank God for that. And you.'
모모
모모Hillman came and stood over me, shaking the ice in his glass. 'Just what was the situation with those people? Was Tom in league with them against us? Understand me, I don't intend to punish him or do anything at all about it. I just want to know.'
모모
모모I answered him slowly and carefully. 'You can hardly talk about a boy being in league with his mother and father. He was confused. He still is. He believed you had turned against him when you put him in Laguna Perdida School. You don't have to be a psychiatrist to know that that isn't the kind of school he needs.'
모모
모모'I'm afraid you aren't conversant with all the facts.'
모모
모모'What are they?'
모모
모모He shook his head. 'Go on with what you were saying. Was he in cahoots with those people?'
모모
모모'Not in the way you mean. But they offered him an out, physically and emotionally, and he took it. Apparently his mother was kind to him.'
모모
모모'I was always kind to him,' Elaine said. She shot a fierce upward glance at her husband. 'But there was falsity in the house, undermining everything.'
모모
모모I said: 'There was falseness in the other house, too, at Dack's Auto Court. There's no doubt that Mike Harley was conning him, setting him up for the phony kidnapping. He didn't let his paternal feelings interfere. Carol was another matter. If she was conning Tom, she was conning herself, too. Tom put it something like this: she knew Harley was up to something, but she didn't let herself know. You get that way after twenty years of living with a man like Harley.'
모모
모모Elaine nodded slightly. I think it was a comment on her own marriage. She said. 'I'm worried about Tom's heredity, with such parents.'
모모
모모The blood rushed into Hillman's face. 'For God's sake, that's really reaching for trouble.'
모모
모모'I hardly need to reach for it,' she said quietly. 'It's in my lap.'
모모
모모She looked at him as if he had placed it there.
모모
모모He turned and walked the length of the room, returned part way, and went into the bar. He poured more whisky over the ice in his glass, and drank it down. Elaine watched him with critical eyes, which he was aware of.
모모
모모'It settles my nerves,' he said.
모모
모모'I hadn't noticed.'
모모
모모He looked at his watch and paced up and down the room. He lost his balance once and had to make a side step.
모모
모모'Why doesn't Bastian come and get it over with?' he said. It's getting late. I was expecting Dick tonight, but I guess he found something more interesting to do.'
모모
모모He burst out at his wife: 'This is a dismal household, you know that?'
모모
모모'I've been aware of it for many years. I tried to keep it together for Tom's sake. That's rather funny, isn't it?'
모모
모모'I don't see anything funny about it.'
모모
모모I didn't, either. The broken edges of their marriage were rubbing together like the unset ends of a bone that had been fractured but was still living.
모모
모모Bastian arrived at last. He came into the reception hall carrying a black metal evidence case, and he was dark-faced and grim. Even the news that Tom was safe at home failed to cheer him much.
모모
모모'Where is he?'
모모
모모'Taking a bath,' Hillman said.
모모
모모'I've got to talk to him. I want a full statement.'
모모
모모'Not tonight, Lieutenant. The boy's been through the wringer.'
모모
모모'But he's the most important witness we have.'
모모
모모'I know that. He'll give you his full story tomorrow.'
모모
모모Bastian glanced from him to me. We were just inside the front door, and Hillman seemed unwilling to let him come in any farther.
모모
모모'I expected better cooperation, Mr. Hillman. You've had cooperation from us. But come to think of it, we haven't had it from you at any time.'
모모
모모'Don't give me any lectures, Lieutenant. My son is home, and it wasn't thanks to you that we got him back.'
모모
모모'A lot of police work went into it,' I said. 'Lieutenant Bastian and I have been working closely together. We still are, I hope.'
모모
모모Hillman transferred his glare to me. He looked ready to order us both out. I said to Bastian: 'You've got something to show us, Lieutenant, is that right?'
모모
모모'Yes.'
모모
모모He held up his evidence case. 'You've already seen it, Archer. I'm not sure if Mr. Hillman has or not.'
모모
모모'What is it?'
모모
모모'I'll show you. I prefer not to describe it beforehand. Could we sit down at a table?'
모모
모모Hillman led us to the library and seated us at a table with a green-shaded reading lamp in the middle, which he switched on. It lit up the tablecloth brilliantly and cast the rest of the room, including our faces, into greenish shadow. Bastian opened the evidence box. It contained the hunting knife with the striped handle, which I had found stuck in Mike Harley's ribs.
모모
모모Hillman drew in his breath sharply.
모모
모모'You recognize it, do you?' Bastian said.
모모
모모'No. I do not.'
모모
모모'Pick it up and examine it more closely. It's quite all right to handle it. It's already been processed for fingerprints and blood.'
모모
모모Hillman didn't move. 'Blood?'
모모
모모'This is the knife that was used to kill Mike Harley. We're almost certain that it was also used to kill the other decedent, Carol Harley. Blood of her type was found on it, as well as her husband's type. Also it fits her wound, the autopsist tells me. Pick it up, Mr. Hillman.'
모모
모모In a gingerly movement Hillman reached out and took it from the box. He turned it over and read the maker's name on the broad shining blade.
모모
모모'It looks like a good knife,' he said. 'But I'm afraid I don't recognize it.'
모모
모모'Would you say that under oath?'
모모
모모'I'd have to. I never saw it before.'
모모
모모Bastian, with the air of a parent removing a dangerous toy, lifted the knife from his hands. 'I don't want to say you're lying, Mr. Hillman. I do have a witness who contradicts you on this. Mr. Botkin, who owns the surplus goods store on lower Main, says that he sold you this knife.'
모모
모모He shook the knife, point foremost, at Hillman's face.
모모
모모Hillman looked scared and sick and obstinate. 'It must have been somebody else. He must be mistaken.'
모모
모모'No. He knows you personally.'
모모
모모'I don't know him.'
모모
모모'You're a very well known man, sir, and Mr. Botkin is certain that you were in his store early this month. Perhaps I can refresh your recollection. You mentioned to Mr. Botkin, in connection with the purchase of this knife, that you were planning a little trip to Oregon with your son. You also complained to Botkin, as a lower Main Street businessman, about an alleged laxness at The Barroom Floor. It had to do with selling liquor to minors, I believe. Do you remember the conversation now?'
모모
모모'No,' Hillman said. 'I do not. The man is lying.'
모모
모모'Why would he be lying?'
모모
모모'I have no idea. Go and find out. It's not my job to do your police work for you.'
모모
모모He stood up, dismissing Bastian. Bastian was unwilling to be dismissed. 'I don't think you're well advised to take this attitude, Mr. Hillman. If you purchased this knife from Mr. Botkin, now is the time to say so. Your previous denial need never go out of this room.'
모모
모모Bastian looked to me for support. I remembered what Botkin had said to me about The Barroom Floor. It was practically certain that his conversation with Hillman had taken place. It didn't follow necessarily that Hillman had bought the knife, but he probably had.
모모
모모I said: 'It's time all the facts were laid out on the table, Mr. Hillman.'
모모
모모'I can't tell him what isn't so, can I?'
모모
모모'No. I wouldn't advise that. Have you thought of talking this over with your attorney?'
모모
모모'I'm thinking about it now.'
모모
모모Hillman had sobered. Droplets of clear liquid stood on his forehead as if the press of the situation had squeezed the alcohol out of him. He said to Bastian: 'I gather you're more or less accusing me of murder.'
모모
모모'No, I am not.'
모모
모모Bastian added in a formal tone: 'You can, of course, stand on your constitutional rights.'
모모
모모Hillman shook his head angrily. Some of his fine light hair fell over his forehead. Under it his eyes glittered like metal triangles. He was an extraordinarily handsome man. His unremitting knowledge of this showed in the caressive movement of the hand with which he pushed his hair back into place.
모모
모모'Look,' he said, 'could we continue this seance in the morning? I've had a hard week, and I'd like a chance to sleep on this business. I've had no real sleep since Monday.'
모모
모모'Neither have I,' Bastian said.
모모
모모'Maybe you need some sleep, too. This harassing approach isn't really such a good idea.'
모모
모모'There was no harassment.'
모모
모모'I'll be the judge of that.' Hillman's voice rose. 'You brought that knife into my home and shook it in my face. I have a witness to that,' he added, meaning me.
모모
모모I said: 'Let's not get bogged down in petty arguments. Lieutenant Bastian and I have some business to discuss.'
모모
모모'Anything you say to him you'll have to say in front of me.'
모모
모모'All right.'
모모
모모'After I talk to the boy,' Bastian said.
모모
모모Hillman made a curt gesture with his hand. 'You're not talking to him. I don't believe I'll let you talk to him tomorrow, either. There are, after all, medical considerations.'
모모
모모'Are you a medical man?'
모모
모모'I have medical men at my disposal.'
모모
모모'I'm sure you do. So do we.'
모모
모모The two men faced each other in quiet fury. They were opposites in many ways. Bastian was a saturnine Puritan, absolutely honest, a stickler for detail, a policeman before he was a man. Hillman's personality was less clear. It had romantic and actorish elements, which often mask deep evasions. His career had been meteoric, but it was the kind of career that sometimes left a man empty-handed in middle life.
모모
모모'Do you have something to say to the lieutenant?'
모모
모모Hillman asked me. 'Before he leaves?'
모모
모모'Yes. You may not like this, Mr. Hillman. I don't. Last night a young man driving a late-model blue Chevrolet was seen in the driveway of the Barcelona Hotel. It's where Mike Harley was found stabbed, with that knife.'
모모
모모I pointed to the evidence box on the table. 'The young man has been tentatively identified as Dick Leandro.'
모모
모모'Who made the identification?' Bastian said.
모모
모모'Ben Daly, the service-station operator.'
모모
모모'The man who killed Sipe.'
모모
모모'Yes.'
모모
모모'He's either mistaken or lying,' Hillman said. 'Dick drives a blue car, but it's a small sports car, a Triumph.'
모모
모모'Does he have access to a blue Chevrolet?'
모모
모모'Not to my knowledge. You're surely not trying to involve Dick in this mess.'
모모
모모'If he's involved, we have to know about it.'
모모
모모I said to Bastian: 'Maybe you can determine whether he borrowed or rented a blue Chevrolet last night. Or it's barely possible that he stole one.
모모
모모'Will do,' Bastian said.
모모
모모Hillman said nothing.
모모
모모27
모모
모모BASTIAN PICKED UP his evidence case and shut it with a click. He walked out without a sign to either of us. He was treating Hillman as if he no longer existed. He was treating me in such a way that I could stay with Hillman.
모모
모모Hillman watched him from the entrance to the library until he was safely across the reception hall and out the front door. Then he came back into the room. Instead of returning to the table where I was, he went to the wall of photographs where the squadron on the flight deck hung in green deep-sea light.
모모
모모'What goes on around here?' he said. 'Somebody took down Dick's picture.'
모모
모모'I did, for identification purposes.'
모모
모모I got it out of my pocket. Hillman came and took it away from me. The glass was smudged by lingers, and he rubbed it with the sleeve of his jacket.
모모
모모'You had no right to take it. What are you trying to do to Dick, anyway?'
모모
모모'Get at the truth about him.'
모모
모모'There is no mysterious truth about him. He's a perfectly nice ordinary kid.'
모모
모모'I hope so.'
모모
모모'Look here,' he said, 'you've accomplished what I hired you to do. Don't think I'm ungrateful-I'm planning to give you a substantial bonus. But I didn't hire you to investigate those murders.'
모모
모모'And I don't get the bonus unless I stop?'
모모
모모'I didn't say that.'
모모
모모'You didn't have to.'
모모
모모He spread his hands on the table and leaned above me, heavy-faced and powerful. 'Just how do you get to talk to your betters the way you've been talking to me?'
모모
모모'By my betters you mean people with more money?'
모모
모모'Roughly, yes.'
모모
모모'I'll tell you, Mr. Hillman. I rather like you. I'm trying to talk straight to you because somebody has to. You're headed on a collision course with the law. If you stay on it, you're going to get hurt.'
모모
모모His face stiffened and his eyes narrowed. He didn't like to be told anything. He liked to do the telling.
모모
모모'I could buy and sell Bastian.'
모모
모모'You can't if he's not for sale. You know damn well he isn't.'
모모
모모He straightened, raising his head out of the light into the greenish shadow. His face resembled old bronze, except that it was working. After a time he said: 'What do you think I ought to do?'
모모
모모'Start telling the truth.'
모모
모모'Dammit, you imply I haven't been.'
모모
모모'I'm doing more than imply it, Mr. Hillman.'
모모
모모He turned on me with his fists clenched, ready to hit me. I remained sitting. He walked away and came back. Without whisky, he was getting very jumpy.
모모
모모'I suppose you think I killed them.'
모모
모모'I'm not doing any speculating. I am morally certain you bought that knife from Botkin.'
모모
모모'How can you be certain?'
모모
모모'I've talked to the man.'
모모
모모'Who authorized you to? I'm not paying you to gather evidence against me.'
모모
모모I said, rather wearily: 'Couldn't we forget about your wonderful money for a while, and just sit here and talk like a couple of human beings? A couple of human beings in a bind?'
모모
모모He considered this. Eventually he said: 'You're not in a bind. I am.'
모모
모모'Tell me about it. Unless you actually did commit those murders. In which case you should tell your lawyer and nobody else.'
모모
모모'I didn't. I almost wish I had.'
모모
모모He sat down across from me, slumping forward a little, with his arms resting on the tabletop. 'I admit I bought the knife. I don't intend to admit it to anyone else. Botkin will have to be persuaded to change his story.'
모모
모모'How?'
모모
모모'He can't make anything out of that store of his. I ought to know, my father owned one like it in South Boston. I can give Botkin enough money to retire to Mexico.'
모모
모모I was a bit appalled, not so much by the suggestion of crude subornation-I'd often heard it before-as by the fact that Hillman was making it. In the decades since he commanded a squadron at Midway, he must have bumped down quite a few moral steps.
모모
모모I said: 'You better forget about that approach, Mr. Hillman. It's part of the collision course with the law I was talking about. And you'll end up sunk.'
모모
모모'I'm sunk now,' he said in an even voice.
모모
모모He laid his head down on his arms. His hair spilled forward like a broken white sheaf. I could see the naked pink circle on the crown which was ordinarily hidden. It was like a tonsure of mortality.
모모
모모'What did you do with the knife?'
모모
모모I said to him. 'Did you give it to Dick Leandro?'
모모
모모'No.'
모모
모모Spreading his hands on the tablecloth, he pushed himself upright. His moist palms slipped and squeaked on the polished surface. 'I wish I had.'
모모
모모'Was Tom the one you gave it to?'
모모
모모He groaned. 'I not only gave it to him. I told Botkin I was buying the bloody thing as a gift for him. Bastian must be aware of that, but he's holding it back.'
모모
모모'Bastian would,' I said. 'It still doesn't follow that Tom used it on his father and mother. He certainly had no reason to kill his mother.'
모모
모모'He doesn't need a rational motive. You don't know Tom.'
모모
모모'You keep telling me that. At the same time you keep refusing to fill in the picture.'
모모
모모'It's a fairly ugly picture.'
모모
모모'Something was said tonight about a homicidal attack.'
모모
모모'I didn't mean to let that slip out.'
모모
모모'Who attacked whom and why?'
모모
모모'Tom threatened Elaine with a loaded gun. He wasn't kidding, either.'
모모
모모'Was this the Sunday-morning episode you've been sub pressing?'
모모
모모He nodded. 'I think the accident must have affected his mind. When I got home from the judge's house, he had her in his room He was holding my revolver with the muzzle against her head' Hillman pressed his fingertip into his temple-and he had he down on her knees, begging for mercy. Literally begging. I didn't know whether he was going to give me the gun, either. For a minute he held it on me. I half expected him to shoot me.'
모모
모모I could feel the hairs prickling at the nape of my neck. It was an ugly picture, all right. What was worse, it was a classic one: the schizophrenic execution killer.
모모
모모'Did he say anything when you took the gun?'
모모
모모'Not a word. He handed it over in a rather formal way. He acted like a kind of automaton. He didn't seem to realize what he'd done, or tried to do.'
모모
모모'Had he said anything to your wife?'
모모
모모'Yes. He said he would kill her if she didn't leave him alone. She'd simply gone to his room to offer him some food, and he went into this silent white rage of his.'
모모
모모'He had a lot of things on his mind,' I said, 'and he'd been up all night. He told me something about it. You might say it was the crucial night of his young life. He met his real father for the first time'-Hillman grimaced-'which must have been a fairly shattering experience. You might say he was lost between two worlds, and blaming you and your wife for not preparing him. You should have, you know. You had no right to cheat him of the facts, whether you liked them or not. When the facts finally hit him, it was more than he could handle. He deliberately turned the car over that morning.'
모모
모모'You mean he attempted suicide?'
모모
모모Hillman said. 'He made a stab at it. I think it was more a signal that his life was out of control. He didn't let go of the steering wheel, and he wasn't badly hurt. Nobody got hurt in the gun incident, either.'
모모
모모'You've got to take it seriously, though. He was in dead earnest.'
모모
모모'Maybe. I'm not trying to brush it off. Have you talked it over with a psychiatrist?'
모모
모모'I have not. There are certain things you don't let out of the family.'
모모
모모'That depends on the family.'
모모
모모'Look,' he said, 'I was afraid they wouldn't admit him to the school if they knew he was that violent.'
모모
모모'Would that have been such a tragedy?'
모모
모모'I had to do something with him. I don't know what I'm going to do with him now.'
모모
모모He bowed his disheveled head.
모모
모모'You need better advice than I can give you, legal and psychiatric.'
모모
모모'You're assuming he killed those two people.'
모모
모모'Not necessarily. Why don't you ask Dr Weintraub to recommend somebody?'
모모
모모Hillman jerked himself upright. 'That old woman?'
모모
모모'I understood he was an old friend of yours, and he knows something about psychiatry.'
모모
모모'Weinie has a worm's-eye knowledge, I suppose.'
모모
모모His voice rasped with contempt. 'He had a nervous breakdown after Midway. We had to send him stateside to recuperate, while men were dying. While men were dying,' Hillman repeated. Then he seemed to surround himself with silence.
모모
모모He sat in a listening attitude. I waited. His angry face became smooth and his voice changed with it. 'Jesus, that was a day. We lost more than half of our TBD's. The Zekes took them like sitting ducks. I couldn't bring them back. I don't blame Weinie for breaking down, so many men died on him.'
모모
모모His voice was hushed. His eyes were distant. He didn't even seem aware of my presence. His mind was over the edge of the world where his men had died, and he had died more than a little.
모모
모모'The hell of it is,' he was saying, 'I love Tom. We haven't been close for years, and he's been hard to handle. But he's my son, and I love him.'
모모
모모'I'm sure you do. But maybe you want more than Tom can give you. He can't give you back your dead pilots.'
모모
모모Hillman didn't understand me. He seemed bewildered. His gray eyes were clouded.
모모
모모'What did you say?'
모모
모모'Perhaps you were expecting too much from the boy.'
모모
모모'In what way?'
모모
모모'Forget it,' I said.
모모
모모Hillman was hurt. 'You think I expect too much? I've been getting damn little. And look what I'm willing to give him.'
모모
모모He spread his arms again, to embrace the house and everything he owned. 'Why, he can have every nickel I possess for his defense. We'll get him off and go to another country to live.'
모모
모모'You're away ahead of yourself, Mr. Hillman. He hasn't been charged with anything yet.'
모모
모모'He will be.'
모모
모모His voice sounded both fatalistic and defiant.
모모
모모'Maybe. Let's consider the possibilities. The only evidence against him is the knife, and that's pretty dubious if you think about it. He didn't take it with him, surely, when you put him in Laguna Perdida.'
모모
모모'He may have. I didn't search him.'
모모
모모'I'm willing to bet they did.'
모모
모모Hillman narrowed his eyes until they were just a glitter between the folded lids. 'You're right, Archer. He didn't have the knife when he left the house. I remember seeing it afterwards, that same 'day.'
모모
모모'Where was it?'
모모
모모'In his room, in one of the chests of drawers.'
모모
모모'And you left it in the drawer?'
모모
모모'There was no reason not to.'
모모
모모'Then anybody with access to the house could have got hold of it?'
모모
모모'Yes. Unfortunately that includes Tom. He could have sneaked in after he escaped from the school.'
모모
모모'It also includes Dick Leandro, who wouldn't have had to sneak in. He's in and out of the house all the time, isn't he?'
모모
모모'I suppose he is. That doesn't prove anything.'
모모
모모'No, but when you put it together with the fact that Dick was probably seen at the Barcelona Hotel last night, it starts you thinking about him. There's still something missing in this case, you know. The equations don't balance.'
모모
모모'Dick isn't your missing quantity,' he said hastily.
모모
모모'You're quite protective about Dick.'
모모
모모'I'm fond of him. Why shouldn't I be? He's a nice boy, and I've been able to help him. Dammit, Archer.'
모모
모모His voice deepened. 'When a fellow reaches a certain age, he needs to pass on what he knows, or part of it, to a younger fellow.'
모모
모모'Are you thinking of passing on some money, too?'
모모
모모'We may eventually. It will depend on Elaine. She controls the main money. But I can assure you it couldn't matter to Dick.'
모모
모모'It matters to everybody. I think it matters very much to Dick. He's a pleaser.'
모모
모모'What is that supposed to mean?'
모모
모모'You know what it means. He lives by pleasing people, mainly you. Tell me this. Does Dick know about the gun incident in Tom's room?'
모모
모모'Yes. He was with me that Sunday morning. He drove me to the judge's house and home again.'
모모
모모'He gets in on a lot of things,' I said.
모모
모모'That's natural. He's virtually a member of the family. As a matter of fact, I expected him tonight. He said he had something he wanted to talk over with me.'
모모
모모He looked at his watch. 'But it's too late now. It's past eleven o'clock.'
모모
모모'Get him out here anyway, will you?'
모모
모모'Not tonight. I've had it. I don't want to have to pull my face together and put on a front for Dick now.'
모모
모모He looked at me a little sheepishly. He had revealed himself to me, a vain man who couldn't forget his face, a secret man who lived behind a front. He pushed his silver mane back and patted it in place.
모모
모모'Tonight is all the time we have,' I said. 'In the morning you can expect Bastian and the sheriff and probably the DA pounding on your door. You won't be able to put them off by simply denying that you bought that knife. You're going to have to explain it.'
모모
모모'Do you really think Dick took it?'
모모
모모'He's a better suspect than Tom, in my opinion.'
모모
모모'Very well, I'll call him.'
모모
모모He rose and went to the telephone on the desk.' 
모모
모모'Don't tell him what you want him for. He might break and run.'
모모
모모'Naturally I won't.'
모모
모모He dialed a number from memory, and waited. When he spoke, his voice had changed again. It was lighter and younger. 'Dick? You said something to Elaine about dropping by tonight. I was wondering if I was to expect you . . . I know it's late. I'm sorry you're not too well. What's the trouble? . . . I'm sorry. Look, why don't you come out anyway, just for a minute? Tom came home tonight, isn't that great? He'll want to see you. And I particularly want to see you . . . Yep, it's an order . . . Fine, I'll look for you then.'
모모
모모He hung up.
모모
모모'What's the matter with him?'
모모
모모I asked.
모모
모모'He says he doesn't feel well.'
모모
모모'Sick?'
모모
모모'Depressed. But he cheered up when I told him Tom was home. He'll be out shortly.'
모모
모모'Good. In the meantime I want to talk to Tom.'
모모
모모Hillman came and stood over me. His face was rather obscure in the green penumbra. 'Before you talk to him again, there's something you ought to know.'
모모
모모I waited for him to go on. Finally I asked him: 'Is it about Tom?'
모모
모모'It has to do with both of us.'
모모
모모He hesitated, his eyes intent on my face. 'On second thought, I don't think I'll let my back hair down any further tonight.'
모모
모모'You may never have another chance,' I said, 'before it gets let down for you, the hard way.'
모모
모모'That's where you're wrong. Nobody knows this particular thing but me.'
모모
모모'And it has to do with you and Tom?'
모모
모모'That's right. Now let's forget it.'
모모
모모He didn't want to forget it, though. He wanted to share his secret, without taking the responsibility of speaking out. He lingered by the table, looking down at my face with his stainless steel eyes.
모모
모모I thought of the feeling in Hillman's voice when he spoke of his love for Tom. Perhaps that feeling was the element which would balance the equation.
모모
모모'Is Tom your natural son?'
모모
모모I said.
모모
모모He didn't hesitate in answering. 'Yes. He's my own flesh and blood.'
모모
모모'And you're the only one who knows?'
모모
